THE RED PAGE
LAFCADIO HEARN
ONE of the most remarkable careers in
literature came to a pathetic close in
Japan in September, 1904, when
died, at the age of 54, Lafcadio Hearn, author'
of Kokoru, Kwaidan, Glimpses of Un-
familiar Japan, Japan, an Interpretation, and
other remarkable The life of a man
of the literary ,pre T eminence of Hearn,
—beginning on the Greek isle of Santa Maura
(from whose modern designation, Lefcada,
the child was given his name), and, after
wanderings in America and the West Indies,
ending with his burial, with Buddhist rites,
as a naturalised Japanese in Tokyo—must
necessarily prove of interest; but the inner
life of the man was even richer in romance.
Hearn was of a remarkable parentage.
His father was Irish, with a strain of gypsy
blood, his mother Greek, with a hinted ad-
mixture of Turkish and Arabic origins ; the
marriage, which was the result of a passion-
ate love-affair, was afterwards annulled, both
parties re-marrying, leaving Hearn and an
undistinguished brother practically orphans.
Lafcadio's life story may be briefly summed
as one long struggle against poverty, dread-
fully handicapped by physical and tempera-
mental defects. Brought up in the wealthy
home of his great-aunt in Wales, sent for
two years to a Jesuit college in France,
thence to a Roman Catholic college
in England, at the age of nineteen he fled,
apparently in a great fear of priests, penni-
less and friendless, to America. After ex-
periences that were doubly terrifying to one
of his excessive sensitiveness, he ultimately
got employment as a reporter on an Ameri-
can daily paper. Then, slowly drifting south-
ward toward the tropics that ever after-
wards held his exotic soul, he was able,
commissioned by Harper's, to spend two
years in the French West Indies. In that
pause from the long hours of toil upon the
daily press, Hearn was able to indulge to its
utmost his life-long craving for strange arid
exotic knowledge. He had from the first
been interested in French literature, and his
first literary work consisted of trai-.sla-
tions from Gautier, on whom his mar-
vellous pro'se style was modelled, Mau-
passant and Loti.
His first book, One of Cleopatra's Nights ,
a translation of some of Gautier’s fan-
tastic romances, made many journeys
before it found a publisher. The reason
for this reluctance of the publishers may
be divined from the fact that one critie
spoke of the work as “ stories of unbridled
lust, without the apology of natural passion ”:
the translator suffered for the sins of his
author. But now Hearn set himself to
acquire that knowledge of the Odd of which
he was destined to make such remarkable
use. He published a collection of Creole
proverbs; he eulled from strange litera-
tures their most exotic blossoms ; he
probed history for the remarkable ;
be sought assiduously among ancient legends
in quest of the weird ; he knew gypsy poetry,
Hindoo mythology, the music of the ancient
Egyptians, Finnish literature, the poetry of
Pre-Islamic Arabia, the dances of the Orient.
For this reason his letters (The Life and
Letters of Lafcadio Hearn, by ■ Elizabeth
Bisland ; Constable,- London) abound in
strange suggestions.
What a curious article (he writes) might be
written upon “ The Amorous Epochs of National
Literatures,” dwelling especially upon the extra-
vagant passionateness of Indian, Persian and
Arabic belle-lettres —and their offshoots !
The pertinacity with which he pursued his
task may be indicated by his description of
his library.
In positive acquirements I am exceptionally
ignorant. By purchasing queer books and fol-
lowing odd subjects I have been able to give my-
self the air of knowing more than I do ; but none
of my work would bear the scrutiny of a
specialist ; I would like, however, to show you
my library. .. . Every volume is queer. Know-
ing that I have nothing resembling genius, and
that any ordinary talent must be supplemented
with some sort of curious study in order to place
it above the mediocre line. 1 am striving to woo
the Muse of the Odd. . . . Meantime, I am trying
to fill my brain with unfamiliar facts on special
topics, believing that, some day or other, I shall
be able to utilise them in a new way.
Ultimately this longing led him to Japan.
There he became a teacher in a middle school
at Matsue, married a Japanese lady of high
rank, relinquished pedagogy for journalism,
and was (in 189 b) appointed Professor
of English in the Imperial University
of Tokyo. After seven years of brilliant
work there, he was cruelly and deliberately
forced by the Japanese authorities to resign
his post, under circumstances that serve as
an object-lesson to those who think that ever
the East and West can be reconciled His
residence in Japan, however, while proving
fatal to himself, gave to the greater world of
literature a contribution of inestimable
value. In Japan he—“a stray from the
Nineteenth century, moving about in
the unrealised world of the Fourteenth ” —-
wrote that marvellous series of expositions
of the psychology of the Japanese that cul-
minated in his final work, Japan, an Inter-
pretation. And at the time of his death the
universities of Oxford and of London had
recognised his labors to the extent of negoti-*
ating with him for a series of lectures. In
the Life and Letters , however, no mention
is ma le of one fact that has an Australasian
bearing. It is that on the establishment of
the Victoria University, at Wellington
(M.L.), Hearn was one of the applicants
for the chair in English. The posi-
tion was given to a brother of “Jock”
Mackenzie, the famous Minister for Lands
in the first Seddon Cabinet. Under the
circumstances the choice cannot be cavilled
at; despite the immense disparity in
the teaching and literary equipment of
the two applicants, the position of a
Professor of English, with a Japanese wife
and family, in a Maoriland university would
have been awkward both to Wellington and
to Hearn ; but the mind may for one moment
dwell upon the possibility that Hearn in
Maoriland with his training and tempera-
ment might have done for the Maori legends
what he So exquisitely did for Japan.
Lafcadio Hearn went to Japan full of a
fierce enthusiasm for this long-yearned-for
land of promise. The precepts of Buddhism
had early attracted this man who in his youth
had been surfeited with Christianity. He
yearned passionately for the pagan ; Japanese
ancestor-worship seemed to him an almost
unprospected field for the ghostly and the
weird ; and, finally, the charm of another
civilisation was a powerful lure to one whose
restless and probing spirit was dissatisfied,
not merely with places, but with civilisations.
You can comprehend (he writes) how one
becomes tired of the very stones of a place —the
odors, the colors, the shapes of shadows and tint
of its sky ; —and how small irritations become
colossal and crushing by years of repetition—yet
perhaps you will not comprehend that one can
actually become weary of a whole system of life,
of civilisation even with very limited experience.
His first impressions of Japan, in the old-
world town of Matsue, confirmed his antici-
patory enthusiasms ; and his earlier Japanese
books are full of the quaintness, the beauty,
the peace, the happiness of this mediaeval
child among the-modern nations. For, as
Elizabeth Bisland records
He had the good fortune to be ehosen to assist
at one of the great births of history. Out of “ a
race as primitive.as the Etruscan before Rome
was” —as he declared be found them —he was to
see a mighty modern nation spring full-armed,
with all the sudden miraculous transformation of
some great mailed beetle bursting from the grey
hidden shell of a feeble-Jo.oking pupa. He saw
the fourteenth century turn swiftly., amazingly
into the twentieth, and his twelve volumes of
studies of the Japanese people were to have that
unique and lasting value that would attach to
equally painstaking records of Greek life before
the Persian wars. Inestimable, immortal would
be such book's—could they anywhere be found—
setting down the faiths, the traditions, the daily
lives, the songs, the dances, the names, the
legends, the humble lore of plants, birds, and
insects of that people who suddenly stood up at
Thermopylae, broke the wave from the east, made
Europe possible, and set the corner-stone of
Occidental thought. This was what Lafcadio
Hearn, a little, penniless, half-blind, eccentric
wanderer, had come to do for Japan. To make
immortal the story of the childhood of a people
as simple as the early Greeks, who were to break
at Mukden the great wave of conquest from the
west, and to rejuvenate the most ancient East.
It was fantasy and charm that Hearn had
come to Japan to find. And at first he
thought he had discovered the hidden gate
to lvis paradise.
The first charm is intangible and volatile as a
perfume. ... Elfish everything seems ; for every-
thing, as well as everybody, is small and queer
and mysterious : the little houses under their
blue roofs, the little shop-fronts hung with blue,
and the smiling little people in their blue cos-
tumes ... the magical transparencies of distance,
the wondrous; delicacies of tones, the enormous
height of the summer bluq„ and the white, soft
witchery of the Japanese sun.
But disillusion quickly came—a disillusion
that grew in clarity and bitterness till the
end. He learnt fid know the griln and
callous soul behind that childish smil-
ing mask ; he saw the Japanese as they are ;
and, at last, he died, trodden under foot like
a troublesome insect, wiped out with a
monstrous and dispassionate callousness that
cannot be paralleled in the minds of the
West. It is a grim story, plainly told in his
letters. Here and there in his published
works he hints of that almost inhuman
Oi'iental soul ; but in his correspondence he
spoke out all his disillusionment.
Here is an astounding fact. The Japanese
child is as close to you as a European child—
perhaps closer and sweeter, because infinitely
more natural and naturally refined. Cultivate
his mind, and the more it is cultivated the
farther you push him from you. Why ? Because
here the race antipodalism shows itself. As the
Oriental thinks naturally to the left where we
think to the right, the more you cultivate him the
mure he will think in the opposite direction from
you.
Closely that Western observer watched,
and marvellously he recorded the process of
a race, “ live thousand years at least emo-
tionally behind us,” adopting under compul-
sion and at a price to itself that must be
paid for many generations yet, the practices
of a larger civilisation.
Here in Japan I watch, day by day, the de-
struction of a wonderful and very beautiful civili-
sation, by industrial pressure... .
I am now convinced that the deficiency of the
sexual instinct (using the term philosophically) in
the [Japanese] race is a serious defect rather
than a merit, and is very probably connected
with the absence of the musical sense, and the
incapacity for abstract reasoning. That the
Japanese can ever reach our aesthetic stage seems
to me utterly impossible, but assuredly what they
lack in certain directions they may prove splen-
didly capable of making up in others. At least
it seems to me that here is a danger. Japan
ought to produce scientific, political, and mili-
tary haters of “ideologists”—Napoleons of prac-
tical applications of science. .. . Imagine a
civilisation on Western lines, with cold calcula-
tion universally substituted for ethical principle !
The suggestion is very terrible and very ugly.
One would pre c er even the society of the later
Roman Empire.
It is an ugly business, this’[Chinese] war. . .
The people are still good. The tipper classes are
becoming corrupt. The old courtesy, the old
faith, the old kindness are vanishing like snow in
sun. . . The Japanese peasant is ten times more
of a gentleman than a foreign merchant could
ever learn to be. Unfortunately, the Japanese
official, with all his civility and morality rubbed
off, is something a good deal lower than a savage,
and meaner than the straight-out Western rough
(who always has a kernel of good in him) by an
inexpressible percent. . . The old men are divine.
When you meet a horrid Japanese, though, there
is a distorted quality about him that makes him a
unique monster —he is like an awry caricature of
a Western mean fellow without the vim and push
—solid contemptibility in petto.
The students attempt to do on rice and gruel
wljat foreign students can only do on beef . . .
heavy, nutritious diet. In the eternal order of
things, the overstrain comes. The higher educa-
tion will not give the desired results for at least
another generation—because the physique of the
student must he raised to meet it. The higher
education requires a physiological change—an in-
crease of brain capacity in actual development of
tissue, an increase of nervous energy, and, conse-
quently,- a higher standard of living. . . . The
stuff ent of to-day is not sufficiently strong and
sufficiently nourished to hear the tremendous
strain put upon him at the higher schools and the
university. . . . Some students I liked l>ave gone
mad; numbers have died ; numbers have had to
give up. The strain is too great because the
hardship is too great—the cold, the poor, cheap
food, the poor, thin clothes. . . . Long-sustained
effort, protracted and unbroken study, are things
foreign to Japanese existence.
We are all in Japan living over earthquakes.
Nothing is stable. All Japanese officialdom is
perpetually in flux—nothing but the throne is
even temporarily fixed ; and the direction of the
currents depends much upon force of intrigue.
They shift, like currents in the sea, off a coast of
tides. But the side current's penetrate every-
where .... and swirl round the writing-stool of
the smallest clerk—whose pen trembles with con-
tinual fear for his wife’s and babies’ rice. ... In
the Orient, intrigue has been cultivated as an art for
ages, and it lias been cultivated as an art in every
country; no doubt. - But the result of the adoption
of constitutional government by a race accustomed
to autocracy and caste enabled intrigue to spread
like a ferment, in new forms, through every con-
dition of society—and almost into every house-
hold. It has become an infinite net—unbreakable,
because as elastic as air, though strong enough to
upset ministers as readily as to oust clerks. . . .
Tokyo takes out of me all power to hope for a
great Japanese future. .. . At present I think it
can be truthfully said that every official branch
of service shows the rapidly growing weakness
that means demoralisation.
It strikes me that in another twenty years, or
perhaps thirty, after a brief artificial expansion,
all the ports will shrink. The foreign commerce
will be all reduced to agencies. A system of
small persecutions will be inaugurated and main-
tained to drive away all the foreigners who can
be driven away .. . Life will be made wretched
for Occidental’s—in business—just as it is being
made in the schools —by all sorts of little tricky
plans which cannot be brought under law-pro-
visions, or even so defined as to appear to justify
resentment —tricks at which the Japanese are as
elaborately ingenious as they are in matters of
etiquette and forms of other kinds. The nation
will show its ugly side to us ... My conclusion
is that the charm of Japanese life is largely the
charm of childhood, and that the most beautiful
of all race childhoods is passing into an adolescence
which threatens to prove repulsive.
&
Hearn did more than write of that swift
racial degeneration ; he was himself one of
the victims of the new regime. He took the
first dangerous step when he married a
Japanese lady. The marriage, which turned
out a perfectly happy one, was celebrated
with the local rites, as to have been married
according to English laws would at that
time have deprived her of her Japanese
citizenship and separated her from her
people, by obliging her to live in one of ’the
open poits. Concern for her future and his
children’s status, should he die, forced upon
Hearn the second step. He saw no way out
of the difficulty but to renounce his English
allegiance and becomea subject of the Mikado.
To do this he had necessarily to Ije adopted
into his wife’s family. He accordingly took
her name, Koizumi. And even as he took
this necessary step he had a premonition of
its result.
I am still hesitating alittle (he writes) —because
of the Japanese. Would they try to take advant-
age, and cut down my salary ?
Hearn had ample reason to fear the loss
of his professorship at Tokyo University.
All his life he struggled with poverty ; his
profits from his books were pitiably little
(“a book brings me in about 300 dollars—
after two years’ waiting ”) ; Inis health was
failing ; his eyesight in a dangerous shite of
weakness. And he notes one warning :
An Englishman in Yokohama, who became a
Japanese citizen, had hi* salary immediately re-
duced to a very small figure, with the ©bserva-
tion: “Having beeome a Japanese citizen, you
must now be content to live like one.”
His position, (nice he had put himself
into the hands of the Japanese, was indeed
precarious. Asked to print his inimitable
lectures, he refused, with the remark :
* When a professor in Japan prints his lectures,
the authorities think they have got all that he
knbws in hand, and lire likely to look about for a
new ihan.
And at length, with weak lungs and an over-
powering anxiety abo.ut his eldest son’s
future—“ My son—a being entirely innocent
of evil—what chance for him in such a world
as Japan '! ” —Hearn learnt the full bitter-
ness of his attempt to understand, and to
serve, the Japanese.
As I anticipated. I was forced out of the uni-
versity, oil the pretext that, as a Japanese citizen,
I was not entitled to a “ foreign salary.” (They
told me that 1 ought to be satisfied to live on rice,
like a Japanese.) ... I was also refused the
money allowed to professors for a nine-months’
vacation after a service of six years, )
Then a way opened to him out of all his
troubles. Lafcadio Hearn died.
“ Indeed,” writes his wife in her artless,
broken-English letters, “he loved Japan
with his whole heart, but his sincere love for
Japan was not very well understood by
Japanese.”
As was timidly prophesied on this,page, George
W. Lambert has made secure his position in the
London art-world by his pictures in the Exhibi-
tions of the Modern Society of Portrait Painters
and of the International Society, of which lie is
an Associate. The critics are especially enthusi-
astic about his five portraits in the former show.
The Academy, for instance, after speaking of a
“striking and dramatic portrait,” of F. Derwent
Wood, gives detailed criticism of a portrait group,,
“a very charming tigure-composition of two ladies
going down to the sea, one carrying a child about
to paddle, while by her side trots a naked
youngster, eager for his swim. It is a de-
lightful little work, charming in color and
composition, and the contrast of the nude
and clothed figures is as successful and
natural here as it is unnatural and forced in
the same artist’s The Sonnet at the New Gallery.”
The Athcnceurn gives Lambert first place in its
criticism: —“Portrait painting has, and has
always had, two ways of satisfying a client: it
may succeed by intimacy, the revelation of
character, or in less spiritual fashion by display, by
celebrating the splendor and costliness of Ahe
sitter's appointments; and there is coming into
existence among the younger portrait painters
something like a generally recognised style based
on the demand for the latter qualities. The pre-
pared scales of tints which Mr. Lambert would
seem to use offers a method admirably adapted
to rendering brilliantly and cleverly delicate
differences of color and texture, effects of sheen
and shimmer and transparency, the pomp of the
toilet. In The Old Dress in the present exhibi-
tion he attempts a tour deforce in this region—
loses his way slightly in the elaborate scheme, and 1
recovers himself by the use of a rather dirty glaze.
The face meanwhile is a somewhat perfunctory
detail of the composition, and not to be compared
with the two heads by the same painter (one of
himself and one of Mr. Derwent Wood) which are
among the best portraits here!” The fact that
Lambert has “arrived ” is indicated, not by the
details of the criticisms, but by the necessity the
art-critics feel so closely to scrutinise his work. 1
Liza of Bourke's, by Cyril Vaile (D. W. Pater-
son Go., Melb.), is a collection of readable short
stories of out-back which have already made their
appearance in various newspapers of the con-
tinent. The yarns are quite devoid of style,
somewhat diluted with sentimentality, but, on
the whole, they are faithful and interesting pic-
tures of Australian' life. Cyril Vaile might
consider the question of a novel.
Two prominent English publishing firms an-
nounce their intention of publishing novels in
England at the colonial price of lialf-a-crown.
An English review sees in the drop in priep an
increased difficulty for the unknown author in
finding a market: “At the most, his first book
(unless, indeed, he be exceptionally lucky) would
sell to the extent of from five hundred to one
thousand, and these at half-a-crown would admit
of very little plunder coming his way. In fact,
the publishers would be almost obliged to con-
centrate their efforts on pushing the important
man at the expense of those whose spurs are still
unwon.” We have heard that wail before. ' -
The North American Review has become an
enthusiastic convert to Esperanto—the language
that looks like a dynamited Tower of Babel
running amok—and means to promulgate it with
frenzy. In addition to articles upon the lan-
guage, it publishes in every issue instalments of an
Esperanto primer.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Top Booms.
The top rooms lie up many stairs
Where gloomy shadows frown,
Although the steps these people tread
Upon that road uncarpeted
Are nearly always down.
Their windows, poorly mended, give
A view of roof and sky ;
A prospect that is rather blue
And gives to many things its hue —
Life there is seldom high.
Yet there’s a compensation giv'n ;
The lip may there be curled,
And tenants living up so high
May, with a proud and scornful eye,
Look down upon the world.
The top rooms need not share the gloom
That hangs about the stair ;
They may be made the nest of hope;
Their airy height gives pleasant scope
For castles in the air.
Or with some friends from kindred planes
For jovial company,
Some merry hours may yet be spent,
Where bread and butter means content,
And cheese and beer a spree.
But though you say you’re nearer Heaven
Than are the folk below,
These and a lot of things may be—•
Alas, for sad humanity,
They’re seldom really so !
For women who up there reside
Sweat morn to eve, as slaves ;
And oft when labor’s at an end
They fancy that the dark stairs bend
Down —downward to their graves !
A sempstress, stale with toil, is there,
And, dry with loveless years,
Still bends above her ceaseless tasks
To earn the little that she asks;
She has forgotten tears.
And weary wrecks and work-worn serfs
Creep there to seek their rests;
The toil and want of many a year
Has shut the heaven that seems so near,
For ever from their breasts.
And if from some that lovely sky
Some upward looks would earn,
They, city slaves, to work must go:
They rise before its glories show;
’Tis dark ere they return.
’Tis this reward their strivings earn—
A cupboard up a stair !
They work the freshness from their lives
To honey make for others’ hives ;
And this is all their share !
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Till All the Bad Things Came Untrue.
By blacksoil plains, burned grey with drought,
Where desert shrubs and grasses grow,
Along the Land of Furthest Out
That only Overlanders know,
I dreamed I camped on river grass
In bends where river timber grew—
I dreamed I dreamed the days to pass
Till all the bad things came untrue.
I dreamed that I was young again,
But was not young as I had been ;
My path through life seemed fair and plain,
My sight and hearing clear and keen.
No longer bent nor lined and grey,
I met and loved and worshipped you—
I dreamed I dreamed the days away
Till all the sad things came untrue.
I dreamed a home of freestone stood,
With toned tiled roofs as roofs should be,
By cliff and fall and beach and wood,
With wide verandahs to the sea.
I dreamed a hale gudeman and wife,
With sons and daughters well-to-do,
Lived there the glorious old home life,
And all the mad things were untrue.
From blacksoil plains burned bare with drought
Where years are sown that never grow—
From dead grey creeks of dreams and doubt,
Through black-ridged wastes of weirdest woe
I tramped and camped with fearsome fare
Until the seascape came in view ;
And lo ! the home lay smiling there,
And all the bad things were untrue.
n.s.w.
HENRY LAWSON.
Crucifixion.
I had a carven cross of ivory, wondrous white,
And on it writhed a broken God in pain ;
It hung upon my wall, and when I woke at night
The vision purged my soul from carnal stain.
I have an evil love, whose limbs are wondrous
white,
A purple bloom of passionate desire;
A goddess worships she with strange and shame-
ful rite,
The Venus of the handmaidens of hire.
And so I sold my cross of ivory, wondrous white,
No more at dusk my spirit’s flame leaps high ;
But with the gold I gained I purchased my
delight,
And on her breast my soul I crucify.
N.S.W.
WILFRID MAILLER.

THE ALLEGED MAIL-CONTRACT DIFFICULTY.
Even if the Mail Contract falls through, what does it signify? It will only hasten the time when Australia
will paddle its own canoe.

By sending direct to Tiie Bulletin Office Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have Tins Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription . for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
S/>ee.ial Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, ,"wse sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in lllacfe anil White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical draivings in line or ivash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event.,
ivill be paid fur same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
—i-e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—ivill be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under rohich
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 7, 1907.
A FEDERAL LOAN AHEAD.
It is the sign of the statesman, which dis-
tinguishes him from the mere politician, that he
is able to look ahead. All politics are, to some
extent, matters of expediency. Perfection in
legislation is never attained, and every princi-
ple, however true and well-founded, has to
submit to some occasional modification when it
comes to be put into legislative effect;. The
statesman recognises, whilst perhaps he regrets,
this. But in what sacrifices lie makes to ex-
pediency he takes care that no future good is
compromised for the sake of avoiding a present
inconvenience. He is ready to make the right
path as easy as possible—giving it, if need be,
a few curves and digressions—but not to take
an altogether wrong road because it makes
easy travelling to some wayside goal. The Aus-
tralian Parliament seems to be lacking now in
providence. It drifts rapidly and unthinkingly
on to the borrowing reef. The fact that the Aus-
tralian Parliament added nothing to Australia’s
indebtedness that, in fact, it indirectly reduced
the obligations to the foreign money-lender by
making provision for repairs and renewals out of
revenue—has been the one particular bright spot
in the otherwise sordid record of Australian pub-
lic finance. What good it has done it is almost
impossible to over-estimate. Apart from the
direct benefits conferred by staying the mad rush
of a flagrantly improvident people to the pawn-
shop, the non-borrowing policy of the Australian
Jrarliament has done much to purify and ennoble
Federal politics. It has tended to economy in
F ederal ad ministration. It has lifted election issues
from the ruck of road-and-bridge arguments.
If the Australian Government had been a bor-
rowing Government, without a doubt there
would, long ere this, have been found several
avenues (outside the Post Office and military
departments) through which Federal funds could
be scattered to attract unworthy support But
the only money coming in is ordinary revenue,
and that is narrowly—not to say meanly and
grudgingly—scrutinised by the States, all anxious
to get their share, and more than their share ; so
the Australian Government has always been with-
out the means of debauching the constituencies.
* * * *
Now, if borrowing is started, however reason-
able the excuse for the first loan may seem, the
good precedent laboriously established will be
broken for ever, and the way cleared for a Federal
extravagance which will be real, and not the
bogey of a partisan press. What is done once
with seemingly good excuse will be done later on
with a bad one, until Federal finance sinks,
under some future Governments, to the slimy
depths reached in the States, where the junket-
ings of politicians and the outfitting of their
pleasure boats and pleasure houses have been
provided for out of loan money. The duty of
dissent from a pernicious precedent is clear. The
Bulletin repeats that one, and only one, valid
reason can ever be alleged in favor of a national
loan, and that arises when war threatens the
national existence. But Federal administration
rapidly brings the Commonwealth to a position
in which the easiest way out will be a loan—in
which, indeed, there will seem no feasible way out
but a loan—and then the barrier against borrowing
will be swept away, ostensibly for very good reason,
really because the present-day administrators
are too lazy or careless to look ahead and see
the inevitable consequence of piling up ex-
penditure without making any extra provision for
revenue. By pursuing that.policy a little farther,
the time willcome when a deficit which no reason-
able measure of taxation would relieve will be
accumulated, and then “ the absolute necessity of
a loan’’will be mentioned. Yet that “absolute
necessity ” can be provided against now, if the
Australian Parliament will but look ahead.
* * * *
At present the Commonwealth expenditure—
through the growth of necessary Federal services,
and with the provision out of revenue of many
postal and military expenses which the States
used to meet out of loans—is rapidly approaching
its constitutional limit. The time when the fourth
of the Customs receipts will barely suffice for the
needs of the Commonwealth, without incurring
any new commitments, can be foreseen by
anybody with eyes good enough to see
one of Melbourne’s wide streets without taking
two looks. Yet the Government cheerfully incurs
new responsibilities. The Northern Territory is
to be taken over—a sternly necessary step—and
the extra burden on the revenue will be at the
outset probably £250,000 a year. Two trans-
continental railways, both urgently necessary, are
planned, and neither can be built without money.
Old age pensions are seriously talked of. Pro-
bably they are to be paid in cash and not
I O'-U’s. The duties of quarantine and of lighting
the coast will shortly be assumed by the Aus-
tralian Government. Lighthouses, doctors, and
the like cost money. A Commonwealth navy is
proposed, and warships, costing in all £1,000,000,
are talked of. The land defences are to be im-
proved, and, even if the sound and self-respecting
proposals of the National Defence League are
adopted, and military training is treated as a civil
duty and not as a hireling trade, the improved
army and the new navy will cost more than the
present non-existent navy and almost non-existent
army. In the matter of immigration, too, the
Government proposes to do something, and
though talk is the cheapest thing on earth, and
encouraging immigration seems generally to
resolve itself into sending talkers abroad, even to
encourage immigration costs something. Thus,
on all sides, drain-pipes leading from the national
Treasury are being laid. Some of them are small,
some large; the united flow of all represents a
huge depletion of financial resources, and makes
a Federal loan—accursed prospect!—inevitable,
if no inlet pipes are found.
* * * *
Meanwhile, there is no prospect of any very
great increase in the inflow from present sources
to the cash reservoir. True, Australia is pro-
gressing under Federation, and the Federal de-
partments are being, on the whole, administered
with an economy and efficiency unknown to the
States. But as the tariff—by far the largest
source of income—becomes more truly Protective,
it will inevitably become of less value as a revenue
producer. At present a vast revenue flows into
the Treasury, because of the stupidity and wicked-
ness which allows foreigners to make goods, the
manufacture of which should be providing work
for Australian citizens. To that folly a limit will
soon be set, and imports will dwindle as Protec-
tion becomes real. Allowing that the sources of
Federal revenue remain as they are, and giving
full value to the revenue improvements that will
follow growing prosperity, and deducting a fair
sum for the loss of revenue duties as they
merge into really Protective ones, and lose their
value to the Treasury whilst giving a new value
to the Nation—and the prospects are for a de-
creased rather than an increased Federal revenue
in the future.
* • * *
That position has to be faced. With a growth
of expenditure—a vitally necessary growth, for
the expanding limbs of the nation must not be
cramped—with no possibility of any large in-
crease, but a reasonable possibility of "a decrease,
in the revenue from present sources, Federal
finance drifts to a deficit, ’ and a Loan. Means
must be found now to replenish the Treasury
from other sources by using the powers of
direct taxation with which the Common-
wealth is endowed. In neglecting now to
propose that, the Australian Government
is preparing the way for a borrowing policy.
One of the first duties of the Australian Parlia-
ment when it meets again is to find something
like £1,500,000 a year more revenue—not customs
and excise revenue, of which the States will
claim three-fourths, bi t revenue which will
belong solely to the Commonwealth. The Federal
graduated land-tax, income-tax, and other im-
posts must come, and if the burden seems too
heavy, the extravagant State Governments must
learn economy, and make things even by reducing
their expenditure and abolishing some of their
State imposts. At present, N.S. W.—the horrible
example among the States—spends a million or
two more money annually than it did before the
Commonwealth relieved it of over a million per
year of postal, telegraph, military and naval ex-
penditure, and borrows gloriously in addition,
lhat is the direction in which there is room for
economy.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Alleged Mail Contract Difficulty.
■This air is just now full of vague reports and
violently-contradicted rumors to the effect that
something has gone wrong with the new Austr.d-
English mail contract. The Australian Govern-
ment called in good time for tenders, specifying
that it wanted a swift service of large, first-class
vessels. The lowest tender was that of a gorgeous
English syndicate, decorated with names of men
great and eminent in the shipping world—names
that seemed good enough for the supply of any-
thing from a skiff to an ironclad fleet; and its
offer was naturally accepted. The new syndicate
hadn’t the ships, but it undertook to build a fleet
of vessels that would more than meet all require-
ments. Now it is persistently reported (and
denied) that the great syndicate is more or less of
a failure, and can’t raise the money—that it is
about £1,250,000 short of the £3,750,000 required,
and that its attempts to float debentures have
failed. There are also rumors (which are
denied) that it wants the Australian Government
to guarantee the debentures, and further rumors
that the State Governments have been, or may be,
approached to see if they will guarantee any-
thing, and that Bent, of Victoria, has smitten
hard on the fourth button of his weskit and pro-
posed that his State should go bail to the extent
of £150,000. So far, all these things are mist and
mystery, and look very like Tory inventions. But
already the anti-Federal papers, despite all con-
tradictions by the syndicate itself, hungrily take
the reports for granted, and rise in a tentative
sort of way to say that this is just the sort of
thing that might be expected from a Common-
wealth. The parochial papers were bound to say
something like that. If the Australian Govern-
ment hadn’t accepted the lowest tender it would
have been denounced by these same papers from
Sheol to breakfast for corruption and extrava-
gance. If it had adopted the only possible metnod
of making sure that it wouldn’t be left by an
English syndicate—that of refusing to consider
any English tenders—it would have been re-
garded as a lunatic and reviled as a traitor.
Besides, after hearing so much from the parochial
press about the splendid quality of British
goods, the solidity and reliability of British busi-
ness men, and the alleged fact that Britain is
the only place from which to get bishops and
infallible railway managers like Johnson, it
couldn’t be expected to anticipate trouble. But
even if the trouble that is alleged in such a vague
but violent fashion should really come to pass,
there is no reason why this country should turn
a hair. Nothing dreadful will have happened
even then. In such a case, there would be two
simple alternatives:—
(1) To let the contract lapse. To forfeit the contractors’
deposit. Then to call Parliament together in haste and
get authority to build a Federally-owned fleet, so that
Australia could carry its own mails, and thus put an end
to the periodical shindy with private steamship com-
panies. The worst trouble would then be that it w'ould
certainly be impossible to get the new fleet ready
in time. There would be a hiatus between the end of
the present Orient contract and the beginriingof the new
national service, and during that period Australia would
have to depend on the fortnightly P. and O. service, and
on the French, German and Australian-Vancouver lines in
between. This would be no hardship except, in a small
way, to the importer, and he doesn’t matter much.
(2) For the Australian Government to call Parliament
together in haste, and get power to take up (not to
guarantee) the £1,250,000 or so of debentures, which it
seems are to bear interest at the rate of 4& per cent.
The money could be found within a year or two out of
the proceeds of taxation, and if the new syndicate came
to utter grief, and failed to meet its interest, the only
result would be that the Government, as a preferential
creditor, would foreclose, and the fleet would become a
national asset, and Australia would have its Federally-
owned mail service.
With two such easy ways out of the alleged diffi-
culty, and the fact that there is only the (contra-
dicted) statements of the lying Tory press that
there is any difficulty, and the further fact that
even the difficulty it alleges is of no real moment
to anybody save the importer (who doesn’t
matter), the recent foam and fuss are quite
uncalled-for.

The “ S.M. Herald’s” Craze for Imported
Men.
A few weeks ago, before the appointment was
announced of Johnson from England as N.S.W.
Chief Railway Commissioner, it was reported
that one Dent, a man with experience on Irish
lines, was to have the job. And the S.M.
Herald, in its cordial and enthusiastic readiness
to approve of the appointment of anybody, so long
as he was an imported man (and so" long as
Oliver, the eminently successful Australian
manager, who didn’t approve of free special trains
to carry the Sydney morning papers, was shoul-
dered out of his job), published a leading article
cordially approving of Dent. It probably had
never heard of Dent in its life before, but if he
had come he would have been an imported man,
and he would have supplanted Oliver, and what
more could anyone ask ! Under the circum-
stances, this letter, from a man who knows Irish
railways, has points of interest: —
There are 3312 miles of railway open in Ireland. It
takes to run these 3312 miles of line, 270 directors 37
secretaries, and 20 managers. Sir Georgs Findlay'de-
clared that he could do in four days a week all the work
done by all the Irish managers and directors, and spend
the remaining two days fishing in the Shannon. The
ordinary Irish railways (according to Clement Edwards
the author of a recent work on Railway Nationalisation)
have received in Government subsidies about £4,000,000.
Same writer says of the Irish railway system—“ Not
only has it all the evils of preferential rates, but in
a much greater degree than in England. Its rates and
fares are higher than in England, which are much above
those on the Continent. Its deficiencies in facilities and
accommodation are nothing short of scandalous. Its
management is the most wasteful in Europe. And finally
its complete acquisition by the State has been recom-
mended by a Government Commission.
“Rates are so prohibitive that a large amount of
merchandise is carried by road. For instance, there is a
regular weekly, traffic by road from Magherafelt to
Belfast, a distance of 42 miles, the rate being Bs. per ton
whilst the railway rate from Dungannon to Belfast fonlv
40 miles) is 12s. 2d. per ton. *
“It is cheaper to send cattle by road than rail, and it
is cheaper to carry goods to England, and have them re-
shipped to Ireland at through English rates, than to pay
the local rates. Goods are often shipped from the
eastern seaboard of Ireland for Sligo and Badina, on the
west, via Glasgow. A witness before the Committee on
Irish industry stated that it was the common practice of
soapmalters to send their goods to England, and have
them reshipped to Ireland, in order to get the benefit of
the through English rates.
“ The chairman of the Dairy Farmers’ Association
stated, in 1904, that to haul a ton of Irish pork from
Dungannon to Belfast (40 miles) costs 15s. lOd ; a ton of
American bacon from Belfast to Dungannon costs only
10s. Bricks can be carried from England to Dublin
cheaper than from Kingscourt to that city (50 miles) The
Armagh fruit industry has been destroyed by railway
rates. The flax-raising industry has been destroyed in
much the same way. Belfast and the other factory
towns get nearly all their flax now from the Continent
and the West Indies, because, whilst it costs £1 is 8d
to bring- a ton of flax from Stranorlor to Belfast by" rail
(86 miles), it could be landed in the same town from
Ghent (Belgium) for 18s. Bd. per ton.
That was the school in which Dent, the proposed
new Chief Railway Commissioner for N.S.W.,
was trained. As it happened Dent wasn’t
appointed, but when his name was mentioned the
Herald, in its haste to get an Australian out of
his billet, rose enthusiastically to say that the
appointment of Dent would be Just The Thin"-.
Apparently its policy was anti-Australianism
pure and simple. It would probably have ap-
proved of a new' Eskimo manager from Greenland
so long as its countryman, Oliver, got the sack.

The Quick and the Dead.
Consider the dead places, and the live ones, and
the reason of their life or their defunctness. The
old town of Bathurst (N.S.W.) is among thedead
It is a sorry place these days. It is shut in by
big estates which have been rack-rented to such a
degree that the farmer had to tear everything out
of the soil in order to pay the landlord. Now the
impoverished land only yields enough margin in
many cases between its possible output and the
amount of the landlord’s demands to support a
Chinaman, and even the Chow looks hungry. If
Bathurst were deprived of the artificial support it
receives through being a great railway depot it
would sink to the level of a dead-and-gone mining
village. The population is decreasing. The
number of business establishments is diminishing.
Bathurst, Picton, and other dead towns are hor-
rible examples of the strangling effects of big
estates ; and all the dead agricultural centres are
victims of the same trouble. Toowoomba (Q.) used
to be in the same case, and equally dead. But some
time ago there was a considerable cutting up of
the great estates thereabouts, and Toowoomba
resurrected, and now it is on the top wave of pros-
perity. It is inflated with pride about its splendid
hospital, its old people’s home, and other charities,
all locally supported. Its business premises are
all occupied, and it is building more. Its princi-
pal hotel generally tells the stranger that it is
full up, and he has to go to one of the other 14
principal hotels. The population has more than
doubled in 10 years. There are eight State
schools, and a dozen other schools, including a
technical college. Likewise there are over 20
factories of one kind and another, waterworks
and swimming baths, and a tramway system is
projected. But these and a multitude of other
things are the mere inanimate circumstances of
the case; the really remarkable feature is the
people. Everywhere there is joy, confidence,
vim, brag, and cheek; There is joy and cheek
enough to qualify the place to give back-talk to
St. Peter at the Jewelled Gate. Toowoomba,
in its exuberance, reckons on soon being the second
city in Queensland ; it was about sixth four years
ago, and is now third. It doesn’t even despair of
being first one of these days. Meanwhile,
it lets off its superfluous energy on Saturday
nights by street preaching— two or three
sects preach, by the braying of a brass band,
and by running refreshment stalls in the streets,
in addition to going to the theatre, and having
drinks at the 15 principal hotels and the numerous
inferior ones. And on Monday morning it hires
a cab and takes the stranger within its gates to
see the splendid public gardens, and the £lO,OOO
municipally-owned School of Arts, and the gaol
that has been turned into a concert hall, and the
reservoir, and the view from the range, and it
flaps its arms for sheer exuberance. Yet before
the great estates began to be broken up, even
wretched dead old Picton, in N.S.W.— Picton,
with its streets and its look of silence and re-
pose, as if it were always Sunday afternoon there—
wasn’t much deader than Toowoomba.

A Tasmanian Syndicate.
Premier and ex-sea-captain John Evans, of Tas-
mania, was the recipient last week of a document
which should make even that mild old master
mariner boil with indignation, if there is any boil-
ing capacity in him. It was the prospectus of a
company, just floated in England, and called the
Tasmanian Investment and Trust Co. There is a
boundless assurance about the Tasmanian Invest-
ment and Trust Co., and an off-hand way of pro-
posing to deal with other people’s goods which,
if the co. suggested carrying on its operations in
the territory of Premier Price or Premier
Kidston, would make these fiery Democrats long
to get to close quarters with the promoter, and
give him a hurried passage through the window.
The concern proposes to obtain from the Tas-
manian Parliament the right to construct,
and work for its own profit, a railway
from Glenora to Zeehan, and' thus con-
nect the West Coast mineral field with Hobart.
It also proposes to obtain (it is great at obtaining)
from the same Parliament the right to construct,
and work for its own profit, branch railways to
Gormanston coal-fields, to Hamilton, and to
Lake St. Clair. It likewise proposes to obtain
(it is a perfect whale at obtaining) from the same
Parliament, for its own sole benefit, a grant of
490,000 acres of land subject only to a 2J per cent,
royalty on gold and other minerals won from
the ground—in other words, it intends to ask for
a, present of one-thirtieth of the State. The land
is to have a railway frontage. And on top of this
it proposes to obtain (its capacity for obtaining
seems illimitable) from the blessed” old Parliament
already mentioned the right to annex six rivers
gratis, and use them to supply electric power,
which it proposes it should have the right to sell
to the people in any part of Tasmania. The co.
apparently offers not a copper of cash in return
for all this obtainment—only the honor of its
presence in the island, and the favor of its
patronage, and the gleam and lustre of its
top-hat. Tasmania has been foolishly addicted to
dealing with cheeky foreign syndicates in times
past, but still it has its Y.D.L. Co. and the
V.D.L. Co.’s almost untenanted estate still on
hand as a warning; and it has the dreadful,
privately-owned Emu Bay railway, where the
first-class fare is about 3d. a mile and the pace is
about three miles an hour, as an example; and in
view of these things, it will surely decide to have
no truck with the co. which asks for a present of
six rivers and one-thirtieth part of the island.

The Yic. State Labor Party.
Prank Anstey, M.L.A., of the Victorian State
Labor party, writes about the sins of some mem-
bers of that party in connection with the Licens-
ing and Gambling Acts (B. 24/l/’O7) :
As to the Licensing Act, it is said a majority of the
members fought the Bill, and stonewalled in the interests
of Bung. The Bill provided that no person under 21
years of age should be a member of a club ; but it placed
no restriction upon the age of those to be employed in
such clubs. Twenty-seven members (amongst them
Swinburne, Murray, and Graham) voted that under 21
was too young to be a member of a club, but not too
young to pull and serve the poisonous juices.
During the debate on the Bill, William Hutchinson,
member for Borung, acted as Parliamentary mouthpiece
for the Victorian Alliance. In the majority of divisions
upon the Government proposals, a majority of the Labor
party voted with Hutchinson. A majority of the Labor
party voted more often with Hutchinson than did cold-
water Swinburne.
The Bill proposed to extend the monopoly in the license
to the grocery shop owner, and compel the tenant to
meet the extortions of the landlord, or lose the license.
It proposed to extend drink facilities on the railways.
It permitted the tied-house iniquity to continue, repu-
diated fair rent courts, and made a present to the re-
maining owners of the added monopoly value created by
reduction of competitors. The booze on the Bay on Sun-
day, and escape of the breweries from taxation, were sup-
ported by Swinburne and opposed by the Labor party.
Yet the Labor party is held up as the Eighteen Devils of
Perpetual Swill.
The Victorian Parliament consists of the Speaker,
Chairman of Committees, one sick man, six dead men
(never speak), seven members of the Government, 18
Laborites, and 34 others. The indexed utterances upon
the Bill are 819, towards which men outside our party
contributed 610. Seven followers of the Government
(Watt, Murray, Gray, Boyd, Gaunson, Hutchinson,
Keogh) were on their feet more often than the 18 mem-
bers of the Labor party. Next to Prendergast, our most
frequent speaker was Billson, of Fitzroy, a life-long
abstainer, a member of the Rechabite order for 20 years.
This man is said to be the advocate of Bung.
The discussion upon the Licensing Bill occupies 4io
pages of Hansard, or 209 yards of talk, of which one-fifth


was contributed by Bent, Gillott, Mackey, and Swin-
burns in 145 efforts to explain one another. John Murray
designated the Government “ lightning' change artists.”
Peacock (13/12/’O6) said: “ God knows what has been
done ; I don’t.” Swinburne explained. Watt denied
the accuracy of the explanation. Mackey was not
sure, but “ he was under the impression ” that Watt was
correct, and that his colleague’s explanation was so much
fudge. The Government amendments on its own Bill
measure 9ft. Its supporters helped with another 6ft.
The 11 members of the Mackinnon party contributed Bft.
of amendments. The 18 members of the Labor party
contributed only 7in. The Labor party averaged less talk,
moved fewer amendments, and the majority of its mem-
bers voted more often with Hutchinson, of the Temper-
ance Alliance, than did any other section of the
Assembly.
The Gaming Bill was seven Bills in one. The Age said
of it that it carried “condemnation on its face.” The
Anti-Gambling Association (2/10/’O6) said that it drew
invidious distinctions between rich and poor;” and
Bent said he “ didn’t know the silly things were in the
Bill —and, of course, none of them were vital. The dis-
cussion occupies 700 pages of Hansard, or 311 yards of
talk. The indexed utterances are 1080, of which 776
are contributed by members outside the Labor party.
The Government brought in yards of amendments upon
its own Bill, withdrew them, put in others, and withdrew
them and put in others. Bent, Gillott and Mackey put
in 192 explanations of their fluctuating - opinions.
On the 16th Parliament re-assembled. On the 17th the
Age came out with its usual attack upon the obstructive
tactics of the Labor party, and charged it with delaying-
the Gaming Bill by incessant talk upon the Boilers’ In-
spection Bill. It coupled the name of Boyd with the
obstructives. Boyd raised the question under privilege.
It was pointed out that our party had not occupied an
hour of the previous day’s sitting. No publicity was
given to our statements. Upon the Boilers’ Inspection
Bill three Labontes spoke, and occupied 25 minutes.
!~P 01 ? ie Gaming - Bill two Laborites spoke, and occupied
20 minutes. The debate of the 16th occupies 42 pages of
Hansard. Labor members occupy five pages out of the
4- let next day we were denounced as obstructives.
A section of the Labor party did oppose many of the
provisions of this Bill, but not more so than did many
members outside the party. The men who were most
prominent on the Gaming Bill, no matter to which party
they belonged, were the men who are most prominent on
every other measure before Parliament. The record of
the most prominent “obstructive” in the Labor ranks
can find his record matched by the utterances of members
outside the party ; and any one of them of any party can
name other Bills upon which they have been equally
prominent. The majority of the Government supporters
said very little ; the majority of the Labor party said the
! ame -. When at last the Government had some proposals
to which it adhered, the majority of the Labor party in
nla J° nfc y of divisions voted with the Government.
H hen they disagreed, it was from motives just as clean
as upon the propositions with which they did agree If
not, why not ? s “
The avowed enemies of the Labor party charge it with
being the agent of Rum, Wren, and Rome. The Bulle-
tin, in the role of candid friend, affirms the first two but
makes no mention of the third. Nevertheless, avowed
enemy and professed friend are alike in that both request
the general public to accept assertions as an ample sub-
stitute for facts.
In regard to the suggestion that the Labor organisa-
tion of Victoria should sweep out the present men and
put up fresh candidates—personally I have no desire to
be the accepted Labor candidate, either as an act of charity
or through fear of disruption. I shall neither ask fo- the
one nor cause the other. lam at the disposal of the
Central Executive to either go on or go off. I want no
position from the public as a kindness without considera-
tion of worth or fitness, and no nomination from any
body of men dissatisfied with my actions as a representa-
tive.
Anstey remarks, as to the Gambling Bill at
least, that “the record of the most prominent
obstructive in the Labor ranks can be matched by
the utterances of members outside that party.”
In other words, some Labor members were no
worse than some others who are not Labor mem-
bers. The Bulletin doesn’t say they were—
except to this extent, that it is infinitely worse for
a Labor man to lift a finger or wag a tongue in the
interests of Wren or Bung than for the other sort
of man. When and Bung are amongst the worst
enemies of the men whom the Laborites specially
represent ; “ the individual dominated by either
of them is a potential enemy of the worker.”
That was the ground The Bulletin took before
and takes now. And so Frank Anstey, whose
own record in the matter is good and clean,
doesn t disturb the conclusion then arrived at.

For the Bosses’ Sake!
The remnant of the old Lyne-See party in
N.S.W. Assembly, which at the present time
follows Twaddell, is in a quandary. Quite by
accident, Premier Carruthers met Twaddell
somewhere, and it occurred to the mind of the
great Deformer that, after all, there was little
difference between his gang and the crowd that
allegedly followed Twaddell. Twaddell’s
crowd had backed the Carruthers Government
right through the Lands scandals, and had gene-
rally acted the part of the stick by which
the Government climbed out of the mud. A
general election is approaching, and it apparently
occurred to the versatile mind of Carruthers
that he needed Twaddell a great deal worse
than Bent required Peacock of the Laugh and
Donald Mackinnon. It was the rag end of
the Parliament, with only another brief session
ahead, and as Ashton and Barnabas O’Conor
were retiring, being absolutely sure that
they could see the scrawl upon the wall,
it seemed that some good business might
be done with the two vacant portfolios.
So Carruthers proposed that Twaddell and
Perry should join his Cabinet, and that the rest
of the party should join his gang. It is generally
understood that Twaddell and Perry, both
office-hungry, are agreeable, but that the party
cannot see clearly where it comes in. It doesn’t
want to turn on the bosses, now they’ve got an
offer of a decent thing, but when it looks at the
awful load of obloquy which it will have to help
to hump at election-time, it begins to wonder if it
isn’t too big a price to pay for the bosses’ salaries.
The Lands scandals alone are enough to break the
back of any party, but the hideous travesty to
which the administration of justice has been re-
duced, and the amazing fact that the British
Government had to call upon Carruthers
to take steps to prevent the crimping of
British seamen at the port of Newcastle,
make the load fragrant as well as heavy!
To Carruthers this amalgamation means a
lot—or at least he thinks it does. It has come to
be an axiom with the Australian Tory that Labor
wins through the dissensions of its opponents,
and if its opponents cease to have dissensions,
then he holds that it is all over with Labor. The
idea is comforting to Carruthers, but it is
doubtful if there is much in it. At the N.S.W.
election of 1904, twenty members, only seven of
them Laborites, were returned on a minority vote.
That is to say, the Laborites won seven seats,
Which apparently they shouldn’t have won!
through the disagreements of their opponents!
But, on the other hand, the Twaddell and
Carruthers parties gained 13 seats, to which
they weren’t fairly entitled, in the same way. On
these figures there seems just a chance that a
combined Carruthers-Twaddell party may
lose more than it gains, and may wish it hadn’t
spoken.

Now or Never.
Peacock of the Guffaw on succeeding to the
shoes of poor old Sam Gillott, stated that one
Of his first duties would be to “ inquire into the
administration of the Gaming Suppression Act in
Victoria,” to ascertain what effect the new legis-
lation has had upon the totes and toters, and to
take vigorous action if necessary—ha !ha ! Bent,
when he was questioned on the subject, professed
to be well pleased with the working of the Act
thus far. He had been told that the unlawful
gambling places were "not running as in time
past.” But neither Peacock nor Bent cared to
attempt any explanation of the well-known fact
that the betting places in Melbourne, within a
mile radius of the Government offices, are as wide
open as ever to the punting world. In short, the
pair of shufflers would say nothing as to whether
they thought the police authorities had utilised the
new Act to take the action which, by their own
showing, they were unable to take under the old
Act. The people of Melbourne, who practically
accepted Judkins and Co. as their agents for
“reform,” without expressing any admiration for
Judkinism save as an antidote to Wrenism, are
not to be put off with official remarks concerning
the effect of the “reform” Act upon timid
puntgrs. They vfant evidence of its power to
move the police. When Bent and Peacock talk
of making inquiries re the reduced volume of
business which flows into the old unlawful chan-
nels, they are merely fooling with an all important
question. The avowed intention of Bent’s law
was not to scare some punters for a little while,
but to suppress all totes and city “hells” for all
time. As long as Wren’s unlawful establish-
ments are known to be in existence it is certain
that they flourish with the negative sanction of
the powers which ought to wipe them out. And
if they are not wiped out, they must soon be going
stronger than ever, making Wren richer than
ever in the means to fortify his position.

Dam.
A couple of years ago, Engineer-in-Chief
Thwaites, of bhe Melbourne Board of Works,
suggested damming bhe Yarra ab Ivanhoe, aboub
three miles from bhe cib.y in a direcb line, and
making a big lake, from which water might flow
to generate electrical power. The conversion of
the Melbourne tramways into an electrical system
is only a matter of time, and not a long time
either, and the conversion of the suburban rail-
way system is already under consideration.
These demands, coupled with the development of
manufacturing, which will undoubtedly follow an
intelligent revision of the tariff, will call for an
enormous supply of electricity ; and, if Thwaites’
or some similar scheme be not adopted, elec-
tric power will have to be generated in costly
power-houses with expensive coal. The estimate
for forming the Ivanhoe lake, resuming the land,
erecting the dam—doing the whole thing, in fact
—is £322,000, of which no less than £lOO,OOO is
put down for electrical instalment, which will be
required no matter what systeqi is adopted.
Such a scheme would provide 8500 horse-power,
as against 4840 horse-power generated by the big
coal-eating power-house in Sydney. The cost of
maintenance would be a comparative nothing,
and the provision for renewals and repairs and
sinking fund would be much smaller than under
the power-house system. There is a constant flow
of water in the Yarra, and, unless all sheol got
loose, therb is no possibility of the supply failing.
Should there be further demands for electrical
power, Thwaites has worked out an extension of
the scheme, whereby the rivjpr may be further
dammed at different points higher up ; and, as a
whole, he offers, for about a million of money,
a project which would provide all the light
and motive power demanded by a prosperous
city twice the present size of Melbourne.
Other engineers of high repute bear out his con-
tentions, yet, so far, not one step has been taken
towards making even an official inquiry into its
merits. On the other hand, Premier Bent has
spent some thousands already in erecting a power-
house for his Elwood tram. He has further taken
step| to electrify the existing St. Kilda railway
line, and is arranging for the complete electrifica-
tion of the suburban system. When the St. Kilda
line is converted, either another power-house will
have to be erected or the present one extended ;
and the electrification of the remaining suburban
lines will mean that outlying Melbourne will be
dotted over with power-houses costing in the
aggregate infinitely more than the £322,000,
which would supply, not only sufficient electricity
for transit, but for almost every other purpose
for which it is likely to be wanted between now
and the year 1950.

The Cow and the Child.
There was what the daily press calls “a large
and representative gathering” of dairy-farmers
at Penshurst (Vic.), the other day, to inspect the
starting of a set of milking machines on a big,
up-to-date dairy. There were also refreshments
and speeches. And one of the speakers, a local
dairy-farmer, said that the milking machine
being now a proved success, steps should betaken
to do away with the employment of children in
cow-yards. The use of child labor, he urged,
should be made illegal there, just as it is in fac-
tories. From time to time The Bulletin has
condemned the infamous practice of using
children from the nursery or the schoolroom as
attendants on the great god Cow, but this is the
first time to its knowledge that a dairyman has
publicly denounced the custom. This paper,
therefore, feels more cheerful on the subject
of Cow than it has done for some considerable
time. Nevertheless the practice of child slavery
is still so general that, in the heading to this
article, the two animals concerned—the Cow
and the child —are placed in their respective
order of precedence with the farmer. All
oyer dairy-farming Australia, at the present
time, there are thousands of infants, varying
in age from eight to 14, who milk from five to a
dozen or more cows night and morning. These
children then go to school, generally travelling
long distances, where they promptly fall asleep
over their lessons. Some of them—the boy por-
tion—don’t even get the chance to fall asleep.
They are kept at home to take the milk to the fac-
tory, to clean out the cans afterwards, and to do
the dozens of odd jobs necessarv about a farm.
And these boys and girls are the future fathers
and mothers of Australia. If this condition of
affairs were the result of dire necessity—if our
land were so poor and butter sold so cheaply as
to practically compel it—it would even then be
necessary to consider whether it was all worth
the cost. But there is not the shadow of an
excuse for the enslaving of child (with a small
“c”) to Cow(with a big one). Our land is fairly
fertile, the climate is good, and the average
price of butter is so remunerative, that there
is no necessity for a single child under the
age of 15 to enter the cow-yard. There are
only two cases in which the child is, in a manner,
compelled to take its share of the milking. The
first, and most numerous, is where the father has
agreed to pay an exorbitant rent, or has contracted
—on the pernicious “ family ” system—to milk a
herd for a ridiculously low sum. In this case, he
has to sweat his family purely for the benefit of
the land-owner. In the second case, the necessity
is caused by a man paying a high price in pur-
chasing a farm. A farmer with a large family
will often pay £5 or even £8 an acre more for land
than it is really worth ; he reckons on the cheap
labor of his family to make it up, and if he doesn’t
buy it, some other farmer with a family will. He
sweats his family for the benefit of the land-seller.
So the result of legislation preventing the employ-
ment of children as attendants on Cow would be
that exorbitant rents would be reduced, and there
would be a bit of a slump in the prices paid for
dairying land. This, of course, makes it a difficult
matter to secure Parliamentary intervention
The average Parliament regards not only Cow’
but the dirt that Oow stands on, as sacred,

“ A. 8.”: Went through the Abbotsford (Mel-
bourne) Convent, the other day, and am almost
persuaded to become a member of the Protestant
something or other Not that there was the
slightest evidence to back up the wild and woolly
yarns of Parson Tregear. On the contrary, the
place is a model of cleanliness and order. The
wards of the penitents are incomparably better
furnished and more comfortable than those of the
ordinary charitable institution—better even than
the dormitories of the Hawthorn girls’ school run
by Tregear’s particular sect. The washhouses
are perfect in their arrangement, and a model to
private institutions; though, of course, the peni-
tents may be worked hours in excess of the Fac-
tories Act provisions. Further, the alleged herding
of penitents and children is a plain, ridiculous,
dashed lie. The children are kept entirely apart;
even in church they can have no communication
with the ladies with pasts who do the washing.
What I complain about is the convent system
generally. A more intelligent and more sociglly-
attractive set of women than the nuns would be
hard to find, and here they are wasting their lives
inside stone walls when they ought to be outside
married to broad-shouldered citizens and assisting
to populate Australia. I saw one laughing-eyed
girl of about 20, who would have graced the front
row of J. C. Williamson’s best chorus, hiding her
beauty under a hideous cowl. The whole thing is
unnatural, objectionable, and opposed to national
welfare. These women could do just as good
work were they living “in the world.” The four
walls of a convent and the adoption of that form
of sex-perversion called celibacy, are utterly un-
called for, and the sooner legislation is introduced
to prevent a girl imposing lifelong imprisonment
on herself by reason of a sudden access of hysteria
the better for everybody.

Another hard case—from Wellington (M.L.) this
time:—
The defendant, on leaving the Court, exclaimed that
the effect of this would be to drive him out of the coun-
try. He denounced the manner in which the country
was overrun by inspectors, who “ nearly worried him to
death.”
The trouble with this defendant—his name was'
Charles Henry Jones, and he was manager of
the Diamond Confectionery Co. —was that there
werefoundon his premises “576 tins of condensed
milk, the majority of which were ‘blown,’ while
some had burst, giving forth an offensive odor.”
The district health officer swore that this mass of
decayed milk almost certainly contained ptomaine
poison, and that no amount of cooking would kill
ptomaine poison; and the defendant explained
cheerfully that he used the stuff to make caramel
toffee. It was after he was fined £5 and costs,
and had his dreadful substance confiscated, that
he denounced the whole circumstance as another
attempt to drive capital out of the country. If
this is the kind of capital that is being driven
out, it ought most certainly to be washed before
it leaves, if not boiled.

“ Nux Vomica” : I reckon “ Locust” (B. 14/2/’O7)
is a modest man when he says that what he speaks
about may be considered “a small matter.” It
is an almighty big matter. The laxity which
prevails with regard to South Head forts also
prevails with regard to Henry Head and Bare
Island forts in Botany Bay. I have had it darkly
whispered in my ear that persons found in the
vicinity of either of these forts, with the imple-
ments of the artist or photographer in their
possession, are liable to be emptied out of the
said vicinity, and to have the said implements
confiscated. Now, lam an artist, and often
mooch around La Perouse way making sketches,
and I could, without any hindrance whatever,
have made sketches of the forts from all points.
I know people who have both photographed and
sketched them. What sort of military super-
vision is this? Further, up till very recently,
fishing permits were granted to people to fish off,
practically, the wall of Bare Island fort; and the
only thing that prevented one from getting inside
was an ordinary picket gate, which could be
easily knocked down by half-a-dozen invaders
with a few cannons and things, or even by two
men with a bit of broken tree. I don’t know who
is supposed to be in charge of these places, but
he doesn’t seem to be a person who takes his
military biz seriously. I think he must be a
contributor to the comic papers.
Gloomy prophecy by “Caber” from Queens-
land :
If you ask me, I am ready to express the opinion that
you are not attaching sufficient importance to Denham’s
scurry back to his Tory chums. Digby gauges exactly'
the political brew, and knows that the brazen apostacy
of the early Nineties is about to repeat itself. That was
the time when Glassey, Hoolan and Hall battled
against the Griffilwraith, getting a weak support from
the “Remnant”—seven all told—while the Tories, shap-
ing their policy to run on all-fours with the interests of
the big hindieates, called the weak-kneed Liberals to
heel. When the elections transpire, so will many other
events, which are now but seen darkly. It is quite on
the cards that when the Bananaland Parliament meets,
the Kidston Ministry will be ousted, and Robert, with
“ Janny ” Leahy, will have the control of the elections—■
an item worth at least half-a-dozen seats, if Janny’s good
right hand hasn’t lost its cunning. Bell can’t win
Dalby as a Kidstonite, nor can O’Sullivan win Warwick.
And writer cherishes a large-sized suspicion that neither
of them will try.
What Labor has to gain by its virulent hostility to
Kidston, Airey, and Blair is best known to the crowd of
irreconcilables who yell for “ Socialism in our time,” and
want Bob Philp to reach it down to them. But what
Labor will lose is plain reading. Since last election it
has lost Burrum and one of the Charters Towers seats.
The defeat of Jimmy Wilkinson and Dr. Culpin at the
Federal polls is a fair indication that S. Brisbane and the
West Moreton electorates are not safe Labor strongholds.
Any sane person would suppose that these little hints
should have hit something hard, but the Brisbane
Worker keeps jingling its cap and bells, while Philp and
Leahy beckon the Liberals over to their camp. Kidston
must go down. The writing is on the wall, and the Bris-
bane Worker is standing' on its head to read it.

N.S.W. Public Service Association dropped a
mild bomb when it announced in its Journal
that the P.S. Board was becoming every day
more like a creature of the Ministry in office, and
that political interference, which the Board was
appointed to kill, was once again triumphant.
The Association has been such a meek little worm
lately that the service began to suspect it of being
a creature of the Board ; and this announcement
does the service good. Of course, what it says is
true. Not only Ministerial, but ordinary Parlia-
mentary influence counts at the Board’s office.
In fact, the influence is once more applied
openly. Quite lately one of the Departments got
a letter stating unexpectedly that the Board had
granted the application of an obscure rank and
filer, and the letter began—“At the request of
Mr. Pull Ropes, M.L. A., the Board has recon-
sidered,” etc. It seems as if the Board lives in
dread of Parliament reducing it numerically, and
so is in a hurry to placate the Pull Ropes family.
Richard Arthur, M.L. A. (of Sydney), writes :
You call me, not unkindly, a fanatic. Ido not demur.
But it is a fanaticism the outcome of the coolest and most
dispassionate reflection on the future of Australia.
Day and night I have before me the possibility for this
community of the most hideous fate that has ever
befallen a white people. There are some who say that I
am agent for the capitalist and employer—that my secret
desire is to obtain cheap labor for them and assassinate
unionism. Fools and blind ! I am a true friend of the
worker, and the son of the worker. My solicitude for them,
and the ideal Commonwealth, makes me see that the
building of the ramparts must go before, or at least along
Justice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
In microscopic particles our Equity is found
Secreted in disastrous abysses of the Law,
And particle by particle
That marketable article
Is smelted out, till it is free from all alloy or flaw,
By enterprising barristers and magistrates pro-
found,
And through their magnifying glasses scanned
and scanned again,
And when it has been clarified,
And purified and rarefied,
’Tis sold to dauntless plaintiffs and defendants by
the grain.
Those ponderous establishments, elaborately built,
Called courts, with grand interiors designed
majestic’ly,
Are depots where officials who
Have various initials to
Their names, to patrons offer many grades of
equity.
There suitors suing for divorce, and culprits
charged with guilt,
Are profitable customers ; and also heavy fines
Pay very well, though nearly all
The minor counts they merely call
(Like summonses for petty debts) “ the bread and
butter lines.”
This world of sameness wearies, and for innova-
tion yearns,
And connoisseurs despise the same old Justice
every time,
So vendors, to keep up the price—
On clients to heap up the price—
Devise new lines of Justice to experiment on
Crime;
The quality they call “Supreme” brings in the
best returns,
And all well-educated citizens quite understand .
If Justice they have bought to-day
Is an inferior sort, that they
Can change it at a higher Court, and get a better
brand.
A satisfact’ry revenue is Justice to the State,
A compromise for pestilence, droughts, strikes,
and British wars,
That taxes circumstantially
The populace financially,
And institutes a retinue of State Inquisitors,
Who keep the public spending money at a healthy
rate,
Promoting those progressive modern arts so pro-
minent
Called bluster and audacity,
Blackmail, and a capacity
For breeding Bills of Sale, and granting loans at
cent, per cent.
I always scrutinise commercial items in the news,
First stocks and shares and produce markets, then
finance and trade.
Then my attention I devote
To pending sessions, when I note
If J ustice any fluctuation—up or down—has made;
Large claims involving Capital to work them I
peruse,
And superficial “workings” called “Test cases,”
also scan;
And I’m convinced by various
Reports the most precarious
Of speculations is the claim for Justice worked by
Man.
