ALFRED E. BEIDGES.


It usually takes years to get a new invention on
the market, but the Tyree Spray has proved an exception
to the rule. Although this machine has only been three
months before the public, already over fourteen hundred
have been sold, and they are still selling rapidly. All
the Government Departments in Sydney, and the prin-
cipal business houses are now using- them.
The Tyree Spray is pronounced by all experts to be one
of the .most important inventions ever introduced to
public notice. It is being sold largely for, —
Spraying dairy cattle for tick.
Spraying sheep for fly-blow or maggot.
Spraying fruit trees, vines, potatoes and other vegeta-
tion with insecticides.
Spraying hospitals, gaols, asylums, schools and other
buildings with disinfectants.
Painting and white-washing dairies, butter factories,
creameries, piggeries, abattoirs, slaughter houses, poultry
yards, schools, factories, wool sheds, stock yards,
barracks, etc.
Killing prickly pear, gorse, lantana, briars, black-
berries, with arsenic.
Painting green skins for export with arsenite of soda.
Fire extinguishing and cutting out fire breaks as a
prevention of bush fires.
It works without labor, and does the work well. Send
for printed matter to W. TYREE, 314 George-street,
Sydney.*
with, the cleansing and beautifying of the city.
Assume a perfect social state, and each man come
to his own—nay, assume the spirit of altruism and
the golden rule reigning from north to south —
of what avail would all this be when the tide
of Asiatic invasion began to engulf us ? The Japanese
coolie works in the mines at Noumea for lOd. a day.
Does the Australian working man wish to run up against
competition like that ? If he does not, he will put the
immigration of white people, whose standard of living
approaches his own, in the very front of his platform.
And if they be agriculturists, they will do him no harm,
but rather great good. Again, there are those who
accuse me, in my rage for more people, of advocating the
dumping of the riff-raff, swept up from the Old World
slums and ghettos into Australia. This, because I sug-
gested a Jewish settlement in the North, which was to
be composed of picked emigrants, trained or
to be trained to agriculture. I still advocate
this, and also the allurement of suitable immi-
grants from every country in Europe. It is idle,
and worse, to talk as some do, about keeping
Australia for the Anglo-Saxon. In England there is
neither the quantity, nor the quality, sufficient for our
needs. Any policy that looks askance at a man because
he does not speak the English tongue and share English
beliefs and prejudices, will help to invite the disaster
that menaces us. Finally, I cannot do all things. It is
not my business to say what is to be done with the people
when they come here. That is for those in authority.
Sufficient for me if I blow the trumpet of warning with-
out ceasing.

A scheme is afoot in Sydney to give a great send-
off to Oliver, the retiring Chief Commissioner of
N.S.W. Railways. The State owes Oliver a big
debt. Under his management the railways
reached their highest level as a paying concern ;
fares and freights were steadily reduced, and
accidents were almost at a minimum. And
he was not in the position of Eddy, who was
allowed an enormous amount of loan money
with which to do mere maintenance and re-
pair work ; instead, he had to do everything out of
revenue, and also pay off Eddy’s repair and main-
tenance loan. And Oliver toiled under immense
disadvantages. He was called Chief, but legally he
was only one of three, and of the other two one was
old and unmanageable, and the other middle-aged
and cantankerous. Hemade enemiesof Private En-
terprise by his efforts to prevent the Lithgow Coal
Association getting a monopoly of the western
coal contracts ; by his objection to giving con-
tracts for engine-building to private concerns at a
price greater than the figure at which the State
workshops could do the work ; and by his inability
to see why morning papers, owned by immensely
rich proprietaries, should be carried by special
train at the public expense. The S.M. Herald
in particular pursued him with unscrupulous
ferocity. Oliver’s goods rates were far lower than
those of Tait in Victoria, and the Melbourne
Age was much addicted to publishing the two
rates in parallel columns as a protest against
what it called Tait’s exactions ; but even this
evidence didn’t prevent the brutally unscrupulous
Herald, clamoring for Oliver’s discharge so that
he might be replaced by some imported person
who would reduce N.S.W. rates to the Victorian
level. The capable Oliver, with a splendid record
behind him, retires as the victim of a shameless
conspiracy—he retires partly because of the
craze for imported men, and partly because he put
the State’s interests always first. Under the cir-
cumstances, lie deserves well of N.S.W., and it is
up to N.S.W. to hold out a large friendly hand to
him before he goes.
4.
When a quantity of white tiles were wanted, a
while back, for the new Melbourne railway
station, the Mitcham (Vie.) Tile Co. got the con-
tract at £1862, which was 7-2 per cent, over the
lowest English tender, though the Vic. railways
got some of that 7\ per cent, back in freight.
The other day Tait asked for tenders for another
lot, and the Mitcham Co. tendered at exactly the
same rate, although the freights on coal and clay
had in the meantime been about doubled.
The English companies saw a chance to
smash an Australian industry, so their tender
was for £941 13s. 2d., as against Mitcham’s
£1257 13s. Bd. That is to say, in order to knock
out the Australian producer, and have the market
to themselves for the future, they cut in with a
price 25 per cent, below the'Australian’s, or 17J
per cent, lower than their first tender. And the
foreign Tait gave the foreigners the contract!
Now the Mitcham Co. is arguing with Bent, and
pointing out that, though he may pay a few
hundreds more for his tiles, yet as Railways
Minister he gets it all back m freights on coal,
clay and tiles, in workmen’s fares and in other
ways, and as Treasurer he more than gets it back
pi revenue from taxation on men who otherwise
would only harass him as unemployed tile-makers.
Bent is consequently now reconsidering the
matter ; but if the Australian Parliament knows
what is good for Australia, it will save him, by
means of a proper tariff, the trouble of considering
any such situation in the future.
:
Nothing less than marvellous is the S.M.
Herald’s reverence for Johnson. Johnson is
the man who has been engaged in England as
N.S.W. Chief Railway Commissioner, to take the
place of Oliver, the eminently successful Aus-
tralian who didn’t approve of free special trains
to carry the Sydney morning papers. The
S.M. Herald probably doesn’t know Johnson
from a wallaby or a blue giraffe, but it just wor-
ships him. One day recently he crawled into two
successive leading articles. One was about the
scarcity of available State lands for settlers, and
the old paper lamented that there wasn’t a man
like Johnson in the Lands Office to put every-
thing right, and produce land even if there wasn’t
any. The other was about an alleged shortage of
locomotives, and said this among other things
The most sanguine of our business men hardly antici-
pated such a recovery as we have experienced in’the last
three years. The development of the pastoral and agri-
cultural industries in the time has been phenomenal, and
the way in which mining has been encouraged by the
high price of metals has reacted upon our railways at
every section of the system. Even an Eddy would have
been excused for not grasping the possibilities after such
a drought as the one which terminated in 1908. But the
question of importance now is what is to be done ? There
is a definite shortage of locomotives ; and another two or
three good seasons, with a spirited immigration policy
to push on the settlement of our lands, may easily lead
to serious dislocation. Fortunately, Mr. Johnson will be
here directly, and his experience should be invaluable.
And poor old Johnson, coming from a country
where there are no droughts, and no sudden re-
vivals, and where a railway manager never finds
himself expected to carry li million bushels of
wheat in 1903 and over 27 million bushels in 1904,
will doubtless know all about it. Next day, it was
announced that there was no “definite shortage” in
the Railway Department, that the engines aren’t
really being worked so hard as they were two
years ago, and that it is only proposed to order
new ones by way of looking ahead. But that
made no difference in the Herald’s worship of
the great god Johnson.

N.S.W. Premier Oarruthers at Tam worth
One estate of 1350 acres, which under sheep employed
part of the labor of an overseer, one casual hand, and a
few shearers at shearing time, now employs 24 men all
the year round, and 43 at harvest time. Land which
under sheep, returned Bs. to 10s. per acre, under barley
now returns as many pounds. . . . We should get more
into line with France and Denmark, where 80 per cent,
of the people own their own little farms.
But as to the Danish Act, which makes the holding
or aggregating of large estates an impossibility,
Carruthers, as is usual with that indefinite and
unfinished person, was as discursive as an oyster.
Meanwhile, the report of the cry for land in
N.B.W. reads like this:—
For the 14 farming blocks at Brookong a total of 1112
applications has been received. It is expected that 70 per
cent, of the applicants will be sent to the ballot.
Nevertheless Anderson, of the Intelligence
Bureau, states that there is plenty of land avail-
able for immigrants, and instances the fact that
blocks on Myall Creek are still unsold. Of course,
there is plenty of land available—at a price,
mostly a hopeless price. In fact, it’s only the
price that stops people from staking out a bit of
land bounded by, say, George, King, Market and
Elizabeth-streets, Sydney, and ringbarking all the
hotels, and setting to work to grow wheat.

“ Nulla’s ” opinion :
The reason why Germans (and others) hold aloof from
Australian national sentiment is probably because they
ha ve got tired looking for it. And why blame the Ger-
mans for clinging to their old-world traditions, when
Australians (so-called) set the example ? From an Aus-
tralian national standpoint there is little to choose
between the men who shout “ Hoch ! ” and talk of “ Der
Faderland,” when well primed with lager, and the men
who wear green ribbons in their coats on the 17th of
March, or yellow ones on the 12th of July, the men who
wear prickly vegetable and ridiculous clothes at High-
land gatherings, and the men who yell “ Gorsave ” on the
slightest provocation, and speak of a place 13,000 miles
away as “Home,” said “Home” being a spot which
very often neither they nor their parents have ever seen.
As for the prevailing Australian sentiment that
which consists of howling itself hoarse over the
efforts of a man to smite a small ball with a
lump of wood, or of frantic bellowings over the
chances of a weedy horse carrying a mosquito weight
over six furlongs—well, naturally enough, that doesn’t
appeal to the practical Squarehead. Germans and others
became Americanised, and fought and died for America
in its trouble, because they found in Sam’s country a
virile, national sentiment unmarred by the mildewed
monk and monarch superstitions of the lands they
left behind. Until Australia proves—by making the
<£40,000,000 worth of goods it imports every year, by
routing the handful of big landlords who blight the
country, by treading with a heavy hobnailed No. 10 on
the moth-eaten traditions of the Dark Ages, and by pro-
viding means for self-defence—that it has national aspira-
tions, it can’t blame Germans for, in a manner of speak-
ing, marking time.

The waiter’s story :
“ Pietro? No, he not here to-day. He ’way havin’
holiday. Why ? He got oyster fever—too mooch dam
oyster. Las’ night, when no business doin’, Pietro he
start rushin’ up an’ down stair five, ten, twenty time,
howlin’ lika somethin’ awful! The boss he busy, you
know, not notice Pietro fora bit. Then he sing out,
‘ Stoppa ! What the hella you doin’?” Pietro he yell
an’ run upstair lika mad, you know. The boss he say.
‘ Shoot up, you ! ’ An’ run upstair after him, you know !
By-um-bye we hear g-r-reat crash upstair an’ strugglin’,
an’ then Pietro cry an’ say : ‘ They was chasin’ me !
Ev’ry tima I was go upstair the oysters was throw dere
shells at me an’ joomp on my heada. When it is all dark
they will eat my hair. Letta me oop, I tell you ! ’ Then
the boss he say, ‘ By kripe. yon wanta long holiday. You
go an’ sit in the Pitzroy Garden for two, dree days, an’
get your head fix ri’.’ So it is lam here. Did you sav
feesh ?”

♦
“ Robin Hood,” the drama in which Julius
Knight brings his legs Superbus back to us,
transpires in an atmosphere of such artificiality
that, once you get in sympathy with it, you take
anything and everything for granted. That it is
“pretty” goes without saying; Julius Knight’s con-
nection with it, backed by J. 0. Williamson’smeans,
is guarantee enough for that. But, if “pretty,”
it is also often as absurd as melodrama of the sort
in which the chief villain conspires with the
second malefactor in a loud voice, in the most
public place, alongside the pie-stall kept by the
“ comic relief.” More than once it is necessary, in
order to bring the right people in the way of
getting married shortly after the fourth act, to
assume that some of the characters have been
struck with sudden deafness—as, for instance,
when Maid Marian’s Mary Ann seizes a chance
(in itself flagrantly impossible) to send off her
John Little with a message to the invincible
Robin that the heroine needs rescuing.
MISS ELBERT-ORTON as Lady Marian
in “ Robin Hood," at Her Majesty’s, Sydney.
When time and space are things of no con-
sideration, such a brittle substance as history
naturally presents no difficulties to these drama-
tists. As Robin Hood is mostly romance, with
some alleged 12th century Ned Kelly for a
central fact, the dramatists are entitled to do
what they like with him. It has taken only 25
years for us to make Ned Kelly the perfect gen-
tleman who takes off his hat 10 times to a lady
and is a lamb with children, and the world has
had about thirty times 25 years to put a polish on
the Sherwood bushranger ; so at least the modern
person who makes the Sherwood bushranger a
picturesque hero can claim that he merely takes
him at a valuation now centuries old. But with
Kings and such like it is different. King Richard
of the Lion Heart is in this play presented as a
man worthy to be the hero of the hero. He is
the 16st. fairy who arrives in the fourth
act—and a very fine picture did Harry
Plimrner make of him framed in the door-
way of Friar Tuck’s hut. But the thing
doesn’t hang together. Robin Hood is above all
things represented as the friend of the poor, the
enemy of the oppressor, and the most stalwart
Saxon of them all; and Richard is his model—
this King whose awful exactions produced “ Long-
beard” and the first revolt of the “common
people ”in England ! On the other hand,
Eleanor, Henry ll.’s Queen, gets probably worse
treatment than she deserves. John says she
plots with him against Richard, but whether it
is for love of him or hate of Richard he doesn’t
know. Yet it was this same Eleanor who, at this
moment, was scraping up the heavy ransom that
bought Richard out of an Austrian prison. The
drama leaves you to suppose that it was Richard’s
own prowess that got him out of that scrape as it
got him out of others, whereas it is pretty certain
that the Lion Heart’s quarrelsomeness and vanity
got him into the scrape, and Robin Hood’s Saxons
had to bleed money to buy him out of it.
* » * *
These sacrifices are not counterbalanced
by any brilliance in the dialogue. The love-
making passages are especially poor, and Miss
Elbert-Orton, the new leading lady, does little to
rescue them from being obviously commonplace.
A very fine, clear voice she has, though—a voice
that carries far even when she whispers—a perfect
enunciation, and a “presence” that sometimes
recalls our own Mabel Lane, and sometimes
Nance O’Neil. Julius Knight is vigorous and a
trifle husky; also he is picturesque, of course.
All things fall out for the glorification of Robin
Hood, and Julius Knight accepts the special
downpours of Providence in the spirit of a man
who knows he deserves them. And it is the right
spirit; why, anyhow, should Robin start to raise
doubts about the romanticism which it has taken
seven centuries to weave about him—especially
when the romanticism is a paying proposition?
Of the new men who arrive with Julius Knight,
Hubert Willis as Friar Tuck, Gordon Macintosh
as John Little, and H. J. Bentley as Prince John
hit eye and ear hardest; Willis, as the gorman-
dising friar, extracts almost the last ounce of
comedy out of the part, and Gordon Macintosh
(who stage-manages the production) supplies
most of the rest of the “relief.” Harry Plimmer’s
appearance as Richard is so fine that it has forced
itself into the first paragraph, and the little he
has to do is done artistically. With Mias Elbert
Orton come Miss. Mary Godfrey (Eleanor, widow
of Henry II.), in a part in which one dare
not judge her; and Miss Dorothy Sidney, who
turns Maid Marian’s 12th century Adela into a
keen 20th century travelling companion. Walter
Whyte’s three songs are so strong a feature of the
piece that, if the good things deserve mention
first, these shouldn’t have been left till now— bis
singing of “Richard, my King,” and the little
scene that follows, supply the most genuinely
dramatic five minutes in the piece ; and the
scenery and effects keep step with the picturesque
character of the principal robber.
*** * *
To the patriotic Australian who likes his
bellowdrama cast amid local scenes, where the
hero, instead of dying in a huddled heap in a
snow-storm, wanders through a bush-fire with his
tongue hanging out. the news that the Drought
will definitely stop Breaking at Sydney Royal to-
morrow (Friday) night is distressing. The Hurri-
cane Club will be dissolved and the building
presumably let as a mission hall to the Chows
who frequent Paddy’s Market. Also, the
doctor’s gorgeous flat, where the painted lydies
came in for champagne breakfasts, will also be
available, at a reduced rental, till the character of
its former tenants has been lived down. A revival
of that fine play, “White Heather,” will bring
the Blandolt season to a close with a gallop of
seven nights only.
** * *
Out at Wonderland City (Sydney), Asiatic
weddings and nugget hunts have given place to
buck-jumping and wrestling. Jack Prendergast,
who will be remembered as one of the victims of
Bobs when Martini’s show was running in Syd-
ney, has a squad of outlaws in the city, and also a
bucking mule. The bucking mule is a great
institution. Prendergast also offers the owner
of any horse that can throw him the sum
of £5. for the fun of watching it done.
Bobs is at present far away, and, presum-
ably, when he draws this way that offer will
be off, or Bobs will be barred. The wrestler is
Ben Hur, described as a “pocket Hercules.” His
weight is 9st. 21b., and he offers prizes to anyone
under lOst. 51b. who can wipe the floor with him.
His offer was promptly accepted by Tom Curnow,
the lightweight champion of the State, and the
contest, under catch-as-catch-can rules, was
maliciously arranged for Tuesday night, just as
this paper had fled to the press.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli programme is the same
mostly song-and-dance programme that it was
last week, except that Annie Laurie has arrived
to make it more so, and Selbo, the plate-juggler,
has gone, and that has made it more so, too.
The foregoing sinful verses are by way of
emphasising the fact that “The Squatter’s
Daughter” is an unabated success at Melbourne
Royal. For three more weeks the lurid villains
will be put to shame at the end of every act, and
the scenery, the stock-whip cracking, the dead
snake and the alleged bull-ants will continue as
before, also two Australian shearers will shear a
couple of Australian sheep, what time the ex-
cited supers applaud, with hands and feet, as
though they were assisting at an Irish jig contest.
** * *
“ Mother Goose ” has reached the Last
Nights stage, and from various inland and coastal
towns of Victoria the local populace is being en-
ticed to Melbourne by means of “ Mother Goose ”
excursion trains at special panto, rates. A visit
to the panto, begins to present itself to patriotic
minds as a somewhat solemn duty to the State.
Every right-thinking farmer and grazier feels
that “Mother Goose” is the Naples of theatri-
cal attractions. He must see it and die happy.
A first-class'M.G.E. ticket entitles him to a ss.
view of the panto, one supposes. If a second-
class passenger ranks as a throe-shilling sitter at
the panto., it naturally follows that the back seats
of the pit and amphitheatre are reserved for
humble agriculturists who booked themselves as
mere live stock or luggage. The penalty for
travelling on a Mother Goose ticket and not see-
ing the panto, is vaguely understood to be some-
thing awful, without the option of a fine.

“ Cinderella ” is doing heavy business at
Adelaide Royal—especially it is doing heavy
matinee business —in spite of the heat. John F.
Sheridan has most of the comic relief; the other
people whose business it is to be funny don’t
work overtime at it, so to speak. A semi-
public controversy is going on as to .whether
Heba Barlow imitates Nellie Stewart’s manner-
isms ; general verdict that there is no possible
doubt whatever. Irving Sayles has brought back
himself and his cheerful smile to the Tivoli; the
Coleman sisters are at the Hippodrome ; Broncho
George has 50 buckjumpers out on a vacant city
allotment every night; and the military forces
put in an evening on the Exhibition Oval, showing
how at Rorke’s Drift a rampart of flour-sacks and
biscuit-tins was successfully held against the
Zulu—whom nobody calls our Colored Brother,
though he is one of the best of the savage races.
Swimming “carnivals” are frequent, and are the
form of entertainment best suited to the recent
Adelaide weather.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Indignation is gathering round the head of a leading
musical critic on a big paper in a big Australian
city. Not content with criticising the performances of
any young lady who desires to appear on the concert
platform, this dreadful person criticises her personal
attractions, and decides how many kisses he must have
before the youthful artiste can hope for a favorable notice
in his column. A recent refusal to grant “just one kiss ”
was met with a criticism of the lady’s ability quite
sufficient to spoil her professional chances for years.
The musical critic’s advances have of late been more
ardent, and a public scandal is about due.
The advance booming of George Musgrove’s German
Opera Co. includes a painful allegation of humor on the
part of the principal tenor. The newspaper story, w'hich
is fully six inches long, states that when Musgrove and
the tenor were dining together at a Melbourne hotel
there was no lager beer on the premises, so the manager
proposed to order a barrel of it from the German steamer.
Whereupon the humorous tenor remarked that a steamer
which had carried fifteen German opera singers for some
weeks couldn’t possibly have any lager beer left aboard.
It doesn’t sound like a very judicious puff paragraph.
The Australian musical public is not likely to form high
expectations of an opera co. that is accused, even in jest,
of such an enormous thirst for beer.
“Peacock”: The Melbourne panto. girls have several
swimmers in their ranks. Besides Miss Inez Stach
(mentioned by “0.K.” some little time back), there
are two famous swimmers—Miss Mabel Simmons and
Miss Ruby Dolby. Mabel, besides being dainty on the
light fantastic toe, is the winner of many races, and
Ruby, although she has not competed in a race y'et,
makes Mabel use the whip at the finish. Ruby, although
still very young, is also the possessor of a voice which
has been pronounced by experts as exceptional.
Five Kelly Gang biographs are now operating in Aus-
tralia under the management of the Taits. If Ned Kelly
were not dead he would be entitled to charge a commis-
sion.
“ J.A.0.” :Re “ Ruddigore” par. (B. 28/2/’O7). The
opera cannot fairly be regarded as a “ conspicuous
failure ” ; on the contrary, it had a considerable measure
of success. The principal reason why it was not often
produced out of London was the very costly setting of the
second act, where the ancestral portraits became ani-
mated, and descended from their frames— vide advt. for
Somebody’s Whisky. Again, it required a tenor who
could dance a sailor’s hornpipe, but that was a difficulty
which could be got over—Durward Lely, who some years
ago gave Scotch entertainments throughout Australia,
made special saltatory studies for the first production,
and leaped in the air to the satisfaction of a large audi-
ence.
“ Hamer” : The “ Kelly Gang” will never be the stand-
by for the despairing Thespian that “ East Lynneis,
because it takes more people to play ; and the despairing
Thespian only plays “East Lynne” when the soubrette
has announced that she has packed her trunks, and the
comedy man has sworn to be funny no more without
gold, and the old man has determined to cease the supply
of bank notes to his spendthrift son until he has himself
been paid at least ss. “ Kelly Gang” won’t meetcircum-
stances of that sort. “ East Lynne ” can be played with
five people, and that’s its merit. One actor-manager
played it with four.

The London music-hall strike originated entirely in the
interest of the “ small ” people—that is to say, the low-
salaried performers, the scrapers of fiddles and blasters
of trumpets, the stage-hands, and the Gibeonites of the
profession generally. As already hinted in these columns,
the trouble was caused by the matinee-chucked-in
iniquity. A noteworthy feature of the situation has
been the large contributions to the strikers’ fund made
by costly stars who don’t care whether the managers
like it or not. Many of those stars joined the strikers
and refused to appear, thereby losing big money. For
instance, Joe Elvin and his company, and Arthur Roberts
and company, were receiving, respectively, £175 and
£l6O a week for two halls nightly. Among those known
to. Australians are Marie Lloyd, receiving from two
houses £l5O weekly; Alec Hurley, one performance
nightly, £B5; Little Tieh, two shows, £150; and Paul
Martinetti and troupe, £llO. Mrs. Brown Potter didn’t
want to be torn from the shekels, and still continued
doing her “ turn ” at the Oxford. The strikers’ com-
mittee wrote to her, pointing Out the error of her waj's,
but with what result is not stated. On the other hand,
the managers, in voices choked with emotion, declare
that they are ruined by the vast salaries they have to
pay, and it seems that they only keep open out of philan-.
thropy.
“ G.T.” : Lalla Miranda, noticed in the cables t’other
day, is the same Lalla who for years charmed the neigh-
borhood of the Bluestone Mansions of Nicholson-street,
FitZroy, with her “practising,” and who 15 years agone
sent Melbourne crazy with “ After the Ball.” The song
has long since been given Christian burial. The melan-
choly push, with its dirge of “ Harf-tertheßor-r-r-r-r-rll,”

“Melfc.”: Pardon me, but I do think that Pastor
Carey’s baptisms in the Collins-street church, last hot
Sunday night, ought to be impounded by a dramatist as
a new and vivid situation. The marble tank sunk in the
stage was duly repleted with water, and it was a delight-
ful baptismal night, hot as Calcutta. In went Carey, up
to his waist, amid the lovely green ferns and fronds
which made a sweet Palestinian Pool of Siloam. Six
young ladies were severally baptised. Number one, in
black with white collar, was tenderly led forward by an
experienced young lady in white. Firmly the acolytess
stepped into the water with nary splash. Carey had his
back to the people.' The girl stood sideways. With con-
summate tact Carey ducked her right under backwards
and up again to a superb choir and organ accompani-
ment. Out she went dripping, through red curtains, at
the other side of which she was seized with the prompti-
tude of lady attendants in a Turkish bath. I declare that
none of the plays have anything to show so touching and
beautifully dramatic.
A one-time bosom friend of the great actor declares
that he perpetuates and emphasises his familiar chin
dimple by sleeping with a grain of shot in the ravishing
indentation. The shot is held in place with a bandage.
Some years ago, when he was theatrical critic for the
Saturday Review, George Bernard Shaw made this con-
fession : “ With the single exception of Homer, there is
no eminent writer, not even Sir Walter Scott, whom I
can despise so entirely as I despise Shakspeare when I
measure my mind against his.”
“ Kodak ” : This is the age of peculiar amusements.
Nowadays the ordinary girl loves to get on board a Sadly
Circulate (miscalled Merry-go-Round) and revolve
slowly, at enormous expense, for about five minutes,
while a steam organ shrieks “ The Belle of New York.”
If she doesn’t care for the Sadly Circulate, she has a
passion for slithering down a steep incline —at fearful
expense—and getting her sacred hair-pad dashed with
spray. If she doesn’t fancy either the Slither or the
Sadly Circulate, she loves to go—at horrible expense—
into the Tangled Tomb, and have her complexion blown
off in flakes by an artificial wind while she squeals and
clutches in the dark. Finally, if none of the foregoing
sports appeal to her, she can pay some more money,
climb up a long stair, and fall down a polished trough.
Isn’t this sort of thing destructive of the national dig-
nity?

Said the magician : “ For some weeks after we opened
in Maoriland we played to crowded houses—turned away
money. Then a Bulletin par. disclosed my identity with
a certain political aspirant at the last Victorian elections.
After that the local rag in every town, on announcing my
arrival, copied thisinformation, and business immediately
slumped. The remainder of the tour was a frost. While
my stage-name caused people to imagine that I was
imported I coined money. As soon as they discovered I
was a mere local product the large audience mostly
stayed at home, or patronised shows in which the artists
performed some of my feats, and joked in broken English.”
Marie Hall, a girl violinist who learned from Kubelik’s
master, and is said to produce something of the same
“singing” tone that has made the boss pupil famous,
will start an Australian tour in May.

American interviewer Vance Thompson tells of a Bern-
hardt experience. He was once sent by his New York
editor to ask Sarah to write a Christmas story. She
agreed, remarking, “ You will arrange the details with
my secretary.” Said the secretary, “Let me have a
copy of it in French as soon as possible.” “In French?”
“Yes, she will want to read it before she signs it.”
Thompson realised the situation, told his editor, and was
allowed a few days off to write the story—a pathetic
little thing called “Noel.” Sarah read it, approved,
and it was printed in both French and English,
with Bernhardt’s stunning signature facsimiled. Years
rolled by, and Thompson had forgotten all about
“Noel.” He had occasion to call on Bernhardt
in Paris. While waiting for her appearance he wandered
to and fro, looking at busts and statues—by the great
actress. He picked up a book, an edition de luxe. It
was charmingly illustrated—by the great actress. It
was “ Noel.” The interviewer was staring at it when
Sarah came rustling in. “ Oh,” she exclaimed, “have
you read it? A little thing, but real. It was an event
in my own life that haunted me and haunted me until
I simply had to write it—a fragment of my childhood.
Ah, those days, those days ! ” “It touches the heart,”
said Thompson. “You like it?” she asked brightly.
“ Madame, it is your masterpiece,” he said —and he meant
every word of it.
When Edwin Booth returned from Sydney, in 1856, he
played an engagement at Detroit. The manager showed
him a draft of the playbill, with profuse adjectives.
“ Oh,” said Booth, “ I can’t have this rot. Simple Edwin
Booth will do.” The playbills appeared all over the town
with—“ Engagement for One Week Only of Simple Edwin
Booth.”
“N.L.”: I think the actresses who are raised (or fall)
to the peerage ought to be spoken to severely. They
and other healthy plebeian damsels who marry titles are
only prolonging the agony of the old nobility. (The
modern “beerage” is left out of this discourse.) The
real blue-mouldy nobility would have perished of inani-
tion long ago if it had been left to marry among its own
class, as it had got into the way of doing before the craze
for actress or American wives caught on. Why doesn’t
the dramatic profession look after its self-respect, and
exclude stage peeresses from all parts of the theatre as
blacklegs ?
The mask idea in minor vaudeville business is just now
being considerably overworked. The notion seems to be
derived less from the old harlequinade than from novel-
ettes, in which the younger son of the Duke of Fitz Cra-
yfish has to earn his living by warbling in the streets, to
the guitar accompaniment of the lowly maid whom he
has rashly married against his stern parent’s wish. Of
course the duke accidentally hears the musicians, pene-
trates their disguise, and forgives all. This is all very
well in the Empress’s Halfpenny Novelette, but some-
how the hearers of fifth-rate variety performers at a
suburban hall don’t seem able to believe that they are
listening to five wronged earls and two crushed coun-
tesses. In fact, some masculine members of the audience
go so far as to assert that “ those toms must be blamed
ugly,” and also cast grave doubts on the sanity of anyone
“ wearin’sich a nose-warmer on a ’ot night like this.”
Still the masking continues, even when nearly everyone
present knows that the mysterious ones are merely
Gerties and Harolds from an adjacent suburb.
“Cockaigne”: Tree’s sumptuous “Antony and
Cleopatra ” provokes thought on how the cuddle and
hugger-mugger have got into the drama. Vicious as the
days and plays of Wycherley, Etheredge, and so forth
were, their audiences would never have endured the
squeezedom of the modern play. We have reached the
stage of the girl who sat on a log, and was grabbed by a
grizzly bear from behind, when she exclaimed “Squeeze
me harder, Charley 1” the said Charles having scooted up
a tree. A crisis of perspiration is substituted for one of
passion. How obviously Cleopatra stood in the eligible roles
of Mrs. Siddons ! Yet she never acted it. She said “If
should feel degraded if I acted Cleopatra properly.” Nor
would Mrs. C. Kean act it, though she and her husband
were so cut out for Cleopatra and the weak Antony. The
human compression in the part didn’t appeal to them.

are evidently numbered. Every time I have been to a

time—ah !” or words to that effect. Here are a few of
the things serios have recently told me :
(1) Walk on the sunny side.
(2) Be good to mother.
(3) Give and take a little hit.
(I) Be careful.
(5) Take the latch-key.
(6) Pay the rent regularly.
What is the reason of it ?

The fault of Bruce Smith is, not only that
he goes among Japs and Chows for information to
enlighten Australia, but that he doesn’t collect
the right kind of information from these sources.
Now, why has he not drawn attention to the
Mikado’s new Constitution ? It may be remem-
bered how valiantly B. Smith fought against pay-
ment of members in N.S.W. Parliament, and
how, when the measure was passed, he professed
to refuse, for a while, to take a penny of his
salary. Well, the Mikado seems to have had a
few like Bruce. Accordingly the new Constitu-
tion lays it down that any member who refuses to
accept his salary, “through pride or other
reasons,” shall be subject to fine and dismissal
from Parliament. Why doesn’t Bruce Smith find
out Jap facts like that ?
A woman sends this
I was threatened, cajoled, forced into joining an artists’
picnic party. I wasn’t warned, though, until well on the
journey, that most of the odd pairs were kindred souls.
“ Kindred souls ” means that two people with an approxi.
mate lack of physical attractions, and an identical
degree of talent, hold hands surreptitiously, and
talk platonism with their noses almost touching and
moist, glistening eyes. Beside the souls, there were two
couples with gross ideas, a lugubrious male, and I. When
we reached the idee-al glade which was our goal we pro-
ceeded to feed. I had brought a salad, which the sepul-
chral personage devoured appreciatively. He unbent to
me a little after it. The star of the party was a lady who
looked like a water-carafe bound in green lustre, and who
was on the point of converting her twin-spook into a mere
matrimonial mate. She was horrid. She wouldn’t talk.
She’d only quote from Yeats’ “ Land of Heart’s Desire,’’
and try to look like Monna Lisa. She wouldn’t eat—she
scorned every edible, from draught beer to my salad.
Ocasionally, she said, she partook of an olive with a
little cayenne.
Presently they all drifted out of sight—in opposite
directions—to renew their investigations into Plato.
And the dejected one and I were left alone. He
brightened up a trifle, and said things about “the won-
drous purple in the brown, brown earth.” Also, he asked
me if I made the salad. He said that although I was
plump, and did my hair like a danseuse, he was in sym-
pathy with me, and “understood.” (What. I really
can’t guess.) He said when he was old and grey he
would like to retire to a delta in a river marsh, and die
sitting on a rush chair with his feet in the water. On our
way back to town, he confided to me that in the cup-
board of his life there was a skeleton—who played the
’cello !—and asked if he might call on me. I told him I
lived in Brisbane. Then I fled at the first opportunity.
Never again will any human lure induce me to join an
artistic picnic party. Never 11
A modest little improvement on present
Australian railway and tram services would be a
“complaint box” at the various stations, into
which travellers could drop their tales of sorrow
about unpunctuality, incivility, and the hundred
and one other ills that now keep them prancing
up and down the stairs of newspaper offices. It
would save the newspapers trouble, and produce
many precious records of language hot off the
stove, so to speak. The complaints would be
written against dirt bins, on leaves torn out of
pocket-books, on the backs of old letters—on any-
thing, in fact. The aggrieved railway traveller
would serve up his grievance strong and fresh,
and garnished with curses, and it would be very
different from the pale, anaemic thing he writes
when he gets home, and sends to the press. That
may look violent enough, but compared with the
shriek written in the pub. at the tram terminus-
written in blood and beer, so to speak—it would
be as a moonbeam is to a burning kerosene bond.
“ N.L.” : Be par. (B. 14/2/’o7)on woman’s
reluctance to adopt the masculine fashion of horse-
riding. It is not so in all localities. A Gippsland
(Vic.) girl tells me that she and her sister have
attained notoriety as the only two equestriennes
within a wide radius who cling to the old style of
riding. After some recent straddle-seated ex-
periments, they, too, have decided to abandon
the uncomfortable side-saddle and its frequent
accompaniment of “needles and pins” in a
numbed limb. The divided riding skirt is also
fairly common in parts of Western Victoria.
From a Fat Man’s diary :
I had just alighted from the tram this morning, and
had called to mind that I bad again forgotten to pay my
fare, when a rough-looking person touched me on the
coat sleeve. I brushed the spot quickly, and turned
round to remonstrate with the fellow, or, if he were im-
pudent, to give him in charge ; hut before I could do so,
he removed his hat with a respect that might be copied
with much advantage by the whole of the laboring
classes, and handed me a sovereign, which he
believed I had dropped in leaving the car. I could
not help being impressed with the fellow’s
honesty, in spite of his clothes, and after making
a few remarks appropriate to the occasion, I took the
sovereign and rewarded him with sixpence, and resumed
my walk to the warehouse. No doubt he had mistaken
me for someone else, but his intentions were good, and
my duty in the matter was clear. Qualities of upright-
ness deserve every encouragement amongst the working-
classes.
The incident, coupled with the fact that I had just
read in the papers that, the Gas Company was about to
pay al5 per cent, dividend, put me in good spirits. A
distressing scene followed, however. As I reached
my business-house, I was accosted by a thin,
shabby woman, who either shivered with cold
or pretended to do so. She, too, plucked at my arm.
Recalling the previous incident, I turned to her
pleasantly, and said, “ Well, my woman ?” She blushed
slightly', and, grasping my hand suddenly, she poured
out her story, which moved me deeply'. Her husband, it
appeared, had been in my employ for 20 years, but had
been killed in a lift accident 12 months before. Since
then her family of six young children had depended
entirely on her exertions for existence, and she was now
too weak, from want of food, to work any longer. They
had not even the means of making a fire on this cold day.
I was deeply' moved, and, feeling in my pocket, handed
her a match., I could not bear to think of the poor
people being cold. Her tale was probably exaggerated,
but I had no time to inquire into its merits, so acted on
the spur of the moment.
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co. report : —At last
Monday’s and Tuesday’s wool sales akeen demand existed
for all descriptions, with an upward tendency in values.
Although very little wool is now arriving, there is still a
fair volume on hand to be disposed of. In the absence of
many buyers at the Brisbane sales, transactions in local
scoureds and passed-in lots during the past few days
have been on a limited scale. In accordance with roster
arrangements, our next sale is fixed for 11th March, when
we anticipate offering about 1000 bales, comprising many
notable Queensland brands.*
“ Kodak ” : Going down Bourke-street,
Melbourne, the other day, I saw a happy-looking
derelict smiling like a sleepy cherub at a heavily-
laden lorry climbing the hill to Menzies’. The
lorry was ai’ranged like an altar to Bacchus.
Next the driver was a cask of ale, and the rest of
the space was tightly packed, with pretty well
every variety of bottled joy. The liquidator
stood for a moment speechless on the kerb, then
raised his hat and solemnly remarked “Hurrah!”
It was the finest tribute I have ever seen paid to
Beer.
THE PATHETIC BALLAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The experienced playgoer always knows when the
pathetic balladist is due. The orchestra commences to
churn slowly, as if an evil spirit w'as pouring out the
accumulated musical dregs of 20 years. Then the singer
strides on to the stage, wearing a depressed expression,
and remarks in a high nasal voice :
The Old Man Bowed His Head.
Ist Verse.
Or. night, when walking down the street, an agfed man I
saw,
Who held a crust of bread in either hand.
I knew that he was famished as the food I watched him
gnaw,
And I asked him, as before him I did stand, ,
“ Oh, why are you so hungry, dad, and why are you so
cold ?
The Summer and the Autumn both are fled.”
God knows what dreadful narrative that old man might
have told,
But instead he gave a sigh and bowed his head !
Ist Chorus.
When the Summer and the Autumn days are fled,
Then it is I wonder what he might have said ; '
But he sat there in the cold,
He was hungry—also old (pause),
And he gave a sort of sigh and bowed his head.
2nd Shrielc.
The years rolled on. In foreign lands I made a fortune
large, «>
And, coming home, was walking up the Strand,
When suddenly I heard a voice say, “Take the man in
charge !
The old one with the bread-crusts in his hand I”
“ No, no!” I cried, and laid my hand on the policeman’s
arm.
“ The man is old, as all of you can see.
While I have strength old Daddy here shall never come
to harm,
And all of you can take this tip from me ! ”
2nd Chorus — (very proudly).
When the Summer- and the Autumn days are fled,
Then it is you will remember what was said.
When you sit there in the cold,
And you’re hungry—also old,
P’r’aps you’ll give a choking sigh and bow your head !
3rd Shout.
I looked about and saw the tear-drops stealing down each
cheek.
The pTeeceman cast his eyes upon the ground.
I saw that he was sobbing, and I knew he could not
speak,
Nor could any of the people standing round.
But, in an instant—so it seemed—l heard a voice, “Stand
back 1
I will not see my father want for bread 1 ”
And there he knelt, that bearded man, and cried, “ Oh !
Dad—it’s Jack ! ”
The old man gave a cry and bowed his head.
Last Chorus—(very tenderly).
When the Summer and the Autumn days are fled,
Then it is t hat we remember what was said.
Ah ! he sat there in the cold,
But (impressively) he had a heart of gold !.
And he gave a hero’s sigh and bowed his head !
First Violin —Bow-wowed his head !

The doctrine that one Chinaman is like
every other Chow was exemplified in a Sydney
police court last week, when the master of a Ger-
man steamer charged a Mongol with having
deserted from his ship a few months ago. The
German captain said that all the heathens on
his steamer were alike to him, and admitted that
he really couldn’t swear that this one was his
missing Asiatic. On the other hand, cer-
tain witnesses were pretty sure that the de-
fendant was very like a Buddhist they
knew who had resided in Bathurst for
years. The case vanished in a haze of
uncertainty and costs.
John Findlay, who was some weeks ago
acquitted of the murder of Mr. Rennie, a school-
master at Papaltio (M.L.) was subsequently con-
victed of burglary at Rennie’s house about the
time of the murder, and sentenced to five years’
imprisonment. The Judge went out of his way
to say that a verdict of guilty on the burglary
charge practically convicted the accused of the
murder of Rennie. But there are cases on record
where a man, intent on one crime, found that
some one else had been before him with a more
serious one. Jonathan Bradford, landlord of an
inn in England, conceived the idea of robbing a
wealthy traveller sleeping under his roof, and,
going to his room, found that the man had been
murdered, and a dagger left sticking in his breast.
Other lodgers were attracted by the noise, and
found Bradford standing beside t' e bed with the
gory weapon in his hand. Bradford was hanged,
declaring his innocence of murder, but admitting
that he had gone to the room with the intention
of robbery. Some time afterwards, the dead
man’s servant, when himself dying, admitted the
murder, but his statement made no difference to
J onathan Bradford, who remains deceased to this
day.

Chief Justice Darley, of N.S.W., got on
the high horse last week with disastrous results
to a cocky. This individual found some sheep
running about on vacant land, and impounded
them. The owner not appearing, he shore the
sheep and sold the wool, under a mistaken idea of
the law re mislaid sheep. Later, the owner
turned up, and Judge Docker gave the cocky six
months. His attorney handed in two written
objections to the conviction, and the case came
before the Full Court in Sydney on a point of law.
The Chief flung the case out without waiting to
hear counsel in opposition, remarking that
it was like someone’s impudence to submit
such a ridiculous point to him. Two days
later barrister Boyce humbly appealed to
the C.J. to hear the case again. Defendant’s
solicitor had not been notified, and the two points
of law he had taken were quite different points,
and they hadn’t been heard at all, and the point
submitted to Mr. Darley was some derelict point
that had been washed up on his Honor’s doorstep
by mere accident. As regards the real points,
Mr. Boyce now asked, quite friendly-like, that
they be discussed there and then. The Chief
then flounced with rage, bounced on Boyce,
trounced the attorney, and announced conviction
confirmed, all in one breath. Amid all this con-
fusion the cocky remains in possession of his six
months.
In the fashionable suburb of St. Kilda
(Melbourne) a policeman recently found something
against a fence in the dark, and could hardly
decide whether it was a boy or a vulture. It was
five feet high and said it was nearly 20 years old.
It also said it intended to commit suicide. It had
neither boots, hat nor shirt, and it had large
callosities on its knees, and tremendous claws on
its feet. Also it was dirty, and it said it had
only been allowed out of the house twice in 18
years, and then only for the dissipation of getting
its hair cut. It was plainly the policeman’s duty
to arrest the apparition, and as it could hardly be
charged with bigamy the case was called one of
insulting behavior. Inquiry revealed the fact
that the name of the spectre was William Spillane,
that its father was a grain dealer and its grand-
father a wealthy jeweller. Its sister, aged 17,
admitted that the apparition had to do all the
cooking, scrubbing, etc., for the family’s seven-
roomed villa, and that it picked if*own meals in
the scullery. The sister thought it had exagger-
ated its case, but on further consideration she
apparently couldn’t remember any occasion in the
last dozen years when it had been allowed out
except to have its hair cut. The pathetic object
was temporarily remanded to the Neglected
Children’s Department, which seems to leave the
unfortunate family rather short of a domestic
servant.
* * * *
Gent One has been so frequent in the
Sydney courts of late that it is time he was called
Bloke Numerous.
* ¥ * *
Victoria lately had a burst of tropical heat
for the first time this year, and Baracchi, the
Government weather prophet, got a chance to be
more strikingly inaccurate than usual. He didn’t
see the heat wave coming, but, when it had
arrived on the Saturday, he ventured a confident
prediction that it would stay until Monday,
when a thunderstorm and cool change would
ensue. No thunderstorm happened. The Mel-
bourne temperature rose on Monday to 104 in the
shade, whereupon Baracchi mopped his brow,
and predicted further warm weather. This was
the cue for the entrance of a “ cool change,” after
which the unhappy prophet became hopelessly
demoralised. The weather shows no considera-
tion for Baracehi’s forecasts. It seems to have
“ got him set,” and heaps scorn upon him.
« • • •
A youthful lawyer in Adelaide reckons
that his luck is altogether too bad. He took the
family housemaid out for the night; and next
day she came and poisoned herself in his office—
which was a consequence not fairly to be looked
for. That evening he poured his troubles into the
bowl-like ear of a friend. “It was not I who sent
the girl wrong. No, she did not want me to
marry her. ... I am a regular stay-at-home, and
if I stayed out at nights it was only to please
her. (!) Thousands of fellows have done worse
than I have done ; but did anyone ever see such
bad luck? ” A startling column interview in the
Register was the outcome of this conversation —-
or so it is alleged ; and certainly some of the talk
doesn’t look as if it was meant for the public
eye.

STILL ANOTHER EGG JOKE.
“ Here , waiter , take this egg out and screw its neck.”
Barristers and their fees were discussed
last week in Sydney Supreme Court. Reid, K. 0.,
said he knew of a case in which a member of the
bar didn’t get paid, and went into long and
harrowing details. The Chief Justice said that
if the profession insisted upon getting its fee on
the delivery of the brief dreadful things like that
wouldn’t happen. Mr. Reid said he would make
a note of the valuable suggestion for future use.
Nothing was said about barristers who get fees
and never appear in court.
* * * *
Like Brother Lawson in N.S.W., Brother
Chelmsford has been involved in great bitterness
of spirit, owing to that portion of the Queensland
community which wears embroidered pinnies and
ham-frills round the neck. Briefly, a large num-
ber of Queensland Freemasons decided td'east off
the old-world trammels and form a Queensland
Lodge, and the idea caused much bickering
amongst the older fossils —the ammonites and
trilobites, and the paleozoic remains, and the
extinct arthropods—and great doings amongst the
Brethren. Then Chelmsford, a potentate of the
English section, cast in his lot with the
Queenslanders, and was made Grand Boss, and,
after that these “Brothers ” started to skirmish
like the cats of Kilkenny, and excommunicated
each other, and the English lot took to airing
their alleged grievances in the papers (orfe advt.
in the daily press occupied four columns), and
cables to and from the “ Mother Lodges” were
daily occurrences. At time of writing, the Bill
Goat is in a state of great excitement, but Chelms-
ford sticks to his gun, though he admits he didn’t
know it was loaded with quite so much bad egg
as there appears to be in it.
The snobbish S.M. Herald reprints this
from the fulsome, oily T. P. O’Connor’s
“M.A.P.”
, There is always something- pathetic in the sight of a lad
still in his teens succeeding to the family honors. This
fate has befallen the new Lord Congleton, a bright-faced
Etonian, who in no wise recalls the most famous member
of the house of which he is now chief—the late Charles
Stewart Parnell. Lord Congleton is just 16, he has two
brothers younger than himself, and a sister who is a year
older, and who will therefore be 01 e of the debutantes
in 1908.
And, on the same principle, it’s most amusing
when a lad still in his teens succeeds to the family
poverty, and it is quite hilarious when a bony
widow succeeds to the job of breadwinner for 13.
O’Connor isn’t so much to blame —he lives in the
atmosphere of snobbery; but the S.M. Herald
ought to know better.

“ S.” writes :
I witnessed a block in Little Bourke-street, Melbourne,
on a recent Monday, when the mercury was touching
104 degrees in the shade. The suspension of traffic was
occasioned by a small, shaggy man who had leaned up
against a pole on the shady side, and drifted into a
drowsy state, being full of beer and borne down with
weariness and atmospheric pressure. Presently a lorry
came along, and, as there was a procession of banana
vans occupying one-half of the narrow' roadway, it W'as
not possible to pass without driving over the sleeper’s
feet. The lorry driver yelled at the leaning citizen,
who presently opened his eyes and blinked a
bit, but did not move. A delivery van pressed
behind the lorry, and a spring cart came after. Both
drivers added their objurgations, Chinamen came out
and passed shrill Asiatic opinions, and a boy with a truck
tried to set his dog on the impediment against the post.
It was only when the lorry-driver came down to fight the
leaning citizen that the City Council was found to be the
real offender, the beery man against the post being
guilty only of contributory negligence in having gone to
sleep standing on a coat of the Council’s nice new tar.
The inebriate couldn’t shift to save himself, and the
drivers had to drag him out of his boots before traffic
could be resumed. Then they drove over the boots while
the suaggy man stood shoeless on the pavement.

From the domestic hints in the kind,
motherly S.M. Herald (27/2/’O7)
When you are choosing lobsters, be careful to select
those that have their tails curled up tightly to their
bodies, for then you will be sure they are fresh, and were
not cooked after death.
Kind old “ Mr. James,” who goes to church, is
anxious that we shall get the lobster that was
boiled alive. We will know it by its tail curled
up in agony.
THE JOYOUS COMET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A comet’s tail, over-charged with oxygen, would
send us all mad with liveliness and activity for a time.—
Vie. Government Astronomer Baracchi.
Do you hear the joyous news ?
We’ll be tight on airy' booze,
And Life will be a reckless honeymoon ;
We’ll be drunk, but not on ale,
When we strike that comet’s tail,
And we pray that dancing firework’s coming soon.
We’ll have a skinfull then
Of champagne oxygen,
Exhilarating as a Bacchic hymn ;
Nor heed our solemn teachers,
Sedate, sin-shifting preachers,
Nor prophets dour nor Worralls small and dim.
Oare quits us with a thud,
And, clad in beer-leaves, Jud
Will act the giddy goat and gay buffo n ;
All Pleasant Sunday moans
We’ll plant ’mid bones and stones,
Oh ! we pray that cheerful comet’s coming soon.
We’ll dance and sing and revel,
Casting Oare unto the devil.
We’ll forget our sleepy psalms and laugh at Fate.
This world’s colossal razzle
Begins. Lo ! See that dazzle ?
Hurrah ! the comet’s sprinting up the straight.
Can’t you see yon bucking star
(God’s beauty-bright cigar),
It’s whisking tail’s already past the moon ?
We’ll all go mad with laughter;
Who the h cares what comes after ?
And we pray that joyous comet’s coining soon,
vie.
LOUIS ESSON.
THIS ANIMAL BITES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A paternal councillor of Wodonga (Vic.) is endeavoring:
to induce the municipal authorities to hang;-posters on
the walls of the township, warning: young- men to have a
care when strange females seek to allure them. Apart
from the fact that the woman whose hair is artificial
gold and whose tresses are a snare wouldn’t make bread
and cheese in Wodonga, and would have to take out the
wages of sin in pumpkins, the suggestion only shows how
little some people have learned of human nature since
Adam’s time
When Eve appeared on Adam’s run,
She raised an everlasting fuss,
Although he had a hint to shun
The animal as dangerous.
It was the hint that lured him on—
At least, so much we understand—
And Eve, ere many hours had gone,
Had Adam feeding from her hand.
So ’tis to-day with man. The cuss
Is contrary. If, to deter,
You mark a woman dangerous,
He straight will run away—with her f
Vic.

THE NEW PATIENT.
THE HOSPITAL NURSE : “ You have got pyjamas, I suppose ?”
PATIENT : “ Maybe, ma'am, but ’twas hydatids the doctor called it.”
The additions to the Union Bank, Pitt-
street, Sydney, have necessitated the demolition
of some very old buildings in Hunter-street.
Away in the thirties they were occupied by
Flower, Salting and Co., merchants, with which
firm J. H. Challis, the Sydney University bene-
factor, was connected. The corner was a place
of interest, however, before the Flower-Salting
era. In March, 1814, Captain Richard Brooks,
who had previously made voyages to N.S. Wales,
arrived in his brig Spring and harnessed the vessel
to Circular Quay. He sold the Spring, and pur-
chased a house and land at the corner of Pitt and
Hunter streets, the allotment now covered by the
Union. Bank and the old buildings that are being
■demolished. The Captain opened a store at the
head of Cockle Bay (now Darling Harbor) with
the cargo which he brought in his brig. He
resided for 10 years on the corner. The Union
Bank originally started at the N.E. corner of
■George and King streets, Flower, Salting and Co.
being directors. It soon moved to the point
adjacent to the stores of the firm, and has
remained there ever since.

“Kaylee” : The authorities at Adelaide
have changed the name of the street in which the
late Mr. Habibulla of Afghanistan slew his wife.
Now it isn’t Bristol-street, and no doubt the
name was altered chiefly because Fat owns most of
the house property in the district. If all the
house-occupiers in Bristol-street had owned their
premises, the name might have remained ;* but,
in any case, nothing better could have happened
than a decision to wipe Bristol-street right off the
map. No one would live there for choice.
I know one young man who lives in Angas-street,
which backs onto Bristol-street, and lie told me
that he’d got his rent reduced one-half simply by
going to his landlord and complaining in a pained
voice about the character of the neighborhood.
* » * *
Hanging runs in families. There is (or
was) a family in Albert Park, Melbourne, which
demonstrated the fact beyond argument. In
10 years seven members of this family suspended
themselves, and if strangulation no longer runs
in the family, it is because there is no more
family left to asphyxiate.
* * * *
There is not going to be much rampage in the
Vic. elections. T. Bent will not fight “ on his own ” for a
progressive land policy, but is going to open his mouth
and shut his eyes, and take what the Council will give
him. Now, if the Council would only take an extra nip of
D.C.L. Whisky, which is the spirit of human kindness as
well as wisdom, every intending settler would soon be
fairly fixed with an allotment of land. D.C.L. in politics
supplies the touch of inspiration which makes progress
a joy and altruism a possibility.*
“Shellback,”' ©n a maritime matter :
South. Coast Co.'B new steamer, Moruya, met with a
little mishap en route from Liverpool to Sydney, by
stranding'on.the Victorian coast in the vicinity of Port
Albert. Information supplied to the dailies states that
a “ general chart of the world” was in use at the time.
Whilst professionally sympathising with the skipper
in his misfortune, I suggest that a general chart of
the world is scarcely a suitable one for close coasting
navigation. Captain. Lecky in his “ Wrinkles ” (and I
doubt if a more practical navigator ever stood on a ship’s
bridge) points out that only the largest scale
charts possible to obtain should be used in
coasting navigation. He further adds the word
“Admiralty,” and that’s another point. The naval
hydrographical office has done and still is doing
invaluable work for the sailor-man. Its charts are as
absolutely correct as it is possible for human agency to
make them. The chart to which is attributed the un-
fortunate error, causing the Moruya’s stranding, is
probably (of course, I cannot say for certain) one of
the variety known as “blue-backs.” These are, as a
rule, the great stand-by of the sailing-ship master, who,
by custom, is compelled to find himself in navigating
gear. These “blue-backs” are issued by private firms,
and so far as they go are very excellent documents, in
that they give the shipmaster a lot for his money. But
they lack the official stamp of accuracy. The cause of
the stranding was the change of color in Cliffy Island
light. (The newspapers got it Seal Island ; they really
meant Deal Island, in Hogan’s Group.) If I remember
rightly. Cliffy Island light was changed from a revolving
red to revolving white light soms 18 months ago, if not
earlier. Further, I remember reading the official notice,
advertised some nine months before that. Such official
notice would, in due course, reach the Admiralty chart
office, and be incorporated on the sheet to which it refers.
Now it is just possible that Private Enterprise missed
this information, as at times it must do, since all
official sources are not open to it. Hence such change
might pass unnoticed in even the latest “blue-back”
publications. The moral is, when taking any ship between
any two ports, use only Admiralty charts and those of
the latest date, in preference to any publication of Private
Enterprise. This advice sounds like Socialism, but it is a
good thing to keep off the rocks with, anyhow.
♦ * • *
Healesville has a shire council that is the
weirdest, cussedest, most divided shire council in
Victoria, and that is saying a good deal. It is
split into two sections and a secretary. One
section thinks the secretary does his work well;
the other thinks he does not. A casting vote
separates the two, and, as a result, the secretary
is always being either sacked or reinstated. Just
at present one-half of the council maintains that
the secretary is no longer the secretary, having
been dismissed and paid off early in February.
On the other hand, the secretary is still sitting
beside the president, who, with his casting vote
and the other section of the council, says the
secretary has not been sacked. The secretary
himself says that he has not been paid off or been
sacked, and offers, when he can make himself
heard above the tumult of the council chamber,
to produce counsel’s opinion to that effect. Any-
how, no shire business has been transacted for
ever so long. As soon as the council meets, the
sight of the secretary leads one section to cry out
opprobrium at the roof and turret of its voice,
and the other section yells in reply, and every-
body shouts at once till the meeting disperses.
Last meeting screamed at the uttermost pinnacle
of its voice for about four hours, and used every
epithet known in the gardens of Billingsgate.
* * • *
Melbourne is the most tarry city on
earth, and threatens to be blacked out if the
City Council is not put under restraint. The
other day a team of horses actually stuck in the
tar in Flinders Lane, and had to be thawed out.
There is some hope that one of these days a fat
alderman well get bogged in the tar, in which
case citizens who have lost many suits as a result
of the lavish outpouring will hasten to cover his
confusion with feathers.
A fire broke out in the R.C. church at
Maryborough (Vic.) the other Sunday, and in
their terror the choir girls, who were placed aloft,
started jumping from the gallery. Fortunately,
as the newspaper scribe baldly puts it, “ their fall
was broken by some active young male members
of the congregation.” How ? Present scribe once
offered to catch an eight-stone girl jumping off a
12ft. embankment, and was subsequently taken
home on two saplings. Now on the question of
the momentum of falling bodies he knows, and if
a plump damsel offers to throw herself at him
even off the level, he exercises the utmost pre-
caution. Yet none of the active young male
members of the Maryborough church are reported
as having been crumpled up. Either the gallery
was low, or the girls were mere skeletons, or the
R.C. young men thereabouts are as powerful as
gorillas.
* * * *
The trade of Pretender has fallen away
sadly of recent years, and the world to-day hasn’t
enough Pretenders to secure registration as a
union under the Arbitration Act. Still, one has
turned up in British Columbia. She is an
almond-eyed yellow lady of Manchu origin, is
married to an Englishman, and claims to be a
direct descendant of the third Duke of Chow, or
something of the sort. This gives her a better
right to the throne of Crockery than the Dowager
Empress. The lady, according to recent reports,
is trying to float herself into a limited liability
co. to secure the capital necessary to assert her
rights. Which recalls the fact that an Australian
pressman, who was in China during the Pekin
squabble of five or six: years ago, thought out a
great scheme for seizing the Chinese throne
and making himself Emperor over 300 million
people. Want of money to acquire the necessary
throne and reigning apparatus blighted the pro-
ject, and he still struggles with his old job.

Some notions :—
There’s many a slip ’twixt the Oup and the tip.
A rolling stone gathers no mildew.
Never throw down your cards—a scror g bluff often
beats a full hand.
Be kind to the fool. Without him you mightn’t get a
living.
Every mite looks upon his cheese as the world.
It is not necessary to use a muck-rate to cultivate the
mind.
Honesty is the best policy to impress on your friends.
The hen is mightier than her lord.
Men are gregarious animals; if you want to make
enemies try to get out of the ruck.
Patience sits upon monuments ; men lie upon them.
Luck is the thing that sticks to the other fellow.
Death reaps with the cookery book.
N UI.LA.
From a paper printed for the instruction
of English boys :
It is interesting to note that Australian mail carriers are
garbed in pale green uniforms with a red girdle about
the waist. The mounted carriers wear long green coats,
high riding boots, and strapped to the saddle is a brace
of pistols for protection, and a bugle with which to
inform the patrons of the carrier’s arrival. The mounted
carriersare usually accompanied by several fierce-looking
dogs, as a still further means of protecting the postmen
and the mails they carry.
But it would have been still more interesting if
little John Bull-pup had been told about the
Australian telegraph messengers—how, dressed
in alligator’s feathers, they fly through the
country mounted on specially-trained turtles, and
often deliver a message within a week. Perhaps,
however, the most interesting thing of all about
the Australian Postal Department is the school
where they train kangaroos for the parcel-post
delivery. For many years the pouch of the kan-
garoo was one of the great wasted natural resources
';of the country, until at length the Governor-
General offered a prize of two dozen wombat
skins for the best suggestion to utilise this curious
provision of kangaroo nature. The prize was
awarded to a bright boy from Manly, who sug-
gested that kangaroos should be trained to serve
as parcel-post carriers. The suggestion was in-
stantly adopted, and the Little Boy from Manly
thus became so famous that his picture is printed
nearly every week in the newspapers.

Agricultural Show committees are wearing
a worried look over the provisions of the N.S.W.
Gaming Act. With trotting events, where Style
and other things are taken into consideration" as
well as speed, it is thought taat the Act won’t
interfere ; but the purely record-breaking events
have been pronounced illegal. At Bathurst they
have a £lOO prize always on the programme
for the breaker of the Australian record—
Almont’s 2.121-5 at Ascot (Vic.) in 1903—but
at present it looks doubtful whether the event
will be risked. Parliament, however, is noto-
riously kind to the producer of the prize pig and
the bloated pumpkin, and already several politi-
cians, who barracked hard for the Gaming Act,
have been explaining that they didn’t know it
was loaded ; so, presumably, the local trotters
will raise the dust as usual.

OUR GALLANT DEFENDERS.
An Australian Barrack-Room Ballad.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Six naval and military bands took part in the display.
About 180 performers played and marched together with
admirable precision. The performance was held in the
oval, and was witnessed by thousands of spectators.
After the band performances there was a brilliant dis-
play of fireworks in the arena.— News Item.
“In time of peace prepare for war,” my father used
to say—
He’d writ it in his copy-book in England, far away ;
And Dad would often skite to us, when he’d had several
beers,
About the way he’d fought for us when in the Volun-
teers.
And we listened enthralled,
And Fame to us called ;
We were fired by his patriot story !
And we said that same day
We would follow the way
That had led him to milit’ry glory !
When Bill grew up, his martial soul no longer could with
stand
The call (the papers called it) to defend his native land.
He had an eye for elegance, had Bill, and so he chose
The band that wore the strikingest and beautifullest
clo’es.
On the ophicleid, Bill
Keeps Australia safe still.
And he plays like a Triton or Merman,
In that glorious band
They blow hard for their land,
And despise both the Jap and the German.
At eighteen years of age I felt that wild, resistless thrill
To rally round the bandmaster—the same as brother Bill.
I knew my country needed me. I felt contempt for
Death.
I said, “ Upon her altar I will lay my last, long breath 1 ”
Ah ! how I was lauded !
How mother applauded!
How all the girls crowded to kiss me !
And now with the cornet
I face Death—and scorn it,
Though I know that 1113' country would miss me.
When rumors of a trouble coming somewhere from the
North
Excite dismay, we bandsmen brave march jubilantly
forth.
So well we play and look so gay when tramping down the
street
That folk take heart again and say, “ By God ! they can’t
be beat! ”
Why bother with rifles,
And other such trifles ?
Why worry for warships or navies ?
Australia’s main stay
Are the men who can play,
And fellows who know what a stave is !
Vic.
O. O. CABOT.

COLUMN
Howard Smith and Go., Ltd., an inter-State
ship-owning co. which began six years ago with a good-
will item of £40,000: —
Dividends Depreciation
(5 p.c. pref., Written
Dec., Profit. 10 p.c. ord.). Off. Deficit.
1901 .. £57,734 £18,782 £20,668 £21,716
1902 .. 50,435 28,864 19,626 19,771
1903 .. 51,443 29,133 19,536 16,997
1904 .. 52,418 29,721 20,466 14,766
Reserves.
ions / 57,348 30,334 22,436 \ 1(!QQ »
w \ Profit on wharf resumption £27,181 / ’
1906 .. 67,488 30,792 25,728 17,961
That’s how it looks for the year, following the profit and
loss account. But during the last couple of years the
form of the balance-sheet has been somewhat altered
(apparently there was an idea that the public might go
into the co. a bit more freely if it knew more about it),
and in 1905 for the first time the liabilities were divided
up, and this item appeared—“ Fund for depreciation of
steamers and plant, replacements, renewals, insurance,
and contingencies, £219,731.” This year the item has
grown to £275,419, an increase of £55,688 ; so that the
£25,728 shown above, and provided for depreciation in
profit and loss account, amounts apparently to less than
half the actual provision made. What may be called the
cash position can be followed like this: —After paying
£30,663 during 1906 the assets increased by £131,416,
made up as follows: —Capital, £2663 ; increased debts
and overdraft, £71,969: profit (including provision for
depreciation and insurance reserve), £56,784. So (add-
ing the dividends paid during the year on to the
last item) the revenue in excess of working ex-
penses comes out at £87,447 for the year. What is a
fair thing for depreciation everybody has the right to
say for himself. Steamers and plant are in at £534,211
(against £413,457 in 1905); 5 per cent, a year would
call for £26,710, and, as shown above, there was
£56,784 available for this and insurance, &c. Assets also
include “ shares in other cos., £142,265,” against £64,039
in 1905. In the latter year the cos. in which the stocks
were held were at least named—coal and wharf cos.
mostly—but this year the £142,265 is represented by the
bald, unadorned line quoted above. So a sixth of all the
assets are a pig in a bag. The total paid capital is now
£410,894, of which £202,823 is 5 per cent, cumulative pre-
ference ; and a cumulative preference dividend that runs
to £10,145, and might run to more if all the pref. capital
were called up, is something for the others to think over,
even though at present the profits are so much in excess
of dividend requirements.

£50,000 (one-fourth) of capital returned.

This co., whose rights extend for only 28 years, 16i of
which have expired, has for some time been building up
a reserve, nominally for contingencies, really for the
return of capital; and a total of £53,500 having been
reached in June last, it was then decided to repay ss. per
share (£50,000). This payment was made in October,
after the last of the half-year’s dividends was declared,
so that up to the present the 10 per cent, dividend rate
is unaltered. There now remain of more or less liquid
assets that will not have to be handed over in 11J years
£55,505 in excess of liabilities—say £50,000, allowing for
certain assets that are not very liquid. The capital is
now £150,000. This means tbat£loo,ooo must be earned
in 11£ years plus interest. To wipe out that debt a sink-
ing fund of £10,500 a year, invested at 4 per cent., must
be provided. Six per cent, interest on the principal calls
for £9OOO a year ; total interest and sinking fund £19,500,
or £9750 a half-year. To get 8 per cent, and sinking fund
a profit of £11,250 per half-year would be needed. Look-
ing at the history of the co., that seems easy. Certainly
£50,000 worth of securities having now been sold, the
revenue will, for a time, shrink a little. To make up for
that, however, the co. is now collecting the maximum ss.
per 1000 gallons for water used by the mines, the mines
having lately got a Bill through, authorising them and
the Council to form a Trust to bring in a supply of their
own. This independent mine supply will seriously affect
this co.’s revenue. It will probably cut it down nearly
one-half, for the mines have taken three-fourths of the
entire consumption. Means may no doubt be adopted to
encourage Broken Hill to use more water ; but if it does,
it will want it for a good deal less money. Meantime,
while it has the supply in its own hands, the co. will put
a bit up its sleeve to provide for the knocks that are
always liable to fall on a mining town.

The Carlton Brewery, Ltd. (Melbourne)

1900 .. 15,533 Nil 15,533 371,048
1901 .. 26,712 Nil 26,712 344,336
1902 .. 21,443 Nil 21,443 322,893
1903 .. 11,024 3 p.c. = £837 10,187 312,706
(£4OOO added to goodwill.)
1904 .. 17,788 3 p.c.= 956 16,832 299,874
1905 .. 4,911 3 „ „ 1183 3,728 296,146
1906 .. 7,612 3 „ „ 1183 6,429 289,717
The net profit for the year was £10,724 ; but under the
Victorian Licensing Act of 1906 a fund has to be created
to compensate owners and licensees of hotels closed and
to be closed under the Act. This fund is raised by charg-
ing the licensee 3 per cent, on the dutiable liquor
which he purchases; and two-thirds of the total
the licensee deducts from the rent. This co. therefore,
for this year, puts aside £3112 as its contribution, as
owner, to the fund. Further, the directors sav—“ The
tax prejudicially affects the value of hotel freeholds, and
provision has to be made towards this depreciation. The
directors have decided to set aside £3680 for this pur-
pose.” No doubt it is the brewing co.’s correct policy’ to
squeal; and this is an effective way 7 of doing it. The
fact, however, is that the pub that is shut up will be
compensated in money, and the pub that isn’t shut
up will get a return for the money it contributes
to the compensation fund by the extra trade it
will do. If six pubs out of 12 are closed, the six
that are left open will certainly be worth more.
That’s why they have to contribute to the fund!
But this co. not only charges against profits the
amount of the contribution to the fund (which is reason-
able enough—the only other way would be to write up
hotel properties), but it writes down hotel properties.
Of course, in this case it really doesn’t matter two
straws. Every penny that the co. earns after paying
dividend is needed to write down inflated values ; and at
the present rate it can go on for a generation pouring
surplus profits into the hole without filling it up. The
co. can make a song about providing £3680 for deprecia-
tion if it likes ; but it would have to provide just as much
if it didn’t make any song. With a shareholders’
capital written down to £40,287, goodwill still stands
in the books at £154,000 ; and, apart from goodwill,
there is a deficit of £135,711. If, as the directors assert,
the Licensing Act has further depreciated values, then
there is a still bigger deficit; and the 3 per cent, divi-
dend on capital that is more utterly lost than it was be-
fore, is more superfluous than ever. The present debts
amount to £743,399, including £544,631 of mortgage de-
bentures, which are being reduced by the amount of
half the profits annually. An item, which may possibly
be a reserve, appears in the balance-sheet, “ Freehold
property realisation account, £1376.” Possibly this
ought to be deducted from the deficit shown above ; but
its real nature is not yet apparent. It may be a profit
made over book values on the sale of a property affected
by the Licensing Act of 1906—that Act which the direct-
ors say knocks out hotel values. But that can’t be said
for certain.
