The share capital, which stood at £113,525 in 1895, and
at £60,065 in 1900, has been reduced now to £43,800.
The deposits are, however, steadily mounting up ; down
to £4344 in 1900, they have now reached £25,429,
an increase of £14,155 in the past 12 months; but
an overdraft of £5153 has been wiped out.
Except for the society’s building (£7500) and
£2529 in cash, all the capital, reserves, and deposits are
represented by mortgages (£71,354). The latter have
increased by £BO6B in the year. On the mean of the
advances, interest earnings were at the rate of £7 11s. 9d.
per cent., and expenses (apart from interest) were £2 2s.
Bd. per cent. ; leaving £5 9s. Id. per cent, for interest,
profit, and contingencies. The reserves are now suffi-
cient to protect deposits and capital against a slump in
advances and property equal to 2s. Bd. in the £, while
capital and reserves combined protect the depositor
against a slump of about 12s. in the £ ; so the depositor’s
position is comfortable enough. The reserves to protect
capital are, however, none too substantial in view of the
recent rapid growth of advances, which mean risks. A
very great deal depends, of course, upon the margin
required between values and advances in the first place ;
and, as to that, all that can be said is that an average
interest return of £7 11s. 9d. per cent, doesn’t suggest
many “stiff” securities, while the co.’s record for the
past 15 years at least has been very satisfactory.

Five years ago it was announced that the heavy debt was
to be steadily reduced, and the firstcolumn above shows,
among other things, how the matter stood after capital
w 7 as written off and the freeholds written down to

on the management ; and a miscalculation to the extent
of thousands isn’t impossible. A statement showing the
depreciation allowed for would be some help, perhaps ;
certainly, in the absence of it, the reserves in such a con-
cern can’t be relied upon absolutely. And, of course, if the
reserves are open to some doubt, so are the profits as set
out above.

The rate of dividend is stated approximately. For some
years past the share capital has been dwindling, and the
deposits have lately been again increasing. The following

1900 1904 1905 1906
Share capital .. £20,871 £20,568 £17,323 £12,041
Deposits .. .. 47,643 17,708 15,354 20,563

Like most Tasmanian institutions, this society is suffer-
ing from dry rot; its mortgages have shrunk' by nearly
one-half in six years. Under present political conditions
it seems that Tasmania has less need for a building than
a burying society. Emigration from the island keeps
steadily in excess of immigration. As the young men
grow up they quit, and some other State gets the build-
ing of their homes. Between 1892 and 1901 it lost
5091 more people than it received, and in the four
years to the end of 1905 it lost another 6145.
This society, in the face of the shrinkage of share capital,
tries to make itself believe that it has the confidence
of the public. No doubt it has, so far as the Tasmanian
public can have confidence in anything Tasmanian ; but
observe that, instead of fixing £5OOO or so into shares,
the public has taken that amount out of shares and put
it on deposit. The deposit is, comparatively, a temporary
affair ; moreover, it has the security of the shareholders’
capital behind it. It thus looks as though the Tasma-
nian public isn’t satisfied now with the security of a
depreciating Tasmanian mortgage alone. In 1905 the
society had to write off £2236 from its foreclosed secu-
rities : and that was significant. It reduced the reserves
lower than they had been for years ; in other words, it
proved that for some years past, after allowing for depre-
ciation, the dividend had not been earned, and on pre-
sent appearances it looks as though it will have to be
reduced. And that will be merely 7 another pa tof the
price Tasmania must pay for its political misdeeds and
for its political apathy, which is almost worse than
misdeeds.

This co., consisting of men with meat to sell, has an
agent at every fat stock sale in Sydney, and his business
is to see that the other buyers don’t get any bargains.
The other buyers have to bid above him, and what he bids
he doesn’t in most cases have to pay 7, because of the special
rebate allowed on his purchases; as shown above, last
half-year’s rebate, or bonus, ran to £5663. The co, pays
no dividends ; shareholders get their profit through, in
this way, compelling the public to pay more for their
meat than they would often otherwise do. Occasionally
the public seems inclined to kick ; and the people who
run other meat works, and who have to crowd through
without any subsidy, also, at odd times, pass remarks.
But the co. has been going on much in the same way for
30 years, and it has got case-hardened now. Naturally 7,
as the supply of stock has increased, it has had to pur-
chase more heavily in order to keep the market up to
what the stock-owners reckon a proper level; so that,
whereas in the latter half of 1904 it bought only £48,496
of live and slaughtered meat, its purchases in the past
six months reached £113,883.

This mournful co., which was born in the perilous days
of 1887* and hadn’t cut its wisdom teeth when the boom
took it on the rocks, caught its last fleeting glimpse of a
dividend in the middle of 1892. By the end of that year it
found itself with a paid capital of £25,000 (of which
£14,528 was already regarded as lost), and total liabilities
of £152,651. The unfortunate shareholders have been
well drained since those days ; the paid capital is now
£151,317, less £22,024 of unpaid calls (“ of which about
£21,500 is bad”). The total liabilities are £19,015.
Against that are advances, properties, &c., which last
year brought in £1169 gross, worth about £26,000 if
capitalised at 4J per cent. But they may be worth much
more ;it depends upon the kind of assets they are. An
empty suburban block, worth £lOOO, doesn’t bring in
decimal 07 per cent. On the other hand, the liabilities
have been reduced during tlie year by nearly £3OOO
“ from the proceeds of realisations,” and no loss on
realisations is shown ; so there are assets, it seems, now
written down to market value. Still it is about time
there w 7 as a cleaning up. On a re-valuation shareholders
w r ould know for certain if there was a penny to come
back to them; if not, then depositors and mortgagees
might just as well face the situation and get the un-
pleasant corpse out of the way. It is such a small corpse,
anyhow, to cost over £BOO a year to keep above ground.
TheKootscray Gas Co. (Vic.), which is this
month offering present shareholders a new issue of shares
in the proportion of one to five now held :

For the past half-year a 7 per cent, dividend has been
declared. The reserves consist of—Meter renewal fund,
£lOOO ; wear and tear reserve, £647 ; reserve for repay-
ment of deposits, £4OO ; depreciation, £6458; and
undivided profit, £lOl6. The amounts shown above as
written off are probably amounts spent on repairs and
renewals ; there is no charge otherwise on that account.
That, being so, the depreciation fund is not the best
sort of a reserve, if it is a reserve at all; for a patched
plant isn’t as good as new, so the depreciation is
in whole or part a set-off against actual and not future
depreciation. Reserve fund (just created) for the repay-
ment of deposits is a new idea, and not a bad one. The
deposits amount to only £l5OO ; but they and all the
reserves, as well as the capital, are sunk in plant, &c., and
the best way to repay them is out of revenue, and then
write down assets to correspond. But if this reserve
goes into plant like the rest (and it has, so far), how is it
going to pay off the deposits—unless the depositors take
it out in gas-pipe? Which isn’t likely. On the whole,
seeing that all the works, buildings, &c., are still in the
books at cost (£31,624), it looks as though the real profits
have been fully disbursed, and that the amount put to
reserves no more than covers depreciation, including
meter depreciation, for nothing has been written off
meter renewal reserve for some years.

Of the reserves (and the profits) £357 represents id. per
lb. of the butter made for local sale during the last three
months and held as a guarantee fund against loss on the
butter exported, and another £l4 has dropped from
somewhere into the same fund. The more of the butter
that is exported the better for the man who makes for
the local market; so the local supplier has to pay id. per
lb. to guarantee the exporter against loss. There is
another way of looking at it: the local consumer pays an
extra id. per lb. to guaran tee the success of a scheme whose
result is to make his butter dear. Still, until Australia
can eat all its own butter (and it won’t be able to do that
until it has a much bigger manufacturing population),
it will have to export some; and this scheme seems a
very fair and sensible one for the butter-makers to fix up
among; themselves. Meantime, of course, until account
sales are received, this special reserve is not of a fixed
character. The depreciation allowed for and shown in
the table is at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum on
■buildings, plant and machinery.

* * » „
Boulder No. 1, Kalgoorlie, is still more or less
under a cloud, although tributes continue to get very
good returns from the 200 ft. level, and manage to keep a
5-head mill going. The lode they are on may turn out
to be a branch of the Perseverance, or Morty, lode, which
is being worked in the South Kalgurli and Hainault.
Returns run from 30dwt. to 40dwt. per ton, including
gold won from tailings and concentrates ; but the lode
is much further east than anything being worked fron
the Hamilton shaft in the Great Boulder.
* * * *
Boulder Deep Levels, Kalgoorlie, which has
been hung up for some months, is to be re-sampled on
behalf of some of the biggest holders. The outlook is not
very hopeful, but still there is a chance. All one can
say is, that the 1100 ft. level showed the best results,
though it never justified the wild gamble that the sensa-
tional assays inaugurated.
* * * «
Cumberland, Norseman (W. A.), has been again
in the public eye. For years the co. was a disastrous
failure ; but in 1905 it paid three divs. of 6d. each, or
£6OOO, and repeated the dose in 1906. On August 81,19C6,
the co. had a cash reserve of £4200, and since then has
made profits of £3400, and paid divs. of £2OOO. It has,
therefore —or ought to have —somewhere about £5600 in
hand. In addition, on September 30, it had ore reserves
estimated at 10,200 tons, worth 85s. per ton ; and, apart
from fresh developments, it should have fully 6500 tons
in sight, or nine months’ supply. In view of the fact that
the Coolgardie-Norseman railway is to be started within
a month, the possibilities of the Cumberland should not
be lost sight of.
* * * *
Mararoa, Norseman (W.A.), has started its 10-
head. mill, and Manager Henderson is confident that
revenue will meet expenditure. The opening up of the
350 ft. level is awaited with expectancy if not with
anxiety, as, should it prove equal to the two levels above,
the future of the co. is assured. Each 100 ft. is estimated
to give 20,000 tons of ore, and if this maintains its value
of 60s. per ton, the position of the co. is all right.

Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), does not
improve its position. On October 15 ore reserves were
estimated at 13,900 tons, worth 635. 9d., and as at
January 31 they are given as 9790 tons only, of the same
value. In the interval 3000 tons have been treated, so
that something is wrong somewhere. The position of the
mine is very precarious, as the crosscourse, which cut off
the reef to the south, has flattened out so much going
north that it is probable it will cut the reef off altogether.
» * * *
Great Ophir, Davyliurst (W.A.), has had to
pause in the erection of its plant for want of funds. This
is a pity, as the prospects of the property were hopeful;
but, unfortunately, the promoters of the show, Mandel-
stam, Trott and Rodway, “ bit off more than they could
chew ” when they floated the North End, Rothschild and
Ophir, all of which are at a standstill. The Ophir is esti-
mated to have 40,000 tons of 365. ore in sight, and looks
good enough, but has been hampered from the start by
weak finance.
■» * * *
Augusta, Laverton (W. A.), once run by Menzies’
Golden Rhine, is now held in 14 shares, some of which
have recently changed hands at a shade over £2OOO.
Between 1897 and 1903 the co. treated 15,500 tons for
11,0000 z., and then, on a report by “Boulder” Hamilton,
which probably didn’t justify that course, the property
was abandoned. It was acquired by a local syndicate,
which soon opened up a good supply of high grade ore,
and began to coin money. Manager Bower reckons that
there are 18,700 tons of ore in sight, worth 955. per ton, and
enthusiasts say that it is one of the best mines outback.
Anyhow, it is in a pretty strong position.
» * * *
Whitford’s Reward (W.A.) has a fairly good
chance of coming out on top in its new venture, the
Callion, near Davyhurst. This mine was long worked by
a Bull co., but was abandoned as exhausted on the peter-
ing out of the first lens of ore. Subsequent development
suggests the existence of a series of long lenses of
various depths, the beginning of each lens lying on the
top of its neighbor’s tail. The new main shaft, destined
to go to 200 ft., is down over 100 ft., and should reach the
required depth next month. The new lens now being
opened up from No. 3 shaft maintains its width and
value of about 60s. per ton, and prospects are pretty
hopeful.
* • • «
Gwalia Consols, Wiluna (W.A.), looks like
making a name for itself. It has so far been equipped
with a 10-head mill, and up to date has treated 56,000
tons for £60,000. So far there has not been much profit,
as work has been on too limited a scale ; but an extra 20-
head is now going in, and when it starts to hammeritis
expected that 6000 tons will be treated monthly, at a
profit of at least £15,000. There is a big lode on the
leases ; it is estimated that there are 117,000 tons of 30s.
per ton ore developed above the 100 ft. level. According
to all tests, t his is one of the most striking properties in
the West, and, if properly handled, will be easily in the
front rank. The management hitherto h .sn’t helped it.
• IT * *
Phillips River (W.A.) has made its first ship-
ment of 25 tons of copper matte, valued at £l3OO,
or about £52 per ton. This co. is controlled by G. 0.
Klug, formerly of the Fremantle Smelter and Boulder
Perseverance, one of the best metallurgists of the -State.
Regular shipments of copper may be looked for in the
future from the group of mines under Klug’s super-
vision, and the current high price should leave a very
good margin.
* * * *
Oroya Black Range (W.A.) is worth watching.
The contract for erecting a 20-head mill and 8-drill
compressor has been let, and the mine should come on
the list of producers in the next three months or so. Ore
reserves are estimated at fully 40,000 tons, worth 70s. a
ton, on which there should be a fair margin of profit. The
fly in the ointment is the life of the mine. The Sand-
stone Development Co. reckons that, it has the com-
paratively shallow “ deep levels,” and if this prove to be
the case, the latter is the best holding. Shafts are now
being sunk to prove this, and a very 7 short time should
settle the question. r
Concerning the West Kimberley mineral coun-
try, mentioned in B. 28/2/’O6, G. J. Poulton, F.R.G.S.,
writes:—On April 26, 1906, under agreement with the
Narlarla Hills Co., of Adelaide, I visited the West Kim-
berley deposits, originally discovered by me in 1901. I
was accompanied this trip by J. H. Grant, the co. s
representative, whose instructions were to test samples
on the ground. Mr. Grant sent in a very favorable
report, with recommendations, which, hoyvever, were
not followed by the directorate. On May 2 and 3
showed Grant the copper-bearing country at Mon-
dooma, where he pegged off two leases for our co.
I parted from him at the Robinson River on May 3,


The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by VV. and J. Lampriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert and Co., of London, the largest firm in the
world for the manufacture of transport appliances.*

The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “ pops ” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“ hitches ” for timber, and squaring up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“ Shaw ” Hand Rock Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has onlv one working part—the hammer—which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. In some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2Jin. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Shaw
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine may be seen working.* [<-']

and proceeded north on a flying trip into uncharted
country. On Mnj f 13 I discovered a fissure lode
about 40 miles from Mondooma and 70 miles from
Narlarla, and much nearer the coast than either place.
This quartz lode proved to be about 1£ mile long, and I
found two shoots of copper. I took samples into Derby;
some of the specimens assayed 60 per cent, copper. I did
not, however, mark off the ground, wishing first to ascer-
tain by work more of its value. Grant and I visited the
place shortly afterwards, and he at once pegged two
leases for the Narlarla Co. On June 30 I discovered,
about two miles from the last-named place, an immense
copper lode, traceable for miles. We here marked off
live leases, and the ground is now held by the
Obagooma Syndicate of Adelaide. This mine, judging
from surface indications, has big possibilities.
Of course I am aware of the many adverse reports pub-
lished respecting these properties, and the West Kim-
berley mineral belt generally ; but I care nothing for
“expert” opinion given on surface undeveloped shows.
I rely upon the miner’s pick. Some bad errors have been
published in Perth over the name of Mr. Woodward,
Government Assistant Geologist. He says the Narlarla
deposits were opened and worked by R. B. Pettigrew years
back, but were condemned and shut down. I contradict
that absolutely, Pettigrew' was never in the district,
and consequently never saw the mine ; moreover, no
person at all had ever put a pick in the ground
Grant and 1 did so on April 20th last. I know of other
large deposits of mineral that, on being tested, may prove
to be as valuable as those belonging to the Obagooma and
Kimberley cos.

Some private shows on the Murchison (W.A.)
are turning out well in a little way. At Mindoolah,
50 miles N. W. of Cue, Bertram and party have crushed
153 tons for 5530 z., with another lOOoz. expected from
the sands. But the stone had to be carted all the way to
Cue ; so it is only the rich stone that gets to the mill.
The property is under option to a co. Reef is of the
string-of-sausages variety, pinching in from sft. to sin.
At Tuckanara, Judy’s Gift crushed 71 tons for 3600 z.;
and from 173 tons the Union Jack got nearly 900 oz.
From another show in the same locality a small party
of working miners split up 3550 z. from 120 tons. The
Golconda, at the Island, 20 miles the other side of Cue,
was abandoned. by a Bull co., which spent something
like £70,000 on it. Now a working party has dollied
84oz. from a ton. It is still the same old specimen
show, no good to a big co. Island Queen, in the same
locality, has given 2520 z. from 45 tons, and 86 tons
from the Day Dawn returned 200 oz.

The Poseidon (Vic.) rush from the inside :
“North and south runs a low ridge straggled over by
stunted box-trees. It is the Woolshed reef. About six
or eight chains west, a chock-and-log fence marks the
boundary of Wemys’s paddock. A panel pulled out of the
fence forms the ‘ Golden Gate,’ and the shaft alongside is
where John Porter started last Cup day to sink what for
all he knew' was to be another in a long series of duffers.
Away down the paddock the Poseidon lead or gutter, no-
w'here more than 20ft. wide, has been slowly traced for
about half a mile. A few' claims below the fence are
good, then they are fair to poor, but the claim on the
lower fence has given good returns. The gutter seems
to freshen up here. Below this, Law' blocks work. There
is about this part of Poseidon lead nothing more in-
spiriting than a lot of mullock heaps in a scrubby pad-
dock. It is the head of Poseidon that is remarkable —the
part where the township has sprung up. Just inside the
‘ Golden Gate ’ is a hessian shanty, where a compact little
man, known to some N.S.W. miners in connection with
the Frenchman’s reef, dispenses fruit, soft drinks, and
The Bulletin. The Frenchman and his son are musical,
and a platform has been erected hard by, whereon free
al fresco concerts are given vvhat nights the boxing
‘saloon’ does not offer greater attractions. The visitor
will probably look in vain for the saloon, w'hich is merely
a circle of poles standing in the ground, to be clothed on
exhibition nights in a hessian gown. Near by is the
Poseidon Cafe, where the big ’bus twice daily deposits
its load of pilgrims. The ’bus, however, is not sent
empty away, for, besides the mere sig’htseers, there is
already' a steady stream of those who, recognising the
hopelessness of searching over a mile or so of country for
the incomprehensible slug, prudently take a bob’s worth
of ride whilst they yet have the bob. Many others, less
prudent or more sanguine, spend their last coin and walk
away.
“From Jackson’s claim to the prospector’s
shaft (20ft. deep) it is roughly estimated that £20,000
worth of gold has been taken from five claims ; and,
although the gutter is narrow, there is still a lot of
ground to work. Porter, the prospector, is blocked each
end by injunctions obtained by alleged ‘ miners,’ pend-
ing an appeal from the warden’s decision. And across
this golden gutter the pilgrims troop, with scarcely a
glance at the shafts from which small fortunes have
been hauled.
“What came they out for to see? A flat, the
soil and clay of which are being- broken, and the fine mul-
lock heaped up as by a thousand colonies of giant ants ?
As far as eye can see, figures, pick in hand, are bobbing-
up and down like marionettes, and of the hun-
dreds of men on that flat not 5 per cent, get
a pennyweight of gold in a month. Poseidon
is a wonderful and an incomprehensible field.
From 6in. to I2in. of harsh loam, one to two
feet of reddish-yellow clay, and then bedrock. Limestone
it is called locally, though probably it is, geologically, a
slate. And not a pannikin of gravel in a tub of dirt.
Even where good slugs, 101 b. or 121 b. weight, containing
much quartz and ironstone, have been found, there has not
been a stone within yards. Up on the hillside, where the
900 oz. piece was found, it is stonier and shallower ; but
there is no “ run,” no line, no indication. It is a pure
gamble.
“It was a matter of comment that of all the
nuggets got not one had been ‘christened.’ Well, one
Saturday morning, one of the outsiders, chains away
from the golden claims, pulled out 111 b. w'eight of nearly
pure gold. He was pretty well ‘ stumped,’ and that
morning a pound or two—perhaps a shilling or two—
would have bought his interest. Within an hour of
getting the speck, a barrel of beer was set up on the con-
cert platform, and served out to all hands. On the fol-
lowing Tuesday, his mate picked out another piece about
the same weight. Was it luck, or is Poseidon a friend of
Bacchus ? ”
* * ■* *
Adelaide investors have been chasing a will-o’-
the-wisp in the Tyson’s mine, Bendigo. They floated a co.
to re-open and unwater a neglected shaft on the strength
of a yarn that, when sinkingin the old days, a night shift
picked out £79 worth of specimens and quickly timbered
up the hole and went on with the sinking. For years
the mine was neglected, till an ancient male with a long
memory persuaded the Adelaide people to take up the
abandoned lease and put up money for pumping
machinery. The water was easily lowered below the
point where the reef was said to be, and the slabs were
removed. The reef was there, but it was quite barren !
Not a color was met with, and the stripping of more
shaft has not afforded the sight of gold. So much for
the old miner’s imaginary bonanza. The Adelaide share-
holders will, however, be foolish to give up the quest for
some sort of a reef after having done so much prepara-
tory work.

In the North Woah Hawp, Ballarat, Harley
and 00. got into one of those rare patches that stick in
the minds of tributors and make it possible for Ballarat
cos. to get a third of their development work done on a
percentage basis that generally works out at about half
wages. On one day (Feb. 25) the North Hawp party got
out 500 oz. of specimens, and for some months past £2O a
man per week hasn’t been unusual.
* * * *
North Nelson, St. Arnaud (Vic.), is plugging
down to catch the Lord Nelson shoot, and has just got in
further capital by raising the 36,000 shares from £1 10s.
to £2. W. G. Williams, lately manager of the Lord
Nelson, has been empowered by the North Nelson co. to
prepare plans, &c.,ofthe Lord Nelson workings for the
information of the North. It is understood he favors
sinking the North shaft to 2100 ft. before crosscutting,
instead of 2000 ft., as originally intended. The chance of
the North getting the Nelson shoot is better than
middling.
* * * *
* ‘Victor’s Quartz is the hope of Woods Point
(Vic.), and shares, which once were as flat as tied-house
beer, now have a sparkle. This all arises from the work
of the tributor. The co. was in extremis, when the useful
tributor determined to try his luck. And his luck proved
to he in, for he alighted on a fine shoot of stone, out of
which he has taken excellent crushings. Grumblers cavil
at the tribute, but the co. was in a sore plight when
it let the odd man in. Also, it has collected a fairly stiff
royalty all the while. Local people thought the shoot of
stone pitched south into the South Victor lease, where
J. B. Mackenzie and others sank a lot of coin. Local
opinion was probably wrong ; but the deepening of the
main shaft will clear up the point. Not long since Victors
were at 505., and if they hit good stone as the result of
the new sink there maybe another spurt. But Woods
Point is a tricky locality, regarding which the Stock
Exchange maxim is to take a profit, and let the others do
the mining.
• * * *
Berry Consols Extended, Creswick (Vic.), has
to date eaten up £78,000 in calls and £400,000 worth of
gold to pay £70,000 in divs. Spring Hill Leads has swal-
lowed about £120,000 of contributed money and all the
gold won, and has never paid a div. Berry Extended
looks like being worked out save for its 2000 ft. northern
ground, parallel to the Leads boundary, and the Leads
has nothing left save a block of the gutter still farther
on, and towa ds the Berry United boundary. Neither
show has the least chance of getting any money back, if
they work as at present. But there is a chance of a
few divs. if the two amalgamate. Spring Hill
Leads is top-dog just now, because the portion of the
gutter that the Berry Extended owns cannot be worked
at a profit, save from the Leads shaft. It pays that co.,
therefore, to sit down and wait. But it would not pay it
to do so for an hour if the Berry Extended shareholders
decided to go into liquidation, as the pumping would hit
the Leads’ purse in a weak spot. The Berry Extended
ought to make up its mind at once to amalgamation or
to be absorbed. There is no sense in continuing calls
when there is nothing ahead. If the amalgamation card
doesn’t turn out trumps, the thing is liquidation, to
bring an end to a mine that has never been much more
than a costly duffer.
* * * *
When word came recently that “highly-pay-
able coarse gold” had been struck somewhere in the
Mikado mine, Daylesford (Vic.), everyone agreed that
the existence of the show was justified. What else
could be thought even by the most inveterate
“ specker ” ? So the stock was given a rest, whereupon
from the legal manageress downward there was great re-
joicing. But just as Katisha found that something more
than a dimple on the elbow was needed to lure coy man-
kind, so the directors of the Mikado now realise that
something more substantial than a phrase is required
once the battery gets to work. Skimming through the
Mikado reports since the New Year, it can be seen that
the mine, like its namesake, the little brown man, has
been a rare deceiver. The first week of January brought
a report of “payable gold” ; and then there were 3ft. of
stone, “highly mineralised,” showing “gold freely.” Thus
on they read until the crowning message given above.
The battery telltale now shows an average of only sJdwt.
to the ton, exclusive of the mineral. Therefore the
manageress writes that as the yield was so “inconsis-
tent ” with the good quality of the stone reported, she
has called the directors together to investigate. So
“ the sex ” finds that mines are deceivers, too.
* * * *
A. B. P. B. Bayne, of Kalgoorlie, is to help the
new Bethanga mine, Bethanga (Vic.), to its third advent.
Bayne’s report deals only with the Gift and the Martin
shafts. Here a lot of work was done, first by J. A.
Wallace, and later on by the Bull crowd. In the Gift
block, Bayne sampled one body of auriferous copper ore
and reckoned it worth £32,400. He tells of other blocks
that he has skimmed over, but, like a wise man, he only
deals in detail with that one, and two or three trifling
ore bodies left in the stopes. Bayne throws on one side
the chlorination idea. In that he follows Harris, who
wound up his tribulations at the Bethanga by urging the
erection of smelters, and got left on the dump-heap in
consequence. Bayne, in plumping for smelting to a
matte basis, sets 355. per ton of ore as the outside cost.
But here he may set the figure too low, for he has a very
complex ore to handle. Every other method has been
tried, and if smelting won’t answer, then the mine had
better be scrapped ; for it has cost more money in ex-
perimenting than almost any other show in Australia.
* * » *
Chairman Patterson, of the Broken Hill Prop.,
said at the half-yearly meeting that, a reserve fund of
£250,000 having now been built up, the whole profits will
be available for dividend purposes. Last half-year the
net profits rah to £314,284, and the dividends absorbed
£240,000 (ss. per share), so there was enough left over for
another Is. 6d. per share. But it is pretty certain that
the amount written off for depreciation will have to be
heavier in the future—or, what comes to the same
thing, more works will have to be paid for out of
revenue. A heavy expenditure on zinc plant is
pending ; the chances are that, as the co. is only groping
its way, much of it will have to be scrapped before long,
and at best there is the certainty of very heavy wear and
tear in connection with retorts, acid works, etc. For the
moment the promise held out by the chairman looks very
attractive ; but, after all, though the cash, refinery pro-
ducts, etc., on hand exceeded the liabilities on November
30 by £435,051, that represents the accumulation of 21J
years.

Here is a comparison of the recoveries of silver
and lead at the concentrating mills of those Broken Hill
cos. which, publish their records. The figures are taken,
or worked out, from the cos.’ last half-yearly or yearly
reports; and milling costs are added: —■.
Lead. Silver. Costs.
North .. 76T p.c 54'87 p.c. 4/7 per ton.
South .. 71.3 „ 53-2 „ 4/0-3 „
Sulphide.. 67‘2 „ 47'6 „ 4/6'9 „
Block 10.. 65-69 „ 36‘47 „ 4/11 „
Block 14.. 62-5 ~ 38‘4 „ 6/4'2 „
Broken Hill mills differ in details, but most of the
remarkable variations in the recoveries are due to differ-
ences in the character of the ore. It is a fatal mistake to
suppose that, because these mines are on one lode, they
have tlie same problems, and that their ore is worth the
same. It is on account of the refractory nature of its ore
that the Junction North, for instance, has hung fire
hitherto, though as to both the quantity and metallic
value only good reports are now possible. In short, it is
not what is in the ore, but what can be got out of it, that
counts.
* * * *
‘ Shares in Queen Bee, (N.S.W.), sprang
again last week on the report that the optionists had
paid up the first £lOOO ; and that was taken to mean that
the £125,000 (50s. per share) is going to be paid. Fact is
that, under the agreement, £lOOO had to be paid to get
an option for a month ; the agreement was absolutely
binding as to this first £lOOO. If the next £3OOO or so is
put up it will mean something—chief! 3' that Corbould, of
Lloyd’s, who, it is understood, is to report, thinks well
of the prospects. The fact that Corbould is the optionists’
expert indicates that the big money is to come from
England, if anywhere.

“ Interested ” : The old dispute of the Budgery
lode strike had a flicker last week, when the directors,
taking exception to some remarks by the Daily Tklk-
grapii, wired from Cobar to say they still believed the
strike of the Budgery lode to be west of north. As the
mine has now been working 12 months, and the hanging
wall has been cut in three places, and the foot wall in as
many more, it is about time they were able to speak
positively on the point, and say it is so-and-so or it is not.
What between the new shaft, the battery at
the Eleanora section, and the electric power scheme, the
New Hillgrove Prop. (N.S.W.) has its hands full. No
doubt the expenditure is justified ; for the enrichment in
the levels coining in from Baker’s Creek mine proves the
gold shoots to live down to No. 16 level, and to enter the
New Hillgrove. Nevertheless, the co. needs to have a
deep pocket to see it all through.
* * * *
Lightning Ridge (N.S.W.) opal field never had
much of a bottom, but what it had is falling out. Many of
the old hands who have clung tight to it are at last desert-
ing it, and now, although the dailies have had pars about
400 people being on the field, there are in reality no more
than 130, including those in business. Of course, some
fair parcels have been won at the place ; it is alleged that
Bishop and Son, the most successful party, sold £2500
worth. But the stone is too scattered, and the man who
goes there with a tenner or so has about the same chance
as he would have in Tatt.’s, only some of Tatt.’s prizes
are larger. On the whole, the cheapest way to get opal
is to buy , « «
After an almost Homeric fight against adverse
circumstances, the Aberdare Co-operative coal mine,
Ipswich (6.), has gone under. The miners, in 1893, took
it over from Lewis Thomas ; he had been working it at a
loss, and offered it to them at Is. per ton royalty, with a
minimum of £lOO per month. The men struck little but
disaster. For a start they had to purchase a pump cost-
ing £l5OO. Then there was a fall in the mine. After
that a fire broke out. Next another fall blocked the air
passage In 1906 a new shaft was sunk to a depth of
150ffc. without coming- to coal. But Lewis Thomas’
royalty was the heaviest burden the mine had to bear;
in 12 years it amounted to £40,000. Despite all its
troubles, how-ever, the mine had a record to be proud of
in one direction ; only one fatal accident occurred during
the 00-operative Co.’s tenancy, and, strangely enough,
that happened the first week the co. had it.
• • • *
Charters Towers is in a bad way. It neglected
development work shamefully, until lately it has been
forced irito it. Now it wants all the help it can get.
Especially it wants outside help ; because the man with
money doesn’t, as a rule, stay in the Towers. For a long
while the outside reputation of the Towers’ “ mining
crowd ” has been that it picks the bone very clean, and then
passes it on. Just when the field most wanted to knock
out that idea, trouble has arisen over the Carrington, the
best mine the district has dropped on to for years. The
people who have had it have been either fools or frauds,
and outsiders have fallen in badly over its in-and-out
running. Now there are things in connection with the
issue of 60,400 shares that will have to be explained if
Towers’ mining people want to be trusted. A
Towers’ mining man puts it this way:—“We
have a property here called Carrington’s. It was
likely to turn out good, so we reserved 50,400 shares ;
18,000 of them were paid up, and 32,400 contributing.
The co. has a capital of £60,000, in 240,000 shares at ss.
A man went out and pegged out some more ground ;
and we bought that land off him for £9OO. The
directors seem to have had doubts whether it was
worth the price; anyhow, they made him take
18,000 fully paid up shares (55.) at Is. each. That took
the £9OO purchase money from him again—and gave him
18,000 shares at Is. Then he signed an agreement that
he would take up 32,400 contributing shares at sixpence
each ; that came to £BlO. After he had fulfilled his con-
tract by lifting the contribs., the directors declared a
three-penny dividend. So we placed the whole of the
50,400 reserved shares locally, and they only cost the
men who took them up £lBO for the lot. The
transaction was practically carried through some months
ago, but nothing was announced with regard to it, and
the public has dealt in the stock, not knowing that the
reserved shares had Deen issued, or the extraordinary terms
upon which they had been issued. Finally, it is only
necessary to say'that even contributing shares are now
worth about 6s. 3d.; and, though they have been worth
a little less, the question is whether they would have
been worth less if the mine had been rightly worked.”
If the directors have got an explanation, it will be well
for Charters Towers and for themselves to uncork it.

“Mainshaft”: Re the Melbourne reports on
which the Celestial Copper and Options Co. is being
boomed. The Horseshoe doesn’t adjoin the Daisy, which
has turned out 30 per cent, ore, and will turn out some
more if the 9in. or lOin. of good stuff on the hanging wall
holds out. If copper-stained quartz ever becomes worth
£lO7 a ton, the Daisy will be able to sell ore from a lode
6ft. wide ; but that won’t make the Horseshoe adjoin the
Daisy, and it wouldn’t do the Horseshoe any good if it
did. There are plenty of good shows on Cloncurry to be
got by looking for them and paying for them, and it’s a
pity for the field’s sake to see these heaps of painted
silica eating good money.
* * * *
The snarl with which the Mt. Lyell Consols
(Tas.) board received the bone thrown down to it by
T. Coleman and Co., of the London Stock Exchange, has
ended their relations, save of a solemn business kind.
Idea was to lump the Consols and the Tasman and Crown
Lyell into one co. with 300,000 shares, mostly of 7s. 3d.
each, to be cut up in various ways. As the deal is off, it
is not necessary to go into details ; but it looked rather a
soft snap for the Tasman and Crown. Tom Hills, of the
last co., made out that the reconstructed show would
have a capital of £lB,OOO. Even if his addition,
division and multiplication were right, and if there
were no big algebraical cc in the calculation, there
was in one sense not much to cut up between two
shows, and in another sense there was far too much. The
Consols is an extension of the Lyell Blocks copper
clays, and it has to be shown to the investor that the
Lyell Blocks has returned capital, much less paid in-
terest. Tasman and Crown Lyell so far is not anything
except a gamble. The man has still to show up that can
profitably concentrate the minerals out of the Lyell
schists. North Lyell and Lyell Tharsis had a shot at the
problem, and went a “cropper” despite close picking.
However, as the Consols would not hitch on to the Cole-
mans’ motor car, a new rig-out is to be found for the Tas-
man and Crown. What the inwardness of the change is, no
one quite explains. Perhaps as Tom Hills was able to
elucidate the amalgamation problem, he now can solve
this other one.

“Vigilans”: Anywhere but in the Northern
Territory, Brocks’ wolfram mine, at Wolfram Creek, would
be a great concern, with copper at £lOB per ton and
wolfram over £lOO. Some tons of grey ore have recently
been bagged going 50 per cent, metal. The wolfram is
neglected. It is sometimes amusing and sometimes sad
to watch the four Mongolians pounding away with spring
dollies, and two Ah Fats manipulating the old-fashioned
sluice box, while a weary moke, driven by a Pagan, does
the winding.
* • * *
The Citizens’ Life office is thinking over two
schemes for its improvement. One involves profit-shar-
ing with its staff, which would be an extension of the
principle on which the office is founded ; for guarantee-
ing to keep expenses below 15 per cent, of the income,
the shareholders take one-fifth of the profits, and the
idea is now to cut up part of that one-fifth among- the
employees, who, to a very great extent, make the low
expense rate possible. The other scheme, only just
suggested so far, is to let the policy-holders elect
trustees, who would make an annual report about the
securities. Policy-holders in general want two things
—good profits and safe securities. The Citizens’
shareholders, who manage the business, are per-
sonally interested in se.eing that the profits are good—
they get one-fifth of them—and the appointment of
trustees would be a safeguard against the directors-
getting large immediate profits at the cost of probable
heavy future losses. Up to now, certainly, they haven’t
shown any disposition that way ; on the contrary, the
office’s holding of what are reckoned the safest Austra-
lian securities (public stocks) is exceptionally large. The
appointment of trustees ought to do something to keep-
it like that.
Julius Matton, an authority on|thelead market,
says in his circular that the decreased consumption of’
lead in England during 1906 was due to stagnation in the
building trade. This will come as a surprise to those
who have been led to believe that high prices are due to-
ad-round increased consumption. Still, the large increase
in protected countries compensated for the stagnation
in England. Lead touched six cents a pound (£2B per
ton) in New York at the close of the year ; and, in spite
of the prohibitive duty, the Americans purchased 8000-
tons from the English market —a most unusual proceeding.

* * * *
The Heskett-Moore people, who have a patent
for a direct iron-manufacturing process, have started
litigating with an American inventor, whose Australian
patent they lately opposed. The Commissioner of
Patents decided against the Heskett-Moore people, and
gave costs against them, holding that “ one very great
difference between the two processes is that in the Hes-
kett-Moore invention the reduction of the iron ore to the
metallic state and the fusing of it are two distinct opera-
tions, whereas in the applicant’s process the reduction
and fusion are carried out in one process.” The case is,
however, to be carried further—the High Court is the
next step.
■*•***
Proposal to generate electricity at
the Barron Waterfalls (N.Q.), considered as a State-
owned enterprise, should be viewed with caution. There
is a tendency to shove the State into the unprofitable
undertakings, and then howl about Socialism. Queens-
land has a railway right alongside these Barron Falls, and
this power, handy to the line, suggests electrification ;
but such a scheme would have to extend right across the
country, and the traffic revenue would not warrant it.
For the State to use the Barron Falls to light up Cairns,
which is already lit, or to take power to a distant sugar-
mill, is assuming a big risk,' with very little chance of
profit. As to the manufacture of carbide of calcium, or
the generation of power for electrolytic works for
refining copper, it should be noted that Mt. Lyell,
Wallaroo, and Cobar are the largest copper producers,
and that a refining co. to cater for the rest has been
formed. !
* * * *
A Melbourne co. is being formed to again
tickle the Kamarooka quartz reef, 20 miles north of
Bendigo. This line, 20 or 30 years ago, gave a lot of
people a bad taste in the mouth. One co. got a few good
crushings ; then it was said that the reef was petering
out. A slump followed ; and a few people sucked up the
shares. Then one day the men were put on to another
part of the show and got ounce stone ; also, it was stated
that the co. had got stuff on the surface that had been
left by a former co. This had been tested, and was esti-
mated to go about 3oz. Shares sailed up again, and the
men who had got in got out at a fine profit. When
experts examined the big heap of stuff that was to go
over 3oz. to the ton, they said that there would be more
gold in a set of teeth, and, in disgust, the mine was
abandoned and the flash machinery sold. It has taken
over 20 years to get anybody to look at the district since
that experience, and there’s some reason to expect that
the district didn’t quite deserve so much neglect.

A Tasmanian sawmill got a whole family
thrown in its teeth the other day. The letter ran
like this
Dear Sir, —Please if they are any prospect of a Family
of 4or 5 getting employment at your sawmill. Please if
their is a Job, please send immediate reply at once, and
tell me the most couvient way of getting there. I
William Johns Senior is a bit accomplished at timber
handling and Willie Johns Junior is also fairly good at it.
John Johns is a little bit good, and used to a paling
bench or dust hole or timber handling ; and Alfred Johns
is just the same as John Johns. Fred Johns as gone
away at present, an don’t know were he is, but he may
be able to follow soon.
William Johns Sen. get .. .. 6/- per day.
William Johns Jun. 5/- ~ ~
John ~ ~ ~ .. .. 4/- ~ ~
Alfred 3/- ~ ~
Total 18/- „ ~
18/- shillings is our ordinary wages per day.
P.S.—Fred Johns is a bushman.
“Veldt”: “John Moran” (B. 7/2,/’O7)
flings a big stick at “ The Prooshan,” and misses
him badly when he says that the carpet snake
never slimes its prey previous to swallowing. If
“ J.M.” took the trouble to visit a sixpenny hash-
house he would see one hundred and two speci-
mens of the genus homo eating in one hundred and
twenty-seven different ways, from the Domain
resident who hogs his food with his fingers, to
the semi-genteel has-been, who uses a soiled
handkerchief for a serviette and his tumbler for a
finger-bowl. The same, or different, differences
may apply to the carpet snake. At any rate, the
snake 1 knew didn’t know the rules of etiquette laid
down by “John Moran” any better than he knew
the Odes of Horace. One day, after washing up
the dinner things (in a camp not far from
Grafton), I went to the tent f®r forty winks, leav-
ing the tin dish resting against the wall, hollow
side outwards. An hour later, hearing a slight
noise, I looked into the back kitchen and found
the aforesaid dish lying on the floor face down-
wards, and the camp kitten sitting close by with
a dazed expression on its face, and slimed on
one side from observation point to base. On lift-
ing the tin I made a sudden grab for a gun, and
blew the carpet snake that lay coiled up under-
neath into several pieces. Now, I didn’t see the
snake slime the cat, therefore there was no proof
of guilt, but I had sufficient circumstantial evi-
dence to do Jedburgh justice on the snake.
* * * •
“Shingle” : Count me in as one more
witness in “Prooshan’s” favor re carpet snakes
sliming their prey (B. 21/2/’O7). I have had con-
vincing evidence on several occasions that
“Prooshan” is right, and John Moran wrong,
with respect to Southern Queensland snakes at
any rate. When Rosewood Scrub, near Ipswich,
was being first settled, in the "sixties and early
’seventies, carpet snakes were very numerous and
attained to a considerable size. The largest I
ever had a personal encounter with measured
13ft. 2in. When 1 came across this fellow he was
just about to swallow a full-grown ’possum, which
he had crushed until it was nearly half as long
again as its usual dimensions, and considerably
reduced in thickness, and it tv<is slimed all over.
I have seen several other instances where young
wallabies and other animals and birds were either
being swallowed or prepared for swallowing, and
in two other cases, at least, there could be no
doubt about the sliming. By the way, I killed a
goanna in that same scrub, which measured 7ft.
from tip of nose to end of tail. It was too fat
and bulky to move, except very slowly, much less
to get up a tree. Its skin, after being tanned,
made two very fine saddle-bag fronts. [About a
dozen other letters, all supporting “The
Prooshan’s ” statement, are crowded out. As for
John Moran, he doesn’t seem to have a friend
any where.—Ed. B.]

“The Mooch About” on some famous
eooks:—
No shearers’ cooks were better known through the
parching West than “ Jimmy the Rambler,” old “ Scotty
wd ,Y.'' inkler ” (Phil Mowbray), “Jimmy the Rooster,”
Soldier Green,” “ Jacky Without a Shirt,” “Caring-
ton,” “ Blackguard Brummy,” Lady May,” &c. Ail
hard cases, and loaded up to the brim with eccentrici-
ties. “ Jimmy the Rambler” was an excellent cook, but
a terrible “ nark,” and would rambleoff on the slightest
provocation. Above all things, he hated to have his
bread or brownie picked at before it was cold. “The
Wrinkler” took on cooking occasionally. Scrupulously
clean, and methodical in all his work, he was a fair
dough-banger, but objected to associate with the shearers.
In one shed, his bile rose high when a shearer invited
him to take a hand in a game of crib. “Scotty’s”
hair stood up as he addressed the shearers : “ I want'you
all distinctly to understand that I’m engaged to cook for
you, and not to cater for your amusement. When I’ve
filled up your hungry innards I have done with you, and
I don’t wish to see or hear you.” “ The Wrinkler ” was
not a popular shearers’ cook, but beloved by the whalers,
and full of wrinkles. At spinning yarns I’ve never heard
“Jimmy the Rooster” beaten. His cooking was just
so-so ; but once he got in it took something bo move him.
He was a rooster. “ Soldier Green ” was the champion
of all cooks. Had been in the army'. Made big cheques,
and used to go to Melbourne with them. No lambing-
down in a stringy-bark shanty for the “Soldier.” He
would land in Melbourne with from £6O to £BO, and “ do
it in” on “beer sparrers” in Plinders-street. Run in
once, the arresting policeman told the Bench that “ The
Soldier’’was a terrible nuisance once every year. “’E
makes all Flinders-street bloind dhrunk in "a jiffy, an’
give the perlice lots o’ throuble an’ broken "ribs.”
“ Jacky without a Shirt ” was good and generous. Once,
years ago, a Sydney pushite came to Jacky without bags.
Jacky promptly took off his shirt, tore off the sleeves,
filled them with tea and sugar respectively, filled the body
of the shirt with flour, handed them to tlie boy from “ the
village,” and continued working. That’s how he got his
name. “ Carington ” was “ flash.” Used to carry fancy
yeast, coloring for sauces, etc., and “ once cooked for Lord
Carington.” “ Brummy ” was a middling dough-banger,
but a marvellous linguist. Burnt his hands once, and
swore so hard that a dressed sheep on the gallows twisted
loose and cleared. At least so “Joe the Whaler ” says.
* * * *
“The Prooshan ” : On walking over some
newly-ploughed ground one day, I saw a large,
grey, swamp crane, standing very still, gazing
down a furrow. On my approaching, the crane
didn’t shift, and soon I saw a big brown snake
slowly creeping towards it. When the brown
oppressor was within two yards, the crane started
to tremble and flap its wings, but didn’t leave.
On my shying a dry clod at the snake the bird
gave a loud “ Yah ! ” and flew straight into a
wire fence, some fifty yards away, crippling
itself badly. Apparently the clod broke the
spell.

“ A. 8.” : Ptatriotic Victorians should pro-
test strongly against the tendency to Anglicise
the few aboriginal names left to different towns.
In Vic. Jim Bruk has become Gembrook—a
title that suggests an old water-wheel turning in
the background, and half-a-dozen lambkins frisk-
ing in charge of a person in knee-breeches.
Korumbura is beginning to be called “The
Borough,” after a particularly disreputable
quarter of London, and, in a few years, will
probably appear on Government maps as “ Coram
Borough ” ; while Carlsruhe, Kilmore and half-a-
dozen other Vic. townships bear titles that are
simply European names bearing some slight
or fancied resemblance to the original names of
the districts. I fully expect that by the time the
Barren Jack (N.S.W.) reservoir is completed it
will be gazetted as “ Sterile John.”
* * » *
“ Duroc ” : “ Batlow ” (B. 14/2/’O7) says
horses’ homing instinct is marvellous.
I’ve been among them all my life, and
have come to the sad conclusion that for
crass idiocy a horse is the limit, and
that a sheep has more of the instinct
“Batlow” mentions than the “ noble
steed.” I’ve tried that game, “leave it
to the horse ’’—tried it in America, in
Europe, and scores of times in Aus-
tralia, and never once has the horse
brought me right or anywhere near right.
I own a horse now which has been
ridden and driven, in and out for years,
to and from the nearest township over an
ordinary bush track (horse always stable-
fed). Started out from town one black
night, and trusted to the horse. (Same
old game.) In the grey light of a
very-late-risen moon the horse came to
a house I had never seen before. Found
myself and horse up against a homestead
three miles off my track. Yet that horse
is far more intelligent than ordinary.
Again, when pumping water from a
station bore to fill sheep-troughs, have
seen a mob of brumbies come into the
trough paddock to drink, as there was
no other water on the run. There was
only one gate to go or come by, and there
was no feed whatever in the paddock.
Yet I have seen those brumbies actually
unable to find the gate they came in by.
This I can verify by five witnesses, in-
cluding the station-manager, who turned
out the witless beasts after they had
done a three-days’ starve. I have bred
and sold and bought horses, have ridden
and driven them under all sorts of condi-
tions ; and I will back bullock, donkey,
or mule (especially mule) for all-round
intelligence against the average moke
every time.

“ Stony ” : “If such a thing as a Hebrew
bush m an were possi ble, ” says so m eone in B. 21/2/’O7.
I once met a horny-handed Chosen Person who
was a bushman, if ever there was one. I first
struck him at the old Calcifer (N.Q.) smelter, on a
blazing day, when I was unloading a fodder-
waggon. He came up with a swag on his back to
make inquiries—a man with a Hebraic name and
a large Hebraic nose, through which he spoke,
and a huge, black beard. What’s more, he got
work on the mine, and my first impressions of
him as a bushman were borne out by my later
observations. There are bushmen of every human
kind.

“Jeff”: Re records in bailing up and
milking cows (B. 14/2/’O7). Here’s mine. Dad
roused me up at 5.30 to fetch in our milker.
Returned without her at 8 (breakfast time). Dad
mentioned something that would happen to me
if I came in again without her. Chased her all
day, slept that night in the barn, then “bor-
rowed ” a nag, and had her in the bail by 7. My
record in the Book of Time stands thus: “To
bailing one spotted cow-devil, 26| hours.”

has a 400-

for domestic

crossing into

about a three
weeks’supply

the State ss.
per 100 gal-
lons. Last year,
and it was an ex-
ceptional one for

pa’d out. If a dry

£1 a month will not
cover expenses. A 1000-
gallon tank, with the one
at present at use, would
ensure a couple of months’ supply of water, and
the large tank can be delivered at the gate for
£4 10s. But Joseph Carruthers imagines that

an asset that will at least realise £4 for the next
five years.

“ Boosef ” : One Sunday, driving in the
bush, with the thermometer at about 115 in the
water-bag, and the flies averaging one million
and fifty-one to the man, I came across a be-
whiskered individual, accompanied by a young
fellow and a cattle dog. They were carrying
respectively a crowbar, a shovel, and a sore leg.
He (Whiskers) informed me that he was captain
of the Wattergumbla Cricket Club. They had
been playing that afternoon, and the ball had
been hit into a rabbit burrow. He had thereupon
walked to his place, a distance of two miles, and
was returning to dig the ball out. After getting
a drink and a match, and remarking for the
seventh time ‘‘lt’s ’ot,” he left me wondering
whether to recommend him for the Victoria Cross
or Callan Park.
WASN'T LOOKING.
BOB (gleefully): “My! What sort of a lookin'
cove gave yer the bottle?”

I got it.”
“8.D.5.”: When opal-seeking at White
Cliffs I ran across a strange party. Fred’s work-
ing mate was a Salvo bloke, by name Henry.
Now Henry had assumed the leadership of the
concern, and was wont to divide the spoil, lie
had three bags—one for Fred, one for himself,
and one for the Lord, who was alleged to hold a
sleeping interest. Dividing the opal into three
parts, and placing each in one of the bags, he
would say, “That’s for you, that’s for me, and
that’s for the Lord.” And he took possession of
the last two portions. This went on tor some
time,' and they had won altogether perhaps £1
worth of gem, when, one day, a lucky strike pro-
duced £3OO. That evening the division was made
as usual, but Fred reached over, and grasping
two bags, gently said, “ I’ll take the Lords
whack this time, Henry!” adding, reassuring y,
“and I’ll see that He gets it.” bhortly after-
wards a dissolution took place.
Take a little Wolfe’s Schnapps morning
and night to keep the stomach, bowels and kidneys
in good order. And it does the business.
THE TALE OF SARAH JONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is up to someone to write a poem on Sarah Jones.—
B. 31/l/’07.)
Then here’s a poem on Sarah Jones,
Our kitchen girl was she.
When Sarah sang, her heavenly tones
Made richest melody.
Last night our local cop. was bent
On making love to Sal,
And for a moonlight stroll they went
Down past the hospital.
They wandered in the shadows dim,
They rambled arm in arm,
Then sat them down—a lover’s whim—
Upon a bull-ant farm !
They talked of flowers, of wedded bliss,
Of spangles in the sky—
With now and then a stolen kiss,
And now and then a sigh.
But all the bull-ant army rose
And all its hordes did stir.
She screamed, but folks did but suppose
The Law had tickled her.
The whole night long the folks did hear
Most melancholy groans,
But no one sought to interfere
Twixt Love and Sarah Jones !
The mopokes moped most dismally,
Sad was the plovers’ fret.
The crow awoke within his tree—
Methinks I hear him yet !
At twelve, since Sarah was non est
(Her couch unruffled lay)—
Upon a hurried, anxious quest
We took the lockup way !
And there the drunk upon his bed
Was howling dreadfully,
But not a sign of Trooper Z
Or Sarah could we see.
The place w'as roused, the tidings sped,
As in the gloom we groped ;
The witches of the hamlet said
That Sarah had eloped 1
But when the early danvn was red
Behind the ranges black,
We found that she and Trooper Z
Had gone the heavenly track.
Upon the bull-ant farm there lay
Nought but their whitened bones—
Grist to the bull-ants’ store were they—
The cop. and Sarah Jones !
VIC.
NYM

Hergott and Innamincka (Central S.A.)
report widespread rains, varying from 4in. to
13in., the best, in some cases, for 20 years. The
Cooper and the Diamantina are in flood, and
mailmen go in peril of their lives, and have had
to abandon their coaches in several cases.

“Cict”: Be “Prawn’s” porcupine-slay-
ing dogs (B. 17/l/’O7). I will back “porky” to
duck his head (that’s his bill) under him some-
where and commence sinking before “Prawn’s”
dog can get within turning distance of him ; and,
by the time the dog is close enough to commence
operations, he’ll have nought but a lump of spiny
stuff to nibble at. True, as “ Prawn ” has it, the
overturned “porky” is absolutely defenceless
when before a dog ; but, in nine cases out of ten,
it takes a big lift to upend him, even when a
stick is forced clean under him. The tenth case
is when “porky” has started burrowing on a
rock. None but the most unsophisticated pup
will look at a porcupine.

“T.W.D.” : A Hawkes Bay (M.L.) town-
ship had its first agricultural show the other day ;
and among other “ novelty ” items was a chain-
stepping contest. Entry a bob ; prize a guinea.
A Maori woman won. Later, at the pah, with
many grins, they told me how. Our unsophis-
ticated sister had tethered her ankles with a cord,
which enabled her to step a yard exactly, and
her petticoats had hidden the fraud. There was
much waipiro at the pah that night.

‘ ‘ Edyson ” : This is a convincing, work-a-
day romance, in which only the names are
fictitious. It comes from Kyroot (Vic.). The
belle of Kyroot was a beautiful, black-eyed,
raven-haired, rosy-cheeked Methodist maiden of
20, whose father was a hard-headed, hard-fisted
Cornishman named Kershaw. Kershaw," of
Kyroot, was one of those unreasonable Christian
parents, who cannot perceive the natural advances
of young men towards their eligible girls without
wanting to break with the teachings of religion
and the restraints of law. He regarded his
daughter as a working asset, and declined to lose
her services. When he had knocked the fiery
ambition out of three aspirants for Miss Kershaw’s
favor, a decided coolness towards that very
desirable little lady manifested itself amongst
the young men of Kyroot. Then Ball opened
a grocery at the Old Road, and fell in love
with Nellie Kershaw at church on the
following Sunday. He paid Miss Kershaw
some attention, and collided with her father
one night after seeing Nellie home from
prayer-meeting. Then Ball believed the
current reports about Kershaw, senior.
He rested from his labors, and got
well, but he meditated vengeance.
In the course of a fortnight, an
assistant appeared at Ball’s store.
He was a young man, splendidly
set-up, rather good-looking, allow-
ing for a crumpled" ear and sundry
facial damages, and his name was
Dan Corcoran. Dan went to church.
Dan fell in love with Nellie Kershaw.
The fall was so sudden it seemed deliberate.
Dan offered Nellie his services as a gallant,
and Nellie seemed pleased with them.
People ran to tell Corcoran to insure his life,
but he only smiled a wan smile, and said he
would bear his cross like a man and a
Methodist. He and Kershaw met in Car-
ver’s paddock, when Corcoran was holding
Miss Kershaw hard round the waist, and
hostilities opened immediately. Nellie was
sole spectator and ten-seconds-check. The
fight lasted four minutes and a half, at the
expiration of which time Mr. Kershaw was
bleeding in seven places, and slowly regain-
ing his mental perspective. Corcoran was
quite uninjured. The joy of the young men
of Kyroot when they heard of this pleasant
surprise was so great that they gave Cor-
coran a smoke social. Ball paid Corcoran
according to contract, put on his clean collar
and a nice tie, and prepared to resume
operations with Miss Kershaw. Alas for
human frailty ! He struck trouble again,
and this time it was with Corcoran. Dan
licked him first, and informed him after-
wards that he (Dan) intended remaining at
Kyroot to maintain the courtship so auspiciously
commenced. Corcoran married Nellie, and old
Kershaw weloomed him as a son-in-law, and gave
him a partnership in the Kershaw farm. The
moral is that a father-in-law can’t be properly
licked by deputy.
* * * *
“F.H.R.”: Be dog endurance. The
loneliest and most cruel use man puts the dog to
sheep. I have seen dogs, tethered by
a chain and a ring attached to a long piece of wire
stretched between two posts, keep sheep back for
months at a stretch. Certainly they were looked
after in a way. A boundary-rider might come
round every second day, and throw the dog
(which often had more intelligence than the
boundary-rider) a sheep’s fry, and fill a tin with
water; but if anything happened to knock this
over, the poor animal went short, and all the sym-
pathy he would get was a kick and “serve yer
right.” Once, going across some abandoned
country out from Miandetta (N.S.W.), I found an
old hut with the grass growing inside and waving
through the windows. In an outhouse I came
across an old, rusty chain, the collar of which was
still fastened round the neck of the skeleton of a
dog. Don’t know how he came to be abandoned,
but I thought it the saddest sight that I ever
looked upon. How long that dog endured before
the haunting crows stepped in and finished his
sufferings will never be known. I hope his ghost
haunts the fellow who left him.

Never take harsh cathartics to move the
bowels. They do more harm than good. What

Chamberlain’s Tablets, for they are mild and
gentle in their action.*
“ Mallarapoonie ” on bird-snatching :
The desert-bred native tribes of N.-W. Australia prac-
tise a quaint method of capturing the common brown
kite. On desert country, where stones are rare, a large
termite nest is selected and scooped out, chimney-fashion,
until the hollow is large enough to receive a native in a
crouching position. In stony country the blacks build
up a hollow cairn of stones some 4ft. high by 3ft. inside
measurement. In one of these receptacles a native
crouches, armed with a dead goanna, while his mates
place a few cross-sticks and leaves over the openingabove
his head. The mates then fire some adjacent patches of
spinifex, and retire to await results.
The kites congregate to the smoke in search of prey—
half-scorched locusts, lizards, &c. —and while circling
round, their attention is attracted to what appears to
them to be a large lizard fooling round among some fallen
brushwood. Forthwith they begin to swoop down and
snatch at it. For the first half-dozen swoops the nigger
inside just acts the “passive resister”—that is, he just
hangs to his dead goanna with both hands, and sits tight.
Then he gets to business. With the go. gripped firmly
in his left hand, he bobs its head up through the opening
always keeping his right hand ready to snap. A hawk
swoops down, snatches, and for the fraction of a second
gives a clinging tug, but that space of time is sufficient
for the nig. He seizes the bird by the hind legs and
jerks it violently down through the aperture. Then he
wrings its neck, drops it among his feet, and goes on
bobbing the goanna for the next bird. The snatching
propensities of a brown kite are astounding. They have
been known to snatch a chap’s breakfast steak off the
camp-fire, and it is stoutly alleged they could snatch a
lost soul back out of Tophet. But it has never been
correctly ascertained whether they ever snatched a dead
goamia from a Mootburra black.
Was riding over spinifex country on the Sterling, one
day, and noticed about 6CKhawks circling round a cairn
of stones. Saw two disappear hurriedly, so I rode up
closer and said, “ Hullo there !”in a firm voice. Then a
large blackfellow violently burst off the lid and lit out
for a belt of scrub like a wasp-hunted land-agent. There
were 14 dead hawks in that cairn.
■» * * *
“ Goolwa ” : I read recently of a man who
had a soft spot in his heart for Asiatics. After a
recent visit to an Afghan, or Hindu, camp at
Dunolly (Vic.) I’m inclined to think he has a soft
spot in his head. In any case, he should be
closely watched for further symptoms. A dozen
of the dusky Khans, with their white Australian
Dulcineas, and a dozen or so any-colored picanin-
nies, form the population. Houses are made out
of battered kerosene tins, with mud chimneys,
and peace and stink reign supreme. The camp is
situated, above all places, at the rear of the local
hospital. A cordon of hawking vans surrounds it,
and an apparently-Kaffir woman seems to be the
mistress of the emporium. One turbaned coon
proudly informed me that she was married. Then,
I take it, the white Australian girls are not.
These girls are obtained from Melbourne, and I
am told there is no trouble experienced in getting
them. As I gazed on the miserable, God forsaken
scene I thought of many things, and not the least
of them were Bruce Smith and the fearsome E.
W. Cole, of the monkey-house. Anyhow, can’t
the law do something ?

“Cecil P.” : Be sandhills which are all
abrupt on the northern and sloping on the
southern side—by some “mysterious law of
nature’’ (B. 21/2/’O7). Isn’t the mystery very
simple, and don’t the prevailing winds explain
it ? They do, a good few hundreds of miles to
the sou’-west of the country spoken of. They
also give the sandhills, over large tracts of
country, one general “run ” or direction.
TUMUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is far away at Tumut where the terraced mountains
rise
To o’erlook the fertile valley where the dreaming town-
ship lies,
And their snow-caps in the winter shine like silver
diadems,
As if Nature capped their glory with a crown of pallid
gems.
Like the bones of perished gods are they in wild disorder
heaped,
And in their lore and legends all my dreaming soul is
steeped.
It is far away at Tumut where the rolling hills of green
Ever smile upon the valleys lying slumb’ring in between,
Where the timber rears its columns, and in verdant
masses waves,
Tangling in a leafy chaos, making dreamy architraves,
Where the paths w’ind thro’ the woodland—O, I’ve often
lingered there
For an hour alone W'ith Nature, free from sorrow, strife,
and care !
It is far away at Tumut where the wind sings in the
wheat,
Crooning strains of mellow music that are ever strangely
sweet;
Where the festal robes of orchards gleam before, around,
behind,
And the song-birds shyly utter secret meanings of the
wind;
Where the wild vine weaves its tendrils, and the bush-
birds flit and sing
’Mid the thickets, where in garlands blossoms weave, and
sway and cling.
It is far aw r ay at Tumut where the winter tjreath that
chills
Comes with giant, unseen shoulders, striking mountains,
brushing hills,
Where the summer breezes, wand’ring from the scented
wilderness,
Lightly touch the maiden’s dimples, like a roseleaf’s soft
caress ;
Where the morning rifts of sunshine with the noonday
sunrays vie,
And the gleams of evening linger for an hour before they
die.
Towards the lotus land of Tumut, aye, my future course
is planned,
Lying westward from the ocean—far from city’s lethal
land.
When the sweet fruits swell and redden, and the fragrance
brewed from rain
Wafts from corn and wheat crops waving, I shall visit
there again.
And I’ll listen to the voices whisp’ring “Linger here and
rest ” ;
And I’ll heed them, and I’ll bid a long farewell to further
West !
N.S.W.
GEORGE F. BRIGGS.

communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

A medical item from “ Struggler ”
In our little way-back town threeyoungdoctors are wear-
ing their pants thin waiting for someone to cure or kill,
while an old Chinese quack is kept busy from daylight
till dark giving “ adviee.” They all flock to Dr. Ah Hem.
He is a marvel, this dirty, little, yellow Asiatic. He can
cure nearly anything with a penny and the white of an
egg. Went to see a sick man one night. Beads of
perspiration stood out on his white face as the old man
with the slant eyes and dirty paws rubbed him down
with a penny dipped in the white of an egg. I tried to
get him to call in a doctor, for he was very ill; but no,
he would not take anything but Ah Hem’s “medicine.”
Ah Hem told the missus make it herself—
cockroaches boiled to a pulp and mixed with rum': two
teaspoonfuls to be taken when required. And he took it.
Moreover, he is alive ; and the three pairs of medical
pants are wearing thinner.
When troubled with constipation try
Chamberlain’s Tablets. They are easy to take
and produce no griping or other unpleasant
effect. #
“ Hassan” : If you wish to take a lesson
in the art of stalking, let me recommend as
teacher one of thos'e little jumping spiders which
live in the cracks of walls. The disinterested
way in which he will saunter up to an innocent
fly is simply Machiavellian. He radiates good-
humor, his largest eye blandishes while the other
seven judge distances. By the time the fly begins
to think of that appointment, the spider is within
range. It then attaches a thread to the wall
and launches itself into the air. Sometimes the
fly is too quick off the mark for it, in which case
it stands picking its teeth and looking at the sky,
chatting benevolently to the other flies about the
weather, or the cricket match. And sometimes it
tackles a fly a couple of sizes too big for it, and
they both fall off the wall and scrap enthusiastic-
ally in the dust. But when it has done the trick
neatlv it has the victim by the back of the neck,
and bitten to death in a flash. Then it walks off
to a crack, winking at the other prowlers as it
passes with its meat. One of these abandoned
villains lives in the keyhole of my door, and
murders a fly regularly every day.
