The late John Young, of Sydney, is
commonly credited with having formed the first
bowling green in N.S.W., but in the old days of
the Woolpack Inn, Mr. Payten had a green for
the use ©f his customers. Also, bowling greens
were in use in Melbourne in the sixties.
fAtrifdtt McKinley perpetrated the one
joke of his lifetime during his recent trip to
England. He shaved off the lemon-colored
whiskers, Which were generally regarded in Mel-
bourne as Paunch’s insignia of the true humor
—the kind that has a scream of laughter blown
in the bottle.
A unique figure among present-day poli-
ticians of the Commonwealth. . . . An orator who
can throw a halo of attraction around the orifice of
Hades. . . . The one Australian statesman who is
indispensable alike to the Commonwealth and to
the Empire.— M. H. H. Macartney, on Alfred
Deakin , in the National Review.
H. S. Webster, head office manager of the
A.J.S. Bank, who got severely handled at the
Crick trial—-W. P. Crick kept an account at the
A.J.S. while he was Minister for Lands—has
entered into business on his Own account ” A
man who has spent even half of Webster’s 43
years with the sepulchral A.J.S. deserves a
brighter ending than that.
A DISTINCTION.
"An' ef you want a bit o' gard’nin’ done, there's
me 'usban’s bin off the beer this six months,
Miss."
"Off the beer, Mrs. Brown! Why, I saw him
drunk on the road as I came along."
“ O well, it must 'a’ bin whisky; that's all I can
say, Miss."
A veteran lifeboatman crossed the bar,
at Adelaide, recently, aged 80. W. T. Hook
served in the British navy and mercantile marine,
and then joined the pilot service at the Sema-
phore. He was one of the rescuers of the crew of
the wrecked Admella, some years back, and got a
breastful of medals for his work. Also he was
himself wrecked, and came ashore on a raft, the
only survivor from the Corsair, sunk in St.
Vincent’s Gulf.
Melbourne daily papers report that ‘ ‘ Mr.
Frank Hennelly, of Ararat, who has been work-
ing there as a laborer for many years, has come
into a legacy of £lB,OOO by the death of a relative
in Ireland.” That’s the difference between
getting a legacy and a sunstroke. “Mr. Frank
Hennelly, of Ararat, who has been working as a
laborer ” gets the legacy : “a corporation laborer
named Hennelly ” would get the sunstroke.
Mr. Godbehear, candidate for Geelong
(Vic.), struck a patch of bad luck that is almost a
record. He called a meeting of his friends and
supporters,, and about 12 attended. When he
had explained his views, his chief supporter (in
the chair) urged him to retire. Three other warm
supporters asked him to retire. A vote was
taken, and all the warm supporters requested him
to retire. Then Mr. Godbehear retired. What else
could he do ?
The late Colonel Henry S. Olcott, presi-
dent of the Theosophical Society, did one thing
in Australia which merits a sprig of green. A
spooky convert, old Carl Hartmann, of Too-
woomba (Q.), was so struck with Theosophy
that, when dying, he bequeathed his lands and
other possessions, valued at several thousands, to
Olcott, leaving his own family almost destitute.
The mystic warrior left off his chase after the
unfindable mahatma long enough to hand back
the property.
Rev. John Penman, the Recently elected
president of the Methodist Conference, com-
menced life doing a bit of droving up Rqckhamp-
ton (Q.) way. He acted as cook to the camp forj
a time, but says that his services were dispensed
with because he had forgotten to put baking 1
powder in a damper, the size of a cart wheel.
“ Percole ” : Juddy, of Melbourne, is
possessed of a modesty that surpasseth under-
standing. Recently, addressing the Brunswick
(Vic.) electors, he exhorted them to vote—not for
plain him —but for the glory of God and the good
of mankind. What superb being is this who
deigns to tread the mortal earth ? I herewith
start a subscription to buy him a halo, new if
obtainable, second-hand if unavoidable.
Barnum Powell has discovered that he is a
lineal descendant of Captain John Smith, who
founded Virginia, and was the best boy of the
brown girl Pocahontas. At a recent shivoo in
London to celebrate the sailing of the First
Fleeters to America, 300 years ago, Barnum of
the big hat was there beaming. But the affair
turned out to be a poor sort of a maffick after all,
and all the interest centred on one Admiral Rolfe,
who turned up with documentary evidence to
prove himself a descendant of the famous
Pocahontas herself. Powell is understood to
have left in disgust. But aren’t the descendants
on the female side of Montezuma 11. still around ?
Their connection with America is nearly 100
years older than that of Mrs. Pocahontas Smith.
“ Caber ” writes :
Meston is going to run for Warwick. It brings up
reminiscences of the seventies, what time Archie livened
up men and matters in the Ipswich Observer, and John
Irwin, who edited the Queensland Times, used to pick up
the “ reptile contemporary ” in just about as cautious a
manner as a swag-man would his blanket when he knew
a seven-inch centipede was in its folds. Every Limestoner
read “ Smiles,” and Archie managed, at least once a week,
to “ smile ”at Covenanting John. Meston sat for Rose-
wood during nearly the life of one Parliament, but gave
a non-party vote on the “ steel rails ” business. That
vote closed th° political career of the “ Sacred Ibis,” for
in a few days the Rosewood seat was vacant, owing to the
insolvency of the sitting member. t was a fishy piece of
business on the part of the Great Liberal Party, but as
the principal actors have gone where P.N.’s are not re-
coverable, it matters little now.
This is the Victorian record since 1873 of
Arthur Diggs for larceny, housebreaking, and
burglary:—
1873, 1 month : 1874, 3 months ; 1875, 3 months ; 1877,
3 months; 1878,5 years; 1886, 12 months; 1887, 1
month, 1 month, and 2 years; 1889, 5 years; 1894, 1
year ; 1895, 7 years ; 1900, 2 .years ; 1906, 3 months; and
1907, 5 years, repeated three times, concurrent.
One doesn’t know which is the worse failure—the
law as a reformer, or Diggs as a burglar. The
biggest trouble with Diggs is that we know now
what ought to have been done with him in 1873,
but nobody knew for certain at that time; and
that’s one of the snags the theoretically-alluring
indeterminate sentence idea bumps against.
The “bravery” of Queen Wilhelmina’s
husband at the wreck of the Berlin, on the Dutch
coast, stirs Edward VII. to confer the Order of
the Waslistand (or something like that) on the
royal ’ero. The “bravery” will probably boil
down, later, to the fact that the reckless ’Enery
got his feet wet while holding a lamp, or some-
thing. All accounts picture the noble ’Enery as
a brutal lout. His marriage contract with the
Dutch Queen stipulated that he should resign the
job of husband if the union failed to provide an
heir within five years. So far, the heir hasn’t
transpired, though the Queen has suffered two
miscarriages—the first, six months after marriage,
through terror, because s drunken ’Enery tried to
storm the Queen’s apartments to demonstrate her
condition to half-a-dozen intoxicated friends.
This intention was only suppressed because the
guards threatened to slay the crowd on the costly
royal carpet. Perhaps ’Enery is playing to the
gallery for an extension of his engagement as
husband.
“ Henry ” writes :
It is customary to regard champion Bill Squires as a
poor, hard-up fellow who suddenly sprang into opulence
by discovering that he could put his fists to more profit-
able use than work. As a matter of fact, Bill occupied
one of the top notches of the aristocracy of labor, and his
earnings in the pit exceeded those of many a professional
man. Expert coal-miners are always well paid, and
Bill stood out as one of the most hefty hewers
in the Newcastle district. When the Pelaw Main wasn’t
idle through strikes, Bill was one of the cracks who could
knock out 15s. to £1 a shift. Before he took on coal-
mining he was certainly working- for a railway contractor
at ss. a day, but even then -he was worth a lot more.
The only thing that Squires takes a pride in is the work
he has done. Pugilism is a small thing to him. It is
har i to get him to talk about his career as a
boxer, but there’s no stopping him if the conversa-
tion is turned to the quantity of coal that can be
heaved out in a given time. The suggestion to run Bill
as a Labor candidate for the Senate next time isn’t
by a long way the subject for amusement that many
imagine. He is a hard-headed, sensible man, and stands
as much above the average pug mentally as he does
physically. Personally, he is more presentable than half
the sitting members, Labor and Anti-Sosh alike, and he
has been a unionist and a Laborite ever since he knew
how. Bill certainly can’t talk, but that wouldn’t be a
drawback in a place that wastes whole years in conversa-
tion. He would gain, in addition to the direct Labor
vote, a solid bush vote. A proportion of rouseabouts,
and milkers, and rabbiters may be apathetic about
Labor, but they would walk 10 miles to the booth to vote
for Squires. If Bill returns from America with the
championship, he ought to be Senator Squires in three
years.
John Young, of Sydney, the “father of
bowls,” is dead, to the regret of all who knew
him, at the ripe age of 80 years. He was born at
Eootscray, in Kent (England), a rural spot, which
presents no resemblance to sprawling, busy, un-
kempt, bone-mill-haunted Footscray in Victoria,
where they are always boiling down something
aged and horrible. He was a draughtsman at the
construction of the Crystal Palace (London), and
also did work in New York, Paris, and elsewhere,
before coming to Australia and setting up as a
contractor. His work here was immense and
varied, and included St. Patrick’s Ca hedral,
Melbourne"; Ballarat Gaol, Sargood’s store, the
Congregational Church in Collins and Russell
streets, Melbourne, and many other churches and
villas. In 1859 he commenced St. John’s College,
Sydney, and built St. Mary’s Cathedral, Hobart,
and the Cargill monument in Dunedin. In 1866
he came to Sydney to reside permanently, and
commenced St. Mary’s Cathedral. He built the
General Post Office, and the 1870 Exhibition Build-
ing at Redfern. He also built the old Redfern
railway station, Farmer and Oo.’sshop, Pitt-street;
Dalton Bros.’ premises, in Pitt-street; the Lands
Office, in Bridge-street; and the Garden-Palace.
Mr. Young is credited with having given Henry
Parkes one of his Kent villas, at Annandale, rent
free, in the latter years of his life, and certainly if
it wasn’t given rent free, the rent was never col-
lected, for in those days Parkes was penniless.
Parkes named the house Kenilworth, and died
there. Mr. Young was Mayor of Sydney in 18S6,
and had the credit of having introduced wood-
block roadways to the city. He made several
attempts to enter Parliament, but he had no very
definite politics, and the electors signified their
feelings in the usual manner.

William Farmer, the head of Australia’s
well-known softgoods firm, now visiting Sydney,
has been many years “Mr. Sheriff” of the
Corporation of the City of London. London
sheriffs are personages. It is Mr. Sheriff’s
duty to present petitions of the City rate-
payers to the House of Commons, and
to attend at the Bar of the House and
address Mr. Speaker. He is a return-
ing-officer at Parliamentary elections,
and it is his custom, at each of the twelve
annual sittings of the Central Criminal
Court, to entertain the Judges and the
Bar at dinner or lunch. Sheriff Cowper,
of Sydney, used to do some of these
things, as did his predecessors, but of
late the hospitality of the Sheriff has
dropped off.
“ Quan” : The Bulletin has said
of ex-Mayor Crase, of Brisbane, the de-
served term of reproach so often that I
want to put in a good word for him. He’s
the father of an Australian-born family of
16. He, Opposition leader Philp, Queens-
land Chairman of Committees Jackson, and
J. D. Campbell, M.L.A., the chief speakers at
the A.N.A.’s annual dinner in Brisbane the
other day, count 40 Australian-born children
arnone- them.
Joe Charles, the man who gave “Tatter-
sail’s” sweeps the stiffest run they ever had,
died in Melbourne the other day. Charles went
across to Westralia from Sydney when Cool-
gardie was booming, and started a land agency.
Some of his specs, turned out well, so he then
started his “consultations,” appealing to Wes-
tralians to keep the money in their own State.
They were apparently honestly conducted, and
soon became popular. Then Charles waxed
wealthy and ambitious. Leaving Jim Gardiner
in charge of the cheque book, he went up to
Shanghai, and started Melbourne Cup and other
consultations there —his circulars, attractively
printed in Chinese on imitation bank-notes, were
distributed by hundreds of thousands. But the
Chow did not bite, and, after several months of
hard battling, Charles returned to Perth. His
representative, during his absence, went in for
politics, became M.L. A. for Albany, and subse-
quently State Treasurer. Charles became, in the
meantime, involved in various land transactions;
his consultations got besmirched, and he was
compelled to abandon them. At one time he was
a shareholder in Perth Sunday Times, and it is
believed he had something to do with the famous
article, that was never published, denouncing
Judge Parker (now Chief Justice).
Superintendent Brennan, who has just
retired from N.S.W. police force, tells a tale
about the historic medals that Slippery Charley
Cowper struck to commemorate the heroism dis-
played in crushing the bushrangers of the ’6o’s
and ’7o’s. Cowper, then Premier, announced that
he would have gold and silver medals struck at
the Mint for presentation to those who had
signally distinguished themselves. Five years
later the medals were distributed, but the gold
ones were presented to Cowper’s political friends,
while the cheap, silver objects went to the police.
Even Walker, who brought down Thunderbolt,
only received about 2s. worth of silver decoration.
AMERICA IN FUTURE.
“ Ancestor?”
“ No. That’s my great-grandmother Seraphina
Jamieson’s pet society pig Mary.”
Tom Hughes, Sydney’s Lord Mayor, was
born in a house of many fortunes in Elizabeth-
street, where now the plague rat is industriously
chased for 4d. a head. He was early sent to
England, and later picked up law wrinkles from
Tom Slattery and Louis Heydon.
Premier Price, the stonemason, is surpris-
ing S. Australia. Even his friends took him
for a Welsh “ local preacher,” who could
say things, but needn’t be expected to do
anything big. But, besides doing some good
work in an administrative way, he has com-
pleted .the Adelaide Tramway purchase, which
had been dragging on for 10 years, has done
something definite with the Northern Terri-
tory, and has settled the Murray River trouble if
Carruthers can be got in a reasonable frame of
mind. Yet he is absolutely no “diplomatist,”
and he says apologetically, “ I was never much
good at details.” But that is just where he
seores. He takes in the big aspects of a big
question, and doesn’t pursue the trifles with a
microscope. That sort of man often makes what
looks like a bad bargain; but the other sort of
man makes none, and, in the end, his successors
have to make far worse terms than Price gets.
A VAIN TASK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The comet came, a fiery curse
Directed at the universe,
And love, and hate, and hope, and mirth
Were dead upon the quaking earth.
For each man knew that all was done,
The world’s long, weary course was run,
And soon the whole must he destroyed,
Earth’s fragments scattered in the void.
One man alone with heat and weight
Did scream against the law of Fate.
’Twas Judkins on a mountain set
Beneath his ti-tree coronet.
“ Destroy the world,” he cried, “ what spite 1
And just as 1 had set it right! ”
VIC.
SILAS SNELL.
THE FATHER OF BOWLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
vic.
Surely the genial game of Bowls
Is good for bodies as for souls.
His life is long, his smile serene
Who bends the knee upon the Green,
And raising Heavenward his eyes,
Takes whisky with his exercise.
If aged men indeed be old,
It is because they haven’t bowled.
In New South Wales the moneyed game
Won honor through its parent’s name :
For eighty years the sun had shone —
A bowl of light—on Father John
Before his parting knell was rung—
And then good Father John died Young.

“ Petramos ” : When I was a callow clerk
in a house agent’s office I was acquainted with a
bailiff named Bill. Bill had red whiskers grow-
ing wild on his face, and I’m certain that they
had a deal to do with his great success in life.
There was something hypnotic about Bill’s red
whiskers. They frightened and fascinated. I
have seen him enter a house and, after taking up
the passage carpet, calmly order the tenant to
bring him a cup of tea into the drawing-room.
Bill always got the tea. He also got it in the
drawing-room if there was one. I have seen his
red whiskers sweep against the fair young com-
plexion of Miss Tottie Twolegs as he informed
Madame Twolegs that the “ gent for whom ’e was
actin’” thought Tottie wasn’t religious enough to
live in one of his houses. Even when a large,
villainous-looking gentleman loomed suddenly
into view, scowled heavily, and asked Bill what
he meant by coming into the Twolegs house with-
out Tottie’s express invitation, Bill merely
pointed his red whiskers at the new.comer’s eye
and bade him lend a hand at shifting the har-
monium into the street. When Tottie wept, Bill
brushed her with his red whiskers again, and
asked her to join his Wednesday evening Bible
class. Bill was marvellously successful. I don’t
think any other bailiff—even one with green
whiskers—could have done better.

RAI INCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 6, 1907.
My Dear Arini.—
The weather is a scallywag ! Their Federal
Excellencies the Northcotes, if it were not for the
weather, would now be staying at “Hill View,”
the Moss Vale residence of the State Governor.
But Saturday afternoon was a certain hail-storm’s
day out. And it broke 127 windows of “ Hill
View,” and flowed over the rooms that the North-
cotes were to inhabit. Hail-rocks (’twould be
cowardice to call ’em hail stones) also killed
cattle and scattered fruit, and did other damage.
Mr. John Young, whose name is rarely men-
tioned without the affectionate sub-title “Father
of Bowls,” died last week at his beflowered and
foliaged home, “Kentville,” Annandale. Mr.
Young was 80 years of age. He was a successful
builder and contractor, and churches and other
important buildings were erected by him in this
city, in Hobart, and Maoriland. In his Euro-
pean and Asiatic travels he collected many good
or curious works of art. He and Mrs. Young
were great entertainers, and all visiting bowlers
and local players got the glad hand and a welcome
to their dinners and garden parties. Mrs. Young
and two sons and a daughter survive him.
A great contingent of Sydneysiders left these
smiling if moist shores, last Wednesday, by the
Marmora. The W. P. Martins (she is one of the
smartest of dressers), a quartette of Knoxes, the
Gerald Brownes (he is married to the daughter of a
former Governor of Westralia), Mr. and Mrs.
Waterhouse (daughter and son-in-law of the late
Verv Rich Man, Eb. Vickery), and the Hamilton
Gordons and the Comtesse de Meirelles. Last is
a sister of Mrs. Gordon, and a Portuguese. She is
a Hebraic-looking young party, who puts on no
side, but how. a section of Sydney loved to
have her at its afternoon teas and ask her, in a
raised voice, “Two lumps, Countess?” “ Cream,
Countess, or milk?” The “Countess” word
wasn’t allowed to rust in penny trams or Pointer
drawing-rooms. One glory ahead of the Marmora
passengers: the Duke and Duchess of Connaught
and Princess Patricia will join at Colombo.
Miss Tittell Brune has forwarded a cheque for
two guineas to the hon. sec. of the Child’s Study
Association. Miss Margery Mac Masters and
Mr. Arthur Mason also send cheques for the like
sum, and a hidden donor forwards ss.
Miss Elbert-Orton, one of the two leading ladies
towed here by Julius Knight, is a fine example of
middle-aged beauty. Her dressing is knocking
the Hotel Australia flat. She is rich, very rich,
and a widow, and the stage is her distraction.
She has adorned it for a few years only. Imagine
taking up hard labor by way of distraction, when
you might simply have the light job of being a
rich widow!
Julius himself stumbles into Jean’s for the pur-
pose of making floral the buttonhole of his grey
coat. “Grey clothes and an asp-green tie,”
reciteth the chronicler who mostly wears a river
hat and the muslin frock of the ingenue, “and he
chose a fat pink"; bud, and •wouldn’t have any '
maiden-hair around it.” Doesn’t the fierce light
beat upon a mummer as well as upon a throne ?
Last Wednesday, by the Jap liner, went the
J. R. Woods of Newcastle (the Essie Jenyns
Woods) and their children. They have taken
their motor with them, and intend to motor
through Mikado-land.
Mr. Owen Cox returned from South Australia,
Melbourne, and other foreign and hostile parts
last week. I’m sick of mentioning the go-aways
—it’s only fair to show that someone has had the
patriotism to come back.
That all-conquering widow, Mrs.- J. T. Toohey,
and her step-flock, were at Bowral recently, spin-
ning through the- landscape in the. thousand-
guinea motor they brought out with them
recently. Mr. Maurice Toohey, who is in better
health than the kind he hasn’t enjoyed lately,
was to go to Tasmania to escape this hot-wet-
blanket weather.
Miss Marion Poritt, a Victorian singer with a
reputation for warbling Scotch songs, has been in
Sydney for a few days. Highland Society gave a
small, evening sing-song to introduce her to
Itself. Miss Poritt responded with some Cale-
donian airs, and all the members declared that if
she didn’t come over again to set the Thistle to
song in a bigger hall for the benefit of the public,
they’d have to go down to Melbourne to hear her.
Miss Marion Grey, of the new J.K. Co., was at
the last night of the Tittell Brune show, and
proved to be, in appearance, a very slender, very
tall creation, with blue eyes and a splendid head
of dark-brown hair that brightened to russet
wherever the light caught its coils. She is the
original of our Gallery picture, “Reverie,” by, I
think , F. Dicksee. Her face is a Burne-Jones
one, and has a kind of appealing thinness, but
also what the old hypnotiser said of Trilby’s
applies—it has “ beautiful bones.” She ought to
look a peach on the stage. She is a cousin of the
Fenners, of Melbourne, and of Mrs. Percy Chirn-
side. Miss Grey will make her first show here in
“ Raffles,” the next piece.
What a rogue night was that of last Saturday !
The rain was warm and the air was warmer, and"
when Sydney came in out of the wet to welcome
back Julius Knight at Her Majesty’s, the big
theatre was warm like an Ottoman wash. The
galleiy was so packed that somehow the crowd
suggested the work of a hydraulic press The
circle, also packed, looked shabby, but Lent and
the weather would have explained things if it had
been in rags. Stalls and boxes looked a little
smarter than the circle. Perhaps the stallites
and the boxers put Lent and the weather where
they are supposed to put Satan—behind them,
lne circle takes an absorbed interest in the
hairdressing of the stalls. It-the dressing of
the intellectual attic—is so much in evidence
mu i? S - e a ?°, ve at t(ie interest is not surprising,
-he hair of the woman who has “just come back”
is always of interest—parted in the middle, rather
flat at the sides over the ears, then outspread far
and wide, and built up on top with coils, or curls,
or both, and ornamented with millinery, creatures
from the Zoo, stuffed specimens from an aviary,
flowers, foliage or fruit. A damsel in Saturday’s
audience had a wreath of cherries and blossoms.
Another wore a great drooping osprey—the “ re-
versed osprey ” we see so much about in foreign
fashion journals. It has a most dejected expres-
sion, like a wet flag at half-mast, or the trailing
tail of a drenched fowl. But the lady with the
long, thin ostrich feather, its spine gilded and
dotted with tiny paste jewels, was very up-to-
the-minute. The stem was planted above her
left ear, and the feather journeyed, with devia-
tions, across and down the back of her head till
its tip tickled her ivory right shoulder.
A great outburst of green on the stage—Sher-
wood Forest all green, Robin Hood and his merrie
men all in Lincoln green ! Mrs. Geoff. Fairfax,
in the stalls, glittering as to ornaments in the
tone of green beetles’ wings ! The Burdekins,
back from mummied Egypt, made a white boxful.
A boneless Kelso King girl helped to fill another.
(You must not call a plump girl by that adjective
any more; you must call her boneless.) The
Lachlan Beatons and Miss Ruby Adams were a
blueand white-and-silverboxful, trimmed with vin-
dictive-lookingwhitenerinelilies. Mrs. Williamson
and Mrs. C. Deane’s pen wore the strictly-correct
Lenten sombreness. Mayoress Hughes, one of
the David Anderson dazzlers, and in fact most of
the first-nighters, were present.
After the Play.
Time : 11.15 ot first night of “ Robin Hood.” Place
Cloak-room of Her Majesty’s Theatre (Sydney). Persons :
Some sixty women hurrying into their theatre coats, and
all talking at once.
First Coat-Hunter : “ How did you like it ? ”
Second Ditto : “ Rather dull. Julius Knight’s legs are
thinner.”
First Coat-Hunter : “ Well, if they get fatter the
play will get brighter. Eh ? ” (She rushes out.)
Third Coat-Hunter : “ I can’t make up my mind about
the new woman, Elbert-Orton. She’s got a look—full face,
you know—of Mary Anderson and Maud Jeffries and
Edith Crane and Mabel Lane, and yet she isn’t what you’d
call beautiful.”
Superior Coat-Huntf.r (as she flees to the door after
collecting a mackintosh): “ All those had rather classic
faces; Miss Elbert What’s-her-name has classic lines in
her facial outline. Can’t say I care for them out of
marble.”
Large-sized Coat-Hunter (opposite mirror) : “The new
lady would make a splendid Lady Macbeth. (Takes
diamond combs out of her toupie and puts them in hand
bag.) A simply perfect Lady Macbeth. She’s got the
right voice and prizefighter jaw and figure and tragic
intensity. But she isn’t Maid Marian—not to my think-
ing.” (Gathers up her skirts and dives for the door).
Breathless Young Coat-Hunter (pinning motor-cap
on her outspread hair); “I’m certain Melba Horton —
isn’t that her name? No, I mean Elbert Morton, of
co,urse —well, she’s played Rosalind in “ As You Like It.”
When she came on in that doublet and hose she was so
coy and arch, and yet she made all the running—just like
all Shaky’s heroines.”
Hunter, who has put her Coat on, and is Waiting
for a Friend : “ I’m sure Julius Knight is a nice- minded
man. The way he kept Maid Marian’s shoe well-covered
against his heart in both hands, when he found it in the
forest —for it wasn’t a number three, not by several
inches 1 ”
Very Tall Coat-Hunter (snappishly): “ Well, she
isn’t a number three figure- She must be five feet ten
—and her legs are lovely.” ■ . -
Little Old Lady Coat-Hunter (in a whisper to second
ditto) : “ That is the second reference to legs in the same
minute. Young people to-day are—are—very —legumin-
ous.”
Brunette Coat-Hunter (butting her way through the
throng): “ Don’t care for the piece at all. I expected
King John to have Jews’ teeth drawn at least in one of
the acts. He always did that, you know, when he wanted
money.”
Languid and Drooping Coat-Hunter : “ Yes, they ought
to have something like that to make excitement. Per-
haps they will put it in later if the piece doesn’t take—or
an earthquake. I liked the earthquake in ‘ Parsifal ’ a
lot better than Tittell Brune.”
Debutante Coat-Hunter (winding a gauze scarf around
her hair) : “They say Tittell Brune has three children.”
Middle-Aged Coat-Struggler (apparently her mother):
“ Well, and if she has, it would be quite respectable.”
Stout Coat-Burster (stooping with difficulty to put on
goloshes): “O, they are always saying that about
actresses. Why, everyone who takes a trip to California
comes back and says that she saw the seven children of
that widow—you know the pretty widow actress—l
forget her name. Seven children, and all working.”
Belated Coat-Finder: “You should have seen this
room last night. Tittell Brune’s last night, you know.
Millions of hats ! ” (She waves an over-whitened arm
towards the four walls and the ceiling, and speaks
through teeth that hold a hat-pin with a ruby-eyed cat’s
head.) All the girls who adore Tittell Brune wear sailor-
hats with ribbon bands, or black, glazed calico picture
hats, with plumes that never grew on any bird that ever
was on land or sea.”
Voices of Departing Coat-Tails : “Doesn’t Plimmer
look ripping in that Crusader’s get-up ? ” . . . “Plimmer
—oh, yes, you’l' see all about him in that lovely book—a
little blue, al; le same—‘Richard Yea-and-Nay.’” .. .
“ I mean about the man he is on the stage, you know,
Richard Coeur de Lion.” . . . “ Wasn’t the forest lovely?”
. . . “Maid Marian’s riding clothes?” . . . “Oh, yes 1
royal blue and emerald green.” . . . “ Perfectly, sweet
contrast.” . . . “Sherwood Forest! Yes, it’s in the
histories.” . . . “ He’s made Earl of Huntingdon
in the last act.” .. . “Really!” .. . “Why, one
of the Wilson girls, of Ercildoune station, is married
to the present Earl of Huntingdon.” . . . “How abso-
lutely ripping to be married to a grandson—no, I suppose
much later than that —of Robin Hood ! ” . . . “Coffee !”
. . . “ Good night! ”
Mr. O. A. Waters, who lately married Miss
Rose Glenister, was given, in honor of the event,
a dinner at the Australia by some fellow-members
of the Prince Alfred Yacht Club. Yachts sailing
on seas of ices were handed round, whilst the
musicians played the “Sailing, Sailing” waltz.
That’s “featuring” a clubbies’ membership,
isn’t it?

Miss Dorothy Sidney, one of the company
roped in by Julius Knight, is much prettier
off the boards than on them. Also she
has achieved what I thought was impossible
in a woman with a thin, long, narrow face,
and a bony little aquiline nose—to coquette
gracefully, and be arclily yet naturally demure.
No doubt flirting in the twelfth century was done
very much as it is to-day, only the flirter dressed
differently, and said things, such as. “Wouldst
kiss me, varlet!” And the varlet said he would.
Miss Fanny Bauer, the Sydney warbler, has
been singing in Ireland and getting much eulogy,
and let us hope, some gold. She certainly had a
beautiful voice when she left here. The Galway
Observer speaks of her as “ a superior artist of
the first magnitude.” How’s that for high? as
the Yanks say.
Papers coming from English parts, and con-
taining accounts pf the Thaw trial, are enough to
give one a bad fit of disgust. Said accounts seem
to be wired across from America, but not once
does the enraptured newspaper man mention
Mrs. Thaw without prefixing “ beautiful ” to her
name. “Notorious” would be an equally
serviceable adjective, and would break the
monotony. And the murderer is carefully
and respectfully “Mistered” and “million-
aired.” That picture, “The Eternal Ques-
tion,” to be seen amongst the Dana Gibson
series at Angus and Robertson’s, is a profile study
of Mrs. Thaw, or as she was then, the artists’
model, Miss Nesbitt. It is not the Gibson girl—
simply the head, and one long strand of
hair curled at the tip to look like a note of in-
terrogation. The young party has lived with a
large L. She is even now only 21. She started
her “artistic” and stage career at 15, and
has done a lot of travel, been a co-
respondent, got her name tagged with that
of several gay dogs, and finally married a
plain dull dog and saw him shoot one of the
festive dogs, who is now the dead dog that is a
great deal worse than a live lion. So she has had
what you might call a crowded hour of inglorious
life. Murdered man Stanford White was a son
of Richard Grant White, the critic. Australians
who kept track of American literature may
remember the real distinction of his writing.
Voting for the lady to be crowned Queen of
Wonderland City is going on like a fire in dry
reeds; 1000 votes were polled last night, and
Mrs. Bland Holt is leading easily. But there’s a
“dark ” lady (dark in the sense that a horse may
be) who is expected to out-distance her when a
couple of thousand more voters get on to the
ballot
Grade Grahame, the pretty little white-faced
serio with the honev-colored hair, is the most
magnetic young bird the Tivoli has had this
many a year, in my slightly humble opinion.
Her “Waiting at the Church” seems to be the
favorite vocal lure. G.G. is the desperate young
party who, some years ago, when she was a be-
ginner at a London music hall, attempted suicide
by drowning because her one good, very own song
was stolen from her. She could not get justice
or an injunction, and the wrong preyed on her.
However, she was fished out and “first-aided,”
and the attempted tragedy ended in her getting
her song back and a rise of £2O on her salary.
Miss Rawson, vice-president for this State
the Aus. Ex. of Women’s Work, held a big meet-
ing at the Town Hall yesterday, and explained
the objects of the Aus. Ex., and the progress to
date. About 200 women were present, and a
large number of committees for the different
suburbs were formed. Mrs. Erancis Suttor, Mrs.
T. H. Kelly, and Mayoress Hughes were on the
platform with Miss Rawson.
German liner Bremen took, amongst hundreds
of other passengers. Colonel and Mrs. E. B.
Freehill, and Mrs. Walter Elkington and her
small son. Quite a new phase of leave-taking
was inaugurated by a few of the passengers. As
the vessel drew away from the wharf they
cast on shore reels of colored ribbon, retaining
one end. Their friends held the shore end,
unrolling the silk sometimes to the length of 30
yards. It was a case of pull friend, pull liner,
and presently the ribbons snapped. But it’s a
point of sentiment for the one who is going and
the one who is left behind each to retain his or
her end of the silk. It’s a popular way of saying
au revoir in America.
“ Victorienne ” writes from Perth (W.A.): —
What shall I say of this sleepy Western State ? That it
slumbers as usual ? Perhaps I am spoilt, coming from
the East ; but that the Western folks can be
proud of Perth surprises me. Business, you are
told, is almost at a standstill for want of enter-
prise. Saturday morning I went to town, and found
the place overflowing chiefly with loafers, hanging
on to the nearest pub, waiting for invitations to drink.
I only had two places to go to—one was said to be the
“swell” shop in the grocery line here. Well, I walked in,
sat down, and, after waiting a few minutes, a man
lounged up. I told him what I required, but whilst he
was attending me a much-befrilled dame entered. The
man immediately left me, and proceeded to take orders
from the befrilled personage. There was not even a
small boy to look after my wants; I was just a mere
something that didn’t matter. I went to another shop
for a small item which can be got at any place in Mel-
bourne. The girl looked at me in derision and turned up
her nose. “We don’t keep it; never heard of it before.”
Her pride in her ignorance was typically Perthian. What
the inhabitants of this township don’t know is not worth
thinking about, they presume.
One shining light of the Wesleyan ministry, who origi-
nally belonged to an eastern State, found preaching
didn’t pay whenaman had a large family of small Wesleyans
to rear. So he came to this State and took up an hotel,
much to the horror of his brethren and followers, who
forthwith shut their doors upon him. The worst of it is
he was a temperance preacher! Anyway, he is pros-
pering now, and his pubbery promises to be second to
none hereabouts. The Wesleyan ex-parson is the liveliest
man in the trade.
His Ex. the Governor paid a flying visit to Perth last
Monday, when he was met by the new commandanc
and staff officer. The Com. is a thin, military-looking
Johnny, disguised in an eye-glass. Rumor has it that he
intends to shake things up at the next Easter encamp-
ment. Perhaps his e.ve-glass misrepresents him.
Another naval engagement Mr. Miller, the
Admiral’s private secretary, is engaged to a
daughter of the Eric Mackys, a connection of the
Knox family.
I note that Miss Eleanor Manning, of
“Truganini,” Point Piper, was one of the first
five Sydney subscribers to send in her name and
sub. for The Lone Hand. So she gets the
publication free for life. The poor lady can’t read
or write, and has only two teeth. But she has
eyes like pansies and her hair promises curliness,
which latter item alone is something bigger than
a small mercy. Miss Eleanor is nearly a year
old, and is the infant of the Harry Mannings,
and granddaughter of W.P. of that name. Her
mama is the youngest child of the late Chief
Justice Martin. Eleanor is the youngest sub-
scriber for certain to the solitary-fisted paper,
and if her proud young parents don’t keep all
the back numbers and hand them over to her
when she’s of magazine-reading age—say twelve
years—they’ll be guilty of cruelty to curly heads.
My Brisbane correspondent writes
The ladies’ pair-oar contest took place on Saturday
afternoon, with splendid weather, and a couple of
hundred male and female enthusiasts to set off the
event. However, as the river is about choked with
water hyacinth, the struggle evolved itself into a sort of
obstacle race. But how those girls did buck in ! The
club entertained its 200 guests with tea, cool drinks and
doleful music under a marquee, on the river bank.
The Brisbane River has been a mass of water hyacinths
for the past week. Solid acres of the pest have floated
down from the upper reaches of both the Brisbane and
the Bremer. Coal barges and lighters have had to wait
until the tide and the floating heaps of vegetation chose to
go their way ; to fight through the hostile vegetable was
impossible. On February 28 the ferry was held up. Also,
the Humpybong Co.’s excursion steamer Emerald was
arrested below Kangaroo Point, and the passengers were
profane and foodless until the tide turned. Later the
same evening an attempt to run a down river excursion
ended in dismal failure, and the trippers had their
shillings returned to them. Year by year there is talk
of eradicating the pest, and year by year nothing is done ;
and each succeeding wet season sees a bigger lot of
hyacinth drifting down. It’s all very well to say that the
salt water kills the plant. It undoubtedly does, but it
takes three months to accomplish the work, and a lot of
anger and profanity can happen in three months.
From England comes the news that Mrs. D. T. Seymour
has been the victim of a very bad chest attack since her
arrival among the fogs, but the sufferer is now able to
resume her bridge blouse once more, and to hold up the
Australian end of that incurable vice.
Notices are out for a meeting at Hawstead to further
the interests of the Free Kindergarten, to be run in con-
nection with the Institution of Social Service, promoted
by one L. L. Wirt (sometime of Newcastle, N.S.W.). Miss
Cooper will dispense tea and food to those philanthropic
souls who are prepared to support the good cause.
The William Beits, of Ascot, Toowoomba, having
dumped their family down with their grandmother, have
packed their belongings, and departed for London by the
Marmora. Little Beit, as indefatigable a collector of
clothes as the historical Mr. Toots, is expected to return
with many samples of the newest thing in vests, and to
make Queensland totter on its basis with his tie.
Col. Lyster has returned from his Maoriland furlough
to-day. Daughter Phoebe lingers longer in the south.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 4, 1907.
Mr Dear Yarrie,— .
The rally, at Lady Northcote s bidding, to a
conference on the approaching Exhibition of
Women's Work (meaning feminine industry in
all its non-menial branches, save character-wreck-
ing and money-making at Bridge) was a large-
sized one. All the dames of great social tonnage
transpired in their most frivolous apparel. _ Dame
Northcote chaired the meeting in reminiscent
mauve. Owing to dog-bite she wore a surgical
trimming of white silk on her left hand. The right
member was gloved —to keep the Busy Bees off
the grass in the aesthetic sense. Dame lalbot, in
leopard stripes of black and white silk, sat on her
whole-hand side. The Cliveden Dowager was
ensconced on t’other. Mrs. Landale, Mrs. Eel.
Miller, Mrs. Urquhart were a trio most adjacent
to the starring Exes.
This She-conference was a day of urbane com-
pliments and gentle flatteries. The Stoningrton
Ex. uirfolded her prim mind, shook out the
thought draperies awhile, and flung a gossamer
of blarney over her comments. Her rigidity
softened into warm, frank cordiality. Her
eulogy of the Federal Ex. (who bobbed and
smiled at every honeyed allusion) was spirited.
Then she commended the Dowager Clarke;
thirdly, Dame Madden ; and, finally, her compli-
ments spread indefinitely to the working bees,
who could scarcely restrain themselves from
fluttering forward, and sobbing their gratitude
on her stately shoulder. When the President
read the promises of half the world’s princesses
and highnesses to help the exhibition, the con-
ference nearly choked with emotion.
The Chief Judicial dame is looking haggard.
Sickness and the turmoil of travel have left her a
very wan and pallid person. Those who fare-
welled her at Port Melbourne, early in 1906, de-
clared that she looked then as young and crisp
and lovely as her daughters. Now she’s looking
like their grandmother, and medical prescriptions
are part of her day’s programme.
It has become a mark of distinction not to fly
pennants and Union Jacks and the Tricolor from
one’s top-mast of straw. Every Mary Ann has
streamers of scarlet, red flame, or washing blue,
and Claudia is at last compelled to veto the hat-
tail. The neat chip that banks all its material on
the crown or thereabouts is now the toniest of
thatch decorations. The effect is almost old-
maidish in its orderly tail-less state.
The white lounge hat made of felt—the woman’s
nearest approach to the broad-rimmed cricketing
hat of mankind—has become so voguey in Mel-
bourne, that the Austral Mills are now helping to
supply the demand for "‘lady lounge” headgear.
The light and sporty-looking thing is essentially
Australian in its adaptable character, and
promises to be the forerunner of many styles. A
felt hat can lounge in various poses, and the
opportunity for a catchy title is very obvious. r l he
latest Anderson Australian drama suggests a good
business name for woman’s crowning glory—
which is her hat. Why not “The Squatter’s
Daughter ? ”
A correspondent tells a story of the Ballarat
theatre what time lovely Julius was appearing as
Robin Hood. It was at a matinee, and a young
bride was having an afternoon off with two girl
friends. Suddenly the situation became quite
too romantic for the bride. She clutched her
friend’s arm and said, pathetically, “Oh, I do
want my Billy!” Sweet are the uses of an
heroic actor. He can inspire a newly-married
girl with a yearning for her Billy.
In Germany, there is a custom amongst pro-
fessional singers, actors, actresses, and others
of getting up early in the morning and walking
in the dew. From 5 to 6 a.m. is supposed to be
the best time for dew-trotting. Some of the
members of Musgrove’s Grand Opera co. have
the habit. For the next few weeks, therefore,
sundry slumberous policemen will be startled
into consciousness by snatches of “Lohengrin”
disturbing the silly air at dawn. This news
should prove invaluable to the man who misses
his last train and tries to sneak home with the
milk in the morning. He can now look the old
wife in the face and tell her lie has been out all
night improving his voice with the gentle dew
from heaven.
The comet scare, for which the Herald is
mainly responsible, so far as Melbourne is con-
cerned, has provided me with two glimpses of the
female mind. I heard an Albert Park citizen
reading the thrilling particulars to his wife in a
tram. She was horrified, her face paled, her eyes
rounded with apprehension. “My gracious!”
she exclaimed, “ and I’ve only worn my new blue
hat five times ! ” The other glimpse was provided
by a bunch of matrons in a cl rawing-room. They
were discussing the comet over tea and biscuits.
Most of them were very anxious, and appre-
hended universal disaster. Then the eldest and
wisest chipped in. “ Now don’t you let it bother
you, dears,” she said. “ The thing doesn’t come
for a week or two, and Mr. Bent is bound to do
something.” Then confidence was restored.
Surgeon Thomas Fitzgerald and daughter Kath-
leen have just steamered for England. The
eldest daughter married into the navy, lives in
England, and is on speaking terms with countesses.
The youngest flower, Grace Fitzgerald, is spend-
ing her time during the family’s absence staying
with various and assorted friends. At present she
is at Port Arthur (Tasmania), where the early
settlers used to wear ironmongery when they
took their walks abroad.
Aide Lukin, that dandiest of shawl-carriers, is
on the wing again after a brief stay in Melbourne.
He cultivates the figure 8 waist, and a double
comma sort of moustache, and a Family Herald
air of the dashing Marmaduke. With a little
more encouragement from the Talbottles, the
gallant aide would have waxed gay, hut en-
couragement of that sort might as well have been
expected from an Egyptian mummy.
The mice got playing while the cat was away
at a certain Institute for Human Repairs. Ihe
lady who is in command shouldered her kit tor a
holiday, and left a kindly-hearted subordinate in
charge. Immediately tilings grew lively ana
flirtatious. Result: Two engagements betvveen
doctors and nurses. The matron was aghast on
her return to find how fast the march of events
had been—and rather disgusted. No young
people ever got engaged in that establishment
while her eye was on them. People who have
seen the eye say that they couldn't.
Golfers are interested in the threatened mar-
riage of Michael Scott—an English Johnnie with
a blue-blood prefix to his label. He has just
returned from a trip made, presumably, in an
effort to kill his affections with ship-biscuit, or
give them some other fearful blow. They were
adamant, and he returns to woo the young
charmer of his choice, an ex-chick of the chorus.
Mr. and Mrs. Jefferson, a couple of expert

Colonel Bridges is once more anchored here,

A star foot-lighter, who prides herself on her
shapely limbs, is constantly at the beauty-parlor
for toe-trimming. But she doesn t anticipate
baring her extremities for a r6le like I ruby, or
exposing them at all to a gaping audience. Her
feet are for private circulation only, and are
made beautiful for the bath-room and perhaps
occasional Aspendale paddling. Most of the sex
neglects its toes. Out of a host of nice girls in
my own chummy circle, there aren’t two who
have ever let the light of day or the electric glare
of publicity beat upon their bare feet. Small shoes
have cramped the one-time pretty “piggies that
went to market,” and they’ve grown twisted and
gnarled and corny through disregard. I have
known airy, fairy Lilians to expose sans blush
strips of leg and random ivory patches of the rest
of them, while they concealed their feet by a
horde of artful strategies.
Perhaps if we had an expert toe-trimmer the
sex would see that the extreme south of its con-
struction was better cared for. There is a, call
for the she-pedicurist. She should arise, take up
her bag of tools, and start round on a pilgrimage
to make the sex self-respecting as regards its ex-
treme base.
A local bride of some importance broke through
all romantic bounds quite recently and deserted
the day-dreaming & deux habits of the newly-
linked to steep herself in brisk correspondence.
On the third day of the moon she set smartly to
work and scribbled business-like notes (with a
dash of patronage) to all the tradespeople who
had labored to make her marriage a fine spec-
tacular event. Hubby, meantime, was left to
play patience, or to dream beneath a blue sky on
his own.
The christening mug has gone right out of
date. A bachelor friend—on whom the present-
giving role of godfather was last week thrust
tells me that he searched in vain for the mug of
his childhood. Jewellers offered him a case of
bridge-counters, a fancy flask and a miniature
razor, till informed that the infant was feminine.
Then they spread a high pile of debut trinkets and
gew-gaws on the counter. The bachelor asked
querulously if they hadn’t any infant-baubles.
“ We’re out of them,” was the reply. “ Ever since
babies went out of fashion, we’ve not been stock-
ing christening gifts.” The bachelor retorted that
they hadn’t wholly gone out, and one shopman
remarked that for the exceptional baby the more
grown-up present of, say, a fan or a walking stick
was preferred. So the disgusted bachelor bought
a jewelled hair-comb for the poor bald she-baby.
These jewellers are absurd. Infants do still occur
to some extent—l saw two last month—and the
christening mug should be stocked.
Marshall-Hall, of the music-school, while abroad
exhibits his old form—the form he was in when
he advised the pig-tailed petticoats who scraped
violins under his supervision, to read Tolstoi’s
“ Kreutzer Sonata.” He is again in the frame of
mind which produced “ Hymns, Ancient and
Wooden”; wherein naked naiads were pursued
through rocky metres by “eupidous knaves.” He
is himself again ! He has a column and a half
in the Aegus each week. The effect of his ebul-
litions amongst the sober cribs from foreign
journals and silly criticisms of polite literature,
suggests a satyr's gyrations at a suburban social.
He is in Berlin now, and writes pleonastically of
Shakspeare in Germany, obscure performers, and
famous musicians. Some excerpts from him :
“English actors are handicapped in the natural
presentation of a character, by a long disuse of
their finer intellectual faculties.” “Footlights
barbarous abominations ! ” On Kleisler, a German
fiddler: “The chords were as clear as stained
glass—the pianissimo were like soft, deep, car-
mine velvet.’’ A rondo in G was. 4 a. miracle.
And notes “flew through the air like wind-
impelled bubbles of sparkling sound.” And he
finishes up with the admission that he esteems
piano-thumping more immoral than wife-beating,
“ because one hurts many—the other, only one ! ”
A comedy is being played by a St. Kilda marm
with a whole bunch of spare daughters. Among
the family acquaintances is a desirable person,
who had a misfortune to his heart and has looked
rather lonely ever since. Mommer has him
mentally fixed as the kind of moneyed mate suit-
able for one of her girls. She doesn’t care which
one Neither do they. The whole trio of sisters
are having a go for him—quite frankly and
unashamed. A large circle of friends look on, and
a few grin. The only one who knows nothing of
the chase is the hunted object. He isn’t even
warned of his danger. Perhaps he will see this
and fly the country, or dig a moat round himself
to keep the enemy beyond proposing distance.
There’s a Western girl at the Grand just now
whose industry is spoken of with awe. Always
she stitches. Waiting for the lift, between bites
of canarv pudding, while the coffee cools, during
an after-dinner flirtation —all the time she stitches.
Men may come and women go but she sews on for
ever. And she’s fetching. Her eyes, which she
fixes on her flying fingers, are so lovely that the
bystander, and the silent lover, and the envious
cat of the neighborhood, hang about disconsolately
to catch their stray beams. It may be merely
coquetry (which takes all forms), but it looks well,
The Cream gathered thick at Flemington on
Saturday. Every marm for once hung on to her
mate', ihe brute sex has to negotiate and back
the dead bird with the Bridge winnings. It
wasn’t an autumn meeting in the clothes sense.
The legion of great frill came out in its hot-season
rig—Dame Northcote heading the Terrace bat-
talion in dove grey, with a surmounting of
pink to make the hue of peace a gladder
effect. Mrs. Claude Macdonald wraithed along
the Terrace like a graceful smoked cloud—done
brown. Mrs. J. Fisken took farewell of the scene
(she sails by the Marmora) in touching black so
that the Cream understood, if it had tears, Mrs.
Fisken expected them to be shed just then. Ihe
two Chief Judicial chicks footed the Terrace tiles
softly in quiet raiment—one in nut-brown, t’other
(the gem of great price) in thoughtful grey. The
Cliveden dowager stuck to her bit of lavender,
and her chick was clothed in reflective dove
tones.
Mrs. Oswald Watt landed along here for the
gee-gee rally. Her contribution to the accumu-
lated Frill was a fussy cream in the company of a
Romney hat, with a graceful lid, large enough to
accommodate a prayer-meeting of goblins.
A moneyed Toorak marm has jibbed at the idea
of her final chick falling into the paw of some
penniless noodle. She doesn’t see why, because
there’s a scarcity of Man on the Market, her
much-dowered girl should drop as a plum from a
tree into the possession of any spare Gussie who
happens to playfully agitate the precious branch.
Rather, she reckons, let the chick wither on the
virgin stalk. But the Miss isn’t thinking that
way. She’s had the burden of gold for several
fashionable seasons, and has had about enough of
its hard glitter. She’s dead sick of being grand
and golden—she wants to be just glad in a com-
mon comfortable way, like Mary Ann.
Skating is billed to start in a month or two
with a “Poster” carnival. Husbands should
insist on facing a frozen fall with the wives of
their bosoms. Last season, the promiscuous-like
way in which she-skaters clasped male hands (any
that were going), and went dreamily or slippingly
over the frozen floor led to much trouble. Any
mixed couple which found its skating step the
same shape, or its love of iced waltzing of the
same temperature, became attached definitely for
the season. Then hubby, from the place where
the hot pipes are laid, got hot with rage as he
looked on.
A Toorak family of considerable cash and
cheek has invested in a new motah-cah. Pre-
viously the only cah in the family was owned by
one of the sons, who usually took an-hour-and-a-
half to do the trip out from Melbourne. Even
nowadays, the Blash-Hanks don’t often take their
cah out of their own suburb, preferring to let the
neighbors know that they’ve acquired a vehicle
worth having. The chief feature about their
lavish display of motor goods is the number of
people they have clinging all over the cah in its
wild careerings through Toorak. The usual com-
plement is ten, mostly girls, with the overflow
hanging on the step and sitting on the gauges in
front. Some day there will be an accident, and
the Blash-Danks will be fined for cruelty to a
dumb cah that couldn’t say a word on its own
behalf.
The girl who is tired of waiting starts to grow a
very miserable air of staid dignity. I looked on
one of her the other night in a grand crush, and
her beauty, weighted down by excessive decorum,
bruised my regard. Actually bruised it. You
see the girl lias a comely rose-and-cream .beauty
that should be all dimples and bewitching scrolls.
Instead, it fathers an expression of ecclesiastic
coldness. Youth has no relationship with
solemnity, and even waning youth should make a
gentle effort to keep gravity out of range until
disappointment and failure come and crush the
spirit of all endeavor. With the beauty in ques-
tion, youth is fleeing more from severity of
thought than the harsh burden of the years.
“ Rosna ” writes from Adelaide: —
The wife of Senator Josiah Symon astonished the
natives, the other day, by sailing away to Britain, in the
Commonwealth, with her young son and daughter. She
is a quiet dame, with a number of quiet daughters, not in
the least addicted to chasing society and putting salt on
its hurried and elusive tail. The Symons have one of the
loveliest old places in the State, at Upper Sturt, where
they spend their days communing with Nature and doing
soulful things like that. They dwell in the hills up
against the stars all the year round, and don’t even pay
any attention to “ the season.”
The inhabitants of Norwood, a suburb that dreams of
the day when it shall become a city,, held an extensive
fete last week to raise the means to build cottage homes
for the aged, perishable, and impecunious. The affair
was engineered by the mayoress and some influential
committees, which spent a year manufacturing the stereo-
typed pin-cushion and the “ tidy ” that is guaranteed to
shriek on sight. Premier Price, backed up by the only
peerage of the neighborhood, Edwin Smith and his wife,
said the show was open, and the result proved that he
had guessed right first try. ...... „
Clvtie Hine, a youthful nightingale of Adelaide Con-
servatorium, has just grabbed a scholarship in the
warbling industry at Marshall Hall’s school of noise.
The maiden has a graceful presence, and a voice of great
promise, and her success in the next-door city i? regarded
as a whole ostrich farm in the humble sixpenny, hat of
our local institution. • n .
Herds of wild tennis players have been roving this
country vainlv endeavoring to pick laurels off the hedges.
The local players girded up their racquets and achieved
marvellous things ; and the whole girly element of Tram-
Horseville put on its helmet with a chiffon tail and went
forth io witness the deadly conflict. All this with the
temperature bobbing about in a manner suggestive of a
region never mentioned to polite Sassiety except twice on
Sunday. Miss Parr and Miss Payne, who have gathered
in most of the local tennis honors for some years, graced
the South Oss team as usual.
This State has followed its usual ridiculous habit of
choosing an already graduated student of the ’Varsity
for its Rhodes scholar. Latest victim is Dr. Ray, a
youthful medico just through his course. He has already
grabbed more scholarships than any other local student
of that profession which yearns to cut out everybody’s
appendix. His athletic record is also intense ; he has
played in St. Peter’s and ’Varsity matches of almost
every kind, and is reputed a champion footballer,
cricketer, tennis-player, rower, rifle-shot and runner. It
seems absurd to pour another scholarship on a man who
is already clothed with them as with a garment, and who
could make a tent out of his certificates, and retire from
public view inside his own laurels.
The statue habit grows on Adelaide. Having duly in-
stalled the image of Colonel Light where it can keep an
eye on King William-street, the committee began to look
round for another candidate, and fixed upon Captain
Sturt as the most deserving object of its attention. The
committee merely pushed the atmosphere with its hand,
and lo ! a first instalment of £5O appeared. This looks
as though we’ll have the whole statue before we know
where we are. But still Melbourne, by reason of its Joan
of Arc image, keeps ahead. What we want, to give us a
reputation for originality, is a statue of Tubal-Cain or
Rameses the Great—somebody really ancient and obscure
that no one else would think of.
Yours Affectionately,

The Sydney Harbor Trust, aided and
abetted by the N.S.W. Crown Solicitor, has had
a disastrous defeat in its appeal to the Full Court
against a decision that the Trust can’t recover
wharfage rates on certain locomotives imported
for the N.S.W. railways ; and there is a gorgeous
bill of costs. Why didn’t some authority stop
the fool proceeding? When one State depart-
ment sues another to the last extremity of the
law it is exactly the same to the public whichever
wins, and the public loses the costs to a dead
certainty m any case.
The S.A. Register quite unwittingly put
one of the arguments for the transfer of the
-Northern Territory to the Commonwealth very
plainly, and rather forcibly, the other day :
The advocates of the policy of transferring the Northern
territory to the Commonwealth should carefully weio-h
the fact that by greatly , increasing the responsibilities
devolving upon the Federation they will necessarily-
enhance its powers, financial and otherwise, and rela-
tively lessen the influence of the State Parliament.
That’s just it. And the power of the Common-
wealth can’t be enhanced too much. Also,' the
influence of all the State Parliaments can’t be
lessened too suddenly.

Premier Bent shows his limitations in con-
demning Engineer Thwaites’s big scheme for a
park and lake on the Yarra, above Kew. He
perceives only that it would be a source of
pleasure and “turns it down.” The practical
side of the project—the one that would give Mel-
bourne power enough to light itself electrically if
not to drive its trams and trains as well, besides
doing away with much of the existing smoke
nuisance; m a word, the natural power that is
cheap, clean, and easily transmitted—that part of
the scheme Bent dismisses with the remark that
“there are millions of tons of brown coal between
Wilhamstown and Werribee.” M-yes, and who
owns the brown coal ?
“ Syloo ” writes
Consider the farcical administration by the Public
Works Department of the Unused Roads and Water
frontages Act, passed by Vic. Parliament with a view of
deriving revenue from unused public roads and water
, The , Act came into force on Ist January,
1904 but so slowly did the Department proceed to gather
in the rents, that in the shire of Ripon not one penny
was collected in 1904, though the possible revenue from
that municipal district for the year was £6BO. That case
is .typical, and , as the area of Ripon is about 587 square
nnles, whilst the total area of the other 145 shires is
87,000 square miles, one is able to estimate roughly the
tens of thousands of pounds remaining unpaid for that
year. This is now absolutely uncollectable : no -laim
can be made, seeing that no demands were made or
licenses issued in 1904. Furthermore, one of the
provisions of the Act declares that the rent must
be paid in advance, yet for the two following years
(1905 and 1906) only £774 was collected in Ripon
°J a possible £1360. And the occupiers upon whom
the duty of payment falls are not struggling settlers
with whom the Department, might deal leniently ; in
seven cases out of ten they are the owners of 10,000 acres
and upwards of freehold land. The mismanagement of
the Department in failing to issue notices in 1904 is on a
par with the present unsystematic method of collecting
the rents Occupiers within a few miles of each other-
are treated differently. Some have not been required to
pay rent at all; some have been requested to pay from
Ist January, 1905; others have been notified that the
rent commenced in 1906. Under the present Act, one-
half of the moneys received from this source must he
expended by the local councils on roads and bridges but
any council that has made an attempt to obtain some of
this money from the Unused Roads and Water Frontages
Fund has found that the Department is even slowe/to
pay out than it is to collect. There is too much circum-
locution in connection with the Act. The local council
usually sends in the return of the roads and frontages •
the Department sometimes notifies the occupiers • occa’-
smnaly it collects the rents, and nowand then it parts
with them. It is all sometimes, occasionally, and now
and then, also once in a while.

Again we hear of Spruce Myth’s colored
friends. In a recent Perth (W.A.) Chinese
•gambling case, one of the prosecution’s Chow
witnesses got seven days because his evidence
coincide with what he had previously
tQld the police. The interpreter then explained
that this diversity arose through a fear of being
suddenly turned into cold meat. The witness
had told him, that morning, that 100 of Myth’s
local Chow friends had formed a sort of anti-
evidence society, and, as proof of strict bad faith,
had appointed ten of their number to slay any
Chow who gave tetate’s evidence in gambling
cases: These ten supports of Private Enter-
prise received 15s. per week, with a promise
that £3OO would be sent to their sorrowing widows
should, their prospective victims happen to get
the drop ’ first. If they were injured while
slaying witnesses, then the society would pay the
medical exes, and double the' wages of the
cripples till they recovered. And it is also
alleged that the same sort of organisation has
been formed in Sydney, alongside Smith’s own
door.
British motor-car building never had
Protection in its early days, like most of the indus-
tries that are pointed to as triumphs of Freetrade.
It has had the blessings of Cobden pouring
upon it from its infancy,
and according to all the
yarns of all the Freetraders,
it ought to be something
extra-vigorous, and capable
of knocking out the “arti-
ficial products ” from
the “ coddled fac-
tories” of Protec-
tionist countries
with one blast of its
strong breath. But,
as usual,
bump against the
beautiful theory;
last year imports of
foreign (French and
American) cars to Great
Britain were valued at
£4,371,000, an increase of
£1,948,000 in two years, while the exports of
-British cars amounted to only £820,000 an in-
crease of £499,000 in the same two years. So the
increase of the exports from Protectionist coun-
tries into Britain was four times greater than the
increase of the exports from Freetrade Britain to
the rest of the world.
Another large hole has been discovered in
the new Vic. Licensing Act—grocers’ licenses may
be granted 1 by any old licensing court, without
any poll of the ratepayers being taken. Which
proves over again what a great thing it is to have
a second Chamber to prevent hastv, ill-digested
legislation. It recalls the gag that~an office boy
is one boy, two office boys are half a boy, and
three otnce boys are no boy at all.
The threat of Birrell, Chief Secretary for
Ireland, to declare that rack-renting absentee, the
Marquis of Clanricarde, incompetent to manage
hits Irish estates, indicates one of the reserve
powers which are held by Parliament over peers in
Britain, but so little used as to be almost value-
less. Another power, but one rarely exercised, is
that which disqualifies a peer on the ground of
poverty George Neville, Duke of Bedford, was
so degraded by Act of Parliament in the reign of
Ldward IV. The degradation was on the ground
that a peer, the landlord of estates, would if
pressed by poverty, be.tempted to extort unfairly
high rents from his tenants to maintain his posi-
tion. r
“ Crade ” : Consider the descent of Pea-
cock! Turner left a strong party, which looked
as if it would remain in power in Victoria for a cen-
tury orso. Peacock took charge, and thepartyheard
his wild laugh in the Treasury, and melted to about
18 or 20 members. Just before the 1904 elections
the remnant handed over the rickety throne to
Maclunnon, and made Peacock dishwasher. The
change did no good, and the party came back a
dispirited coterie of 11. Peacock has moped
almost silently for three years ; and now, on the
eve of another election, Bent gives a sudden snap
the once-strong party is swallowed, and Peacock
is given a subordinate position in a one-man
Government. Poor Pea-rooster !
There is slaughter. As the first tangible
result of the N.S.W. Lands inquiry, 108 improve-
ment leases have been cancelled in a heap. There
are quaint old place names on the list—Quam-
bone, Mungerbumbone, Mumblebone, Coppymur-
rinn till, and the like. Among the men who held
the leases the most numerous person seems to be
l b —hh C. Body—who had 15 of them,
while F. E. Body had six. Most of the other
people on the list also had several; apparently when
a man commenced to drink improvement lense
he usually came back for more out of the same
bottle. The next step will be the appearance of
Body and co. before a board to show why they
should get their leases back again, and on that
matter they can’t do better than consult Mr.
.Nicholas Willis, whose knowledge of the intri-
cacies of the N.S.W. Lands Acts is as good as ever.
Brisbane Courier, which bitterly opposed
Kidston’s measure to burst up large estates, has
just discovered in the Buderim district that there
. ar . e 11 1 Bch landlords there, except the financial
institutions, whose properties make a disgraceful
comparison with those of the energetic farmers
around.” These locked-up properties are
jungles of lantana and Noogoora burr, just breeding
grounds for these pests. The Canadian system, of com-
pellintf financial institutions to sell their foreclosed pro-
perties to the highest bidder within a limited period,
would apply with great advantage to the district re-
ferred to.
Well, having now seen the evil and admitted the
remedy, what does the Courier propose to do
about it? Is it going to support Philp, who
says he is opposed to every scheme, to do what
the Courier says would be “a great advan-
tage’ ? If it swallows Philp, it has also got
to swallow the “jungles of lantana and Noo-
goora burr.” In fact, as Philp’s policy produced
these jungles, more Philp will mean more jungles
almost for a certainty. Once more, then, what
is the Courier going to do about it ?
Speaking at Tam worth (N.S.W.) on the
urgent need of wresting the magnificent Peel
River Estate from its foreign holders, Joseph
Carrutbers chided the local folk for hollering out
so loud. All this hollering, said Joseph, has
convinced the co. that it has a choice thing on,
and in anticipation of the price at which it hopes
to unload to the Government, the co.’s shares
have risen from £lOO to £l5O. Joseph’s states-
man-like proposal is, therefore, to knock the co.
out, by heaving, not a land-tax, but a Stinking
Fish at it. The fact that N.S. Wales can’t heave
Stinking Fish at the Peel River Estate without
heaving it also at N.S. Wales doesn’t appear to be
of any consequence to Carrutbers, whose one plan
of getting land for the people is the pettifogging
attorney’s trick of crying that the land isn’t worth
having.
The natural effect of a closer settlement
policy unaccompanied by a genuine graduated
land-tax shows itself already in Victoria. In the
Wimmera and the Mallee, the rise in the price of
land has been so swift that local farmers and their
sons who want blocks are unable to pay the
price that Greed demands, and are flocking
to wider N.S. Wales and Queensland. To
attract population, Australia wants dear labor and
cheap land. The Tory ideal has always been
cheap labor and dear land, and the
result has tended to make the country
one vast sheep run.
THE MISSIONARY SHIP.
A Solomon Islands missionary having complained of
hostile treatment, H.M.S. Prometheus opened fire on the
natives with live shell: —Daily Paper.
NAVAL COMMANDER : "Don’t you worry, parson!
They shall hear the Terruth, or by
I’ll
Maonland just now affords the spec-
tacle pf an ex-strike leader, as Minister for
Labor, upholding the Arbitration Law by every
means in his power. The slaughtermen’s strike
started at Wellington, where trouble had been
simmering for some time. One morning the men
rolled up, donned the clothes they murder cattle
in, grabbed the knives, and took up their posi-
tions at the boards. Just before the starting
whistle blew the manager was asked if he would
pay the increased rate, and when he refused every
slaughterman dropped his knife, and walked off
the job. The receipt of the news in the South
Island was the signal for cessation of work there
also, and within two days the Maoriland frozen
meat industry was closed up,' Minister J. A.
Millar immediately announced that the law would
be enforced, and within 24 hours of the start of
the trouble the Wellington men were on trial for
having broken an industrial agreement. They
won the case, on the ground that the agreement
was faulty by reason of vagueness as to its
duration. It would seem that the law requires
all industrial agreements to be for a specified
period. By the time this is in print the Canter-
bury men will have been dealt with, but whether
they will be charged with breaking an industrial
agreement or breaking the industrial peace is not
known at time of writing.

The Hon. William Knox, although the Federal
flections are over, still interests himself greatly in the work
of tue Protestant Defence League, and presided when the
question of selecting- candidates for the Victorian State

Oh ! William Knox,
Who stoutly forth for “ God and Empire ” stands !
The “Empire ” knew the facts,
Slowly, of all the pacts
Of that first floating of the British Blocks ;
And then the “ Empire ” had to kiss the rod,

John Knox we knew :
He died of—well—an accident long years agone
Did naught for “ Empire ” care,
Knowing he could not double service bear—
Kept only his peculiar God in view
His God and faith in just and kindly men !
No “ Empire ’’ then !
Oh ! William-we
Cannot accept such God and Mammon mixed !
Such holy greed !
For don’t we read
That Chillagoes were thirty-nine and three?
Away! Away !
Australia is the only call to-day !
