SPORTING
NOITIONS
It would be hard to imagine a more ideal course
from the spectacular point of view than that of the
Nepean, over which Towns glided and Durnan struggled
on Saturday for the sculling championship of the world.
The course is a straight reach, and from the high banks
spectators with glasses could watch the race from start
to finish. It is said that there are some dangerous mud
banks and patches of weeds, which make it less attractive
to the scullers, and which moved the people who followed
the rfcce in motor boats to tears and curses. But to the
men on those high banks, watching the two specks grow-
ing larger as they came up out of the suspended, fulgent
haze of the afternoon, that didn’t matter. Of course, a
race on the Nepean is not the pageant that it
would be on the Parramatta. There is no gorgeous
homeward rush of hundreds of craft, lashing the smooth
water into waves. The 12,000 people scattered along the
three miles of Nepean course seemed a mere handful.
The race was due to start at 4 p.m., and, just
before that hour, the sky suddenly drooped, the distant
hills became a wall of Indian ink, and a faint, almost im-
perceptible, rain fell spasmodically. Umbrellas were
hoisted out, but hardly were they opened before the rain
ceased. A few sporting’ individuals walked up and down
the bank inquiring anxiously if anyone wanted to back
Durnan. Nobody did. A man in the centre of a waiting
group told how a friend of his laid in six gallons of beer
every Saturday night, and by church-time on Sunday
morning was “as dry as a rat’s back.” Men in shirt-
sleeves ran up and down offering “ chocolates, a penny
each, four for threepence—Sydney prices!” Nobody
took any notice, and the only trade done was by the
fellow who sold peanuts. On the clipped willows, men
with glasses were staring anxiously up the river, and, as
it was after four, concluded that their glasses were frauds.
The sky had receded to its natural position, the far-off
hills turned once more to purple, and a curious effulgence
filled the atmosphere. Down the long straight course
water and atmospheric haze gleamed bewilderinglv.
About 28 minutes past four a man, watching through”a
glass, informed the group around him that they were off,
and had been called back to make a fresh start.
At the start Towns shot to the front. Durnan
gave no sign of the wonderful burst of speed with which
he had been credited, and Towns continued to lead till
he ran into shallow water. Then he slowed down, and
Durnan went to the front. At half a mile the Australian
had again taken the lead, and at half-way was ahead by
several lengths. At two miles Towns allowed Dur-
nan to draw level, and settled down to hold
the Canadian for the final spurt, which commenced
about 300yds. from home. The difference between
the two scullers was enormous. Towns’ rowing
was good to watch. His body swayed back and for-
wards, his sculls flashed, and his boat ran on with amaz-
ing regularity. It never faltered as he bent for his next
stroke, always gliding along with the same unbroken
speed, cleanly and beautifully, as though propelled bv
some hidden machinery under the water. Durnan,
on the other hand, was rowing harder than the
Australian, but as his sculls lifted his boat seemed
to fall off, and rolled slightly. He made a desperate
spurt at last, and tore the water with the dipping
sculls, sending his boat along with a series
of jumps. The Australian seemed to row no
harder. His boat ran on with the same beautiful
motion, and finished about two lengths ahead of the
struggling Canadian. The way he rowed the last part of
the race and his finish suggested that Towns could have
won by a much larger margin than he did. Writer saw
him beat Stanbury, and there was a huge difference in
the way he sculled on those two occasions. On the Par-
ramatta he was pulling ail he knew ; on the Nepean he
was giving an artistic display that was worth going far
to see.
This is the third time that Towns has
asserted his right to the sculling championship
of the world. The first occasion was when he
won it from Jake Gaudaur, at Rat Portage, on the
Lake of the Woods, Canada. The second occasion
was when he recovered it from Jim Stanbury on the
Parramatta last year ; and the third w 7 hen he maintained
his pre-eminence against Durnan on Saturday. Up to
date he has been champion of the world three years
and eight months. Stanbury reigned altogether six
years four months and fifteen days. Gaudaur held the
honor five years ; Trickett, four years and four months ;
and the great Hanlan only three years and nine months.
* * * *
There are some crack operators in the Pacific
cable service, and they put up a good performance in
connection with the Towns-Durnan match that will take
a lot of beating. The Canadian papers were wild to get
news of Towns’ defeat, and in Montreal a procession was
waiting to start, with waving flags and screaming
trumpets, to where champagne was laid on. The special
correspondent of the Canadian press handed his wire
into the temporary office near the course at 4.54 p.m.
At 4.58 p.m. (Australian time) that cablegram hid
traversed the 10,000 miles of land and ocean, and knocked
the Montreal people flat. It passed through the hands
of at least six men, most of whom had to receive
it, and then re-transmit it, and any man who talks
about the “ Government stroke ” after that has not gob
the sense of the animal that spoke at Balaam.
* » « *
After Ebullition had won the Newmarket
Handicap, with his stable companion Step Out in the
fourth place, the man who “ knew it would win,” and
“ ought to have backed it,” was not so numerous as is
usual on such occasions. Outside of the followers of
Hickenbotham’s stable, very few punters gave Ebullition
a great chance, although the touts had marked the colt
dangerous on his track form. It may now be taken almost
for granted that the Newmarket winner was a consistent
failure at Caulfield and Flemington in the spring,
merely because he was raced beyond his distance. He
can stay six or seven furlongs under the severest sprint-
ing conditions, but he probably lacks the stamina to keep
him going with the best of them for a mile. Backed down
to 5 to 4 for the October Stakes at Caulfield, five months
ago, Ebullition finished 2J lengths behind Ellis, who was
receiving only 71b. In the Caulfield Guineas (1 mile) he
was' beaten easily by Booran. And three times during
the Melbourne Cup meeting the disappointing animal
was beaten out of a place. All things considered, the
only inducement to back Ebullition at 14 to 1 for the
Newmarket was the fact that the stable preferred him to
Step Out, who had a reasonable chance for this race on
his previous Saturday’s running.
Fifty yards from/ home Collarit appeared
capable of doing the trick for Crick, but the handsome*
burly colt had too much weight, and a little superfluous
flesh, perhaps. Ebullition and North Head passed him
in the final struggle, and North Head repeated his per-
formance of last year by running second with 8.9 up—or
71b. more than he carried as a three-year-old. Of those that
finished badly, Pompous, Ellis, and Istria were the only
ones that had ever been in front of the field. The
unluckiest of all was Decollette. The mare met with a
mishap in the morning, which caused her to be knocked
back to 25 to lin the betting. In the race Decollette
must have been galloped on, for she came back limping
to the “ birdcage,” and soon afterwards she was carrying
one of her hind feet as though afraid to put it to the
ground.
The Sires’ Produce Stakes brought out some
of the crack two-year-olds that battled in the spring.
And, for the fourth successive time, Scobie found one
good enough to beat him. Tangaroa, the winner, had
beaten him once before at even weights, whereas Boast-
ful, who finished third, was twice defeated by Scobie last
year. In the running of this trio of youngsters there
has been nothing for the finger of suspicion to point at.
And Poseidon, of course, won the Leger. Com-
pared with Antonious, his occasionally formidable rival
among the three-year-olds, Poseidon is an adorable crea-
ture. Antonious, beaten in the Essendon Stakes on
Saturday, has shown some remarkable variations of
“form” since September, without scoring one win.
Poseidon, during the same period, has won nine races in
ten tries. His unsuccessful try was when he ran second
to Solution in the Metropolitan Handicap. The hard-
faced bag-and-book crowd commonly refers to Poseidon
as the“’Baeca Trust horse,” and the term is most apt.
Baccas have good cause to trust him.
* « * •
The V.R.C. committee is being; made to look
more and more ridiculous by the seemingly omnipotent
John Wren. A year or two ago the committee refused
to accept Wren as a nominator of horses for races at
registered meetings. Thereupon Wren took in a number
of partners and nominated his horses in their names, each
partner being perhaps the actual owner of a few hairs in
the tail of every horse he nominates under the Tote
man’s directions. These notorious foolings with the
V.R.C. authorities are now thrown into the shade by the
fact that Wren, whilst blatantly proclaiming himself the
proprietor of two pestilent pony racecourses, is the
thinly-veiled boss of a racecourse which is registered by
the V.R.C.
* * • *
In making the publication of betting odds (in
figures) an unlawful thing, the Anti-Gambling Acts have,
perhaps, hurt the bookmakers, without worrying those
inveterate punters who are interested in early market
operations. The betting reports published under the
new law are sufficiently explicit to keep punting interest
alive without inciting casual readers to rash speculation.
For instance, public appetite for “ market intelligence ”
is sufficiently catered for by such paragraphs as this :
“ The most fancied candidates for the Newmarket Handi-
cap are Istria, North Head, Decollette, Collarit, New-
bury and Curtain Lecture in that order, and a good sum
was secured about North Head and Curtain Lecture.”
* ■» * »
Grade cricket resumed in Sydney on Saturday,
and, judging by the high scoring, the rest did the bats-
men good, or the bowlers harm. No less than four cen-
turies were made, and Norman Deane missed three figures
by one run only. R. A. Duff also had hard luck in failing
to reach the century, being bowled by Syrian Kerim when
he had reached 96. Alphabetical White, of North Sydney,
made 177 against Redfern, and in the same match and
innings Norman Deane carried his bat for the 99 above
mentioned. L. W. Pye, playing for Central Cumberland
against Middle Harbor, carried his bat for 168. M. A. Noble
scored 117 for Paddington against Sydney District. The
same match saw Trumper apparently regain form ; he
collected 110 before he was caught. Butin spite of this
great demonstration with the willow the leather also got
a fair look in. Robson, of Balmain, grounded seven of
University’s sticks for 46 runs; Bowden, of Glebe,
dropped six of Waverley’s for 38; L. P. Deane, of Bur-
wood, took seven of Gordon’s for 78; and Cotter, of
Glebe, captured three of Waverley’s for 49.
* ■* » *
Although Victor Trumper’s recovery of form
did not happen soon enough to save his face in connection
with certain recent bellicose utterances, it did arrive in
time to do some good for Padding ton cl nb. Carty was with
Trumper, and the pair made a fine display, smiting 151
in the first hour. Paddington closed the day with eight
wickets down for 378. Huge as this score was, it did not
top the poll on that willowy afternoon. It was out at
Redfern that the spectacular cricket was on view. There
Duff and White got together, and fell on the bowling as
the Angel of Death fell on the Assyrians. The hundred
arrived in 42 minutes, the hour saw 146, and so fast did
they score that in 90 minutes, when the partnership
dissolved, 259 had been made for the first wicket. Clark
made 45, and the not out men, Deane and Mackay,
accumulated 166 between them. North Sydney went
home feeling good ; it had scored 545 for the loss of
seven wickets. This was something like cricket. Central
Cumberland also did well, making 345 for six wickets.
* * * »
They all do it. Never a champion yet who
seemed to be able to keep out of the ring after having
retired on his laurels, with the exception of old Bill
Beach, the Australian rower. He left it in a blaze of
glory, and stayed out. In the fighting line, Jeffries, after
having retired, is bobbing about anxiously inquiring who
will offer 50,000 dollars for a fight between him and the
next best, whoever he may be. Jim Stanbury came back
to flutter a brief space in the limelight, and be bumped
out of it for ever. And now comes news that Major
Taylor, the black United States cyclist, will re-appear in
Paris right away, and next season will be found whizzing
round Australian tracks. Quite a crowd of old stagers
like Lawson, MacFarland, Taylor and Ellegaarde are
threatening to raid Australia next season.
* * * *
The Royal Sydney Yacht Club’s race for the
Commodore’s prizes was an exciting chapter of accidents.
First, the steamer with the spectators arrived an hour
late, and missed the best of the fun. Rawhiti arrived at
the starting line nearly four minutes late, and never had
a hope in consequence. Kulruburra’s bowsprit carried
away, and the yacht, drifted out of the race. Sunbeam
had two of the crew washed overboard ; they were picked
up by the boat Iris, after which the ’Beam retired. Then
skipper Doran ran the Bona aground at the Sow and
Pigs, and lost three or four minutes getting clear.
Saxton’s new boat, Awanui, came romping through the
hard, squally nor’-easter, and loped away to the front,
and stayed there till the finish, beating Bona by 2min.
56sec., with Culwulla in the third place.
* * * *
Schoolboy Frank Beaurepaire, of the Albert
Park Swimming Club, further improved his record by
winning a J-mile championship of Victoria, at the City
Baths (Melb.), t’other Wednesday night. The rival
starters were—Mason, who latelv represented Vic. in
Maoriland ; and Billy' Clegg, of Ballarat. The schoolboy
finished seven clear yards ahead, in 6min. 9 3-ssec.,
smashing the previous best time for the distance by
6 l-ssec., according to several doubtful watches.
* * * *
The long-delayed news of the Ormond-Daytona
Beach (U.S.A.) speed carnival tells of the repeated
success of the steam Stanley car, which won the world’s
five mile championship in 4min. 62sec.—a record. The
only car to get near the flying steamer was the English
Rolls-Royce car. It was said before the race that the
Honorable Rolls would drive this himself. Rolls is an all-
round man who experiments with balloons, aeroplanes,
motor-bikes, hydroplanes, and other things. The Rolls-
Royce car is practically his own invention, and is the
latest of English cars, and it will be remembered that it
was the same type of car, driven by the same man, that
won the Tourist Trophy race in the British contest, some
few months back. In addition to running recond in the
five miles, the Rolls-Royce car, although only 20h.p.,
pressed the 70h.p. American Mercedes in another five
miles race that was not a championship event.
* * * *
The proposal to hold another Paris to Madrid
autocar test has recalled to popular memory and indig-
nation the awful contest of 1903, which has passed into
history as the “Race of Death.” The French Govern-
ment stopped it at the end of the first day, when such
of the cars as were not wrecked had arrived at Bordeaux.
It was that disastrous contest which led the French
country people to form associations to shoot motorists
at sight, and for a time made it unsafe to drive a motor
car through France at anything more than 10 miles an
hour. Two hundred cars, many of them 90-h.p. racers,
were let loose at short intervals. Some were manned by
experienced drivers, but a huge proportion by men who
were practically amateurs. So great was the cloud of
dust raised that the drivers could not see the road, and
steered by the tops of the long lines of poplar trees
which mark the French highways. In that cloud of dust
tragedies were enacted, and the 342 miles between Paris
and Bordeaux were strewn with wrecked cars, smashed
vehicles, dead and injured motorists, pedestrians,
travellers, children and animals. The first section was
won by Driver Gabriel, who, mounted on a 70-h.p. Mors,
was the 168th man to start, and threaded his way through
the ruin and the corpses at over 65 miles an hour, com-
pleting the whole distance in shr. 14min. 31 l-ssec. The
most tremendous smash was where a 90-h.p. racer dashed
clean over a low-powered carin front and into an omnibus
loaded with a wedding party, and the whole mass rolled
over an embankment down into a ditch. Gabriel was
close behind, and though travelling at a lot more than
a mile a minute, cut down the opposite bank, and
regained the road without slackening speed. His
drive will probably stand as a record till some battle like
Chalons or Paniput is fought between armies mounted
on automobiles.
• * * *
As a reliability test, that proposed motor run
from Paris to Pekin will beat anything ever yet
attempted, and the car that arrives first will get such an
advertisement that the people who make it should all die
millionaires. The European portion of the trip should be
easy enough, but in Asia the only roads that an auto-
mobile could travel on will lie in Russian territory. Pre-
sumably the route would be from Paris to Vienna by any
of the score of routes available, thence to Moscow, along
the Siberian railway route to Irkutsk, across Lake Baikal,
thence once more along’ the railway and down through
Manchuria to the Chinese capital, where the Chinese
have now got an automobile club. The only other
possible route seems to be across Turkey, southern Persia
and Baluchistan to India, thence through Burmah to
China. But the car that runs that wajf will have to dis-
pense absolutely with roads for much of the journey', and
must be prepared to put up some fast runs with pre-
datory horsemen, and armored cars carrying machine
artillery might be necessary. To all appearance the
southern trip is only possible on paper. The expedition
is not likely to come off, though, by either route.
* ■* * *
Cyclist Liddle, on a tour round the world,
recently' made a stop of several weeks in Melbourne,
prior to his departure for Tassy. Liddle started from
Cape Town, dead broke. Working on the honor and
glory ticket, a polite way of battling “on the never,” he
has covered a fair lump of South Africa, and travelled
from North Queensland to Port Phillip, mostly on main
roads. At a cyclists’ smoker, t’other week, he said,
“I’m not allowed to beg.” But he frankly admitted his
ability to force an invite as a conjurer forces a card.
During his stay' in Melbourne he supported himself by
hawking post-cards—•“ My photo’’—interspersed with a
lengthy jabber, principally about African farm-burners,
at cycle-club meetings. Liddle hails from the Orkney
Isles. He went to S’Africa with a kilt cycle corps on a
despatch-carrying job. After the slaughter he took to
counter-jumping; till he struck the idea that he would
cease work and circle the globe at the expense of Kaffirs,
Chows, and any old thing that would spare a shake-down
or a feed. Liddle left Melbourne with a credit balance
and a number of favorable impressions. Compared with
trans-continental Birtles, who has lately been battling
with the drought and the sandhills in Central Australia,
Liddle is a fine sample of the Sunday afternoon city-to-
St. Kilda bikist.
A rowing shindy has broken out between
Launceston and Hobart. Launceston is the home of the
Tasmanian Rowing Association, but most of the rowers
live in Hobart, and the Hobart rowers decline to take
part in the corning Inter-Scate Eight-oar contest if they
are to be selected by a common lot of people at Laun-
ceston. The jealousy' between the two “ centres of
population,” as they fondly call themselves, is so violent
that each faction is in danger of being pushed off the
globe by the other. Even if Tasmania does not turn up
the race will still happen.
„ * * *
Everybody knows that the leading clubs in the
Victorian Football League pay fine, substantial salaries to
their leading players, but the amounts are always
charged as lemons, or bootlaces, or eucalyptus, and in
that way the League is able to still sound the loud
timbrel about its lily-white amateurism. Melbourne
Football Club has for y'ears been the most amateur of the
whole bunch. Sixteen out, of its 18 men did not
receive a bean. As a result Melbourne dropped to
the bottom of the premiership list, and lost sup-
porters and enthusiasm. A bold bid for promi-
nence and success was to be made this coming
season. Months ago the Melbourne committee told the
world at large that it was going to be honest and openly
pay its men. But the zeal for honest professionalism
sputtered out. The whole position is paradoxical. The
League is 70 percent, professional at the present time,
yet the following speech sufficed to squelch the honesty
purposed by the Melbourne committee :
“ After what was said at the last annual meeting of the
cricket club, everyone thinks the club is going to pay
players. If professionalism is brought into football in
Victoria this club will get the credit of it. (Sensation.)
Other clubs do it, and don’t make it public. They elect
a finance committee, and only the members of that com-
mittee know what is done. If you go on talking like
this you will have a difficulty in getting a single permit
through.” (Cheers.)
Being professional is not the sin; the iniquity lies in
openly saying that you are. The fact that people only
know you are doesn’t matter.
* * * *
~ Johnson-Lang fight in Melbourne last
Monday was an example of what the ringsider will put
up with to see a scrap. Twenty thousand of him rolled
up at Richmond racecourse, and stood in pelting rain,
which ran down inside the clothing and fairly spouted
from the boots, watching Johnson and Lang bash one
another on an elevated platform. And, after all, it was
not much of a fight for which the collective rin sider
risked pneumonia and rheumatism. The black man was
taller and a stone heavier than his white rival, and
when the disturbance started Lang was so debilitated
with stage fright, or something, that he could not move
properly, and acted something like the doll-wife in “La
Poupee.” A bash or two from Johnson effectually re-
moved that, and then Lang pulled himself together and
waded in to destroy. The second and third rounds saw
him put up a vigorous fight, only to discover that he was
over-reached, out-classed, and out-weighted. Attheend
of the third round he had shot his bolt, and thence-
forward became receiver-general. Johnson smote him
how and when he liked, he upper-cut him, he short-
jolted him on ribs and diaphragm, and for six solid rounds
the white man endured it, vainly hoping for a chance
to knock out his good-natured antagonist. Then he threw
in the towel. The fighting community is generally of
opinion that the fight has discounted Johnson’s prowess
considerably. He should have knocked out Lang in
three rounds, say the cognoscenti, and if it takes him
nine rounds to finish Lang he has no hope of walking
over Squires, although he might heat him on points—
always provided that Squires didn’t get in close and “hit
him with me hand on the jore !” As there is apparently
no one in Australia of his class just at present, Johnson’s
manager is considering the advisability of letting
him take on two men, one after the other, in quick suc-
cession. Which will be rough on the first man of the pair.
* * * *
“Snell”: The Olympic’s fight at Melbourne
Cyclorama on Monday night, Feb. 25, was Russell, the
youthful Sydney heavyweight, versus Fraser, the not-
too-youthful Melbourne middleweight. There was a
difference of quite l-J stone in favor of the visitor, and
Fraser had no option, when hard up against the slogger,
but to keep out as well as possible, and cover by all
devices within the rules. The Melbournian was miles
ahead of Russell as a boxer, and notched points in a
methodical way right through the fight. Russell, finding
Bob’s head too flighty to hit, contented himself with
slogging for the body, and, in the course of 20 rounds,
wore his opponent down by sheer advantage of weight
and strength. Finishing the fresher of the two, he got
the verdict. On points, Fraser seemed to have capped
the Cornstalk, but the referee did not see it in that light.
It is amazingly hard at times to understand in what light
referees do see it.
* * * •
“Snell”: A few words in reply to Referee
Basto, who, like all referees, gets very feverish when the
quality of his judgment is questioned. I never doubted
its honesty. Firstly, I don’t endeavor to hide my identity
under my pen-name ; the personality behind it is cer-
tainly better known to Bulletin 1 readers than Mr. Basto
is. If Andrews was not disgusted with Mr. B.’s decision,
w’hat was the meaning of his anguished protestations in
the ring? If his seconds approved of it, why did
they behave as if they had been kicked hard
in a tender place? If all right-thinking people
thought the decision absolutely 0.K., why the
frenzied hoots that followed its deliverance? To
be sure, Mr. Basto’s definition of the right-thinking
person may be the one who believes as he believes. Yes,
Griffen had superior strength in his contest with Fraser,
and won because the referee gave him a chance of proving
it after he had been down several times. The people who
crave to see a match carried to the point of inhumanity
are very few (and I’ve joined Melbourne crowds in yelling
to Mr. Basto to call tfie boys off before this), but there is
a medium in all things, and my opinion is that the
referee was squeamish in this case. His opinion is that
he was an apostle of humanity. Well, it’s a case of two
opinions. He’s paid to give his ; I’m paid to give mine.
* * * *
“ Yarrum”: Met. a cricket enthusiast t’other
day in Maitland (N.S. W.). When a boy, he tramped from
Nattai to Sydney—so miles—to see the first English
eleven play Australia. His father had “ tons of money”
at the time, but he looked upon cricket as a waste of
time, and wouldn’t part with the return fare from Camp-
belltown, which was then the terminus of the southern
line. My enthusiast can tell now of every ball that was
bowled—mostly “ under-armers ” —and of every hit that
was struck. The late George Thornton’s belltopper blew
away that day ; and he bought a dinner with the bob he
got for chasing it. Of all the men who played for Aus-
tralia then, he says there is but one, Nat. Thompson,
now living. The old fellow has played the game all his
life ; hut the most he ever put up in an innings was seven,
against Wingecarribee.


By a recent mail The Bulletin received a
letter from R. X. Stephens, a wandering Australian
journalist now located at St. Louis (U.S.A), who inter-
viewed Jeffries at his home at Burbank, outside Los
Angeles. “Bill Squires beat Jeffries? Impossible! In
the first place Squires would look like a baby beside
Jeffries, and in the second Jeffries is not only a great
fighter but a remarkably scientific boxer.. .. Jeffries is a
remarkable example of strength. Every muscle in his
face denotes strength. He has a pair of eyes like
Kitchener’s. He has a great big back, bulky shoulders,
thick neck—he wears al9 collar—and massive arms and
legs. And such hands ! They are as big as the feet of
the ‘Salvation Army Giant’ who some years ago carried
the banner in Bendigo (Vic.)... ‘ What kind of a fighter
is this Squires?’ inquired the champion. ‘ls he a
rusher?’ I admitted that I thought Squires could be
described as a rusher, one of the best Australia has pro-
duced since Joe Goddard. Jeff, smiled. ‘ I like these
rushers. When I’m fighting them I get close in to the
‘ Sons of Guns ’ and I make ’em let go at me and as soon as
they do I turn on lefts and rights. One of my first fights was
with a rusher—Tom Sharkey. I’ll never forget that
fight. He had several ribs broken, and I nearly
chopped his head off.’ Later on, he inquired what
were Squires’ height and weight, and when I told
him Squires was about sft. lOin. high and fought at
about 1721b5., he smiled. ‘I am about 260 now, and if I
agree to fight r.his Australian, I’ll do three months’ hard
work at handball playing and boxing. At the end of
that time I’ll be fit and well, and I’ll enter the ring at
about 210. At that weight lam always very strong and
active. Whenever I agree to fight, I train faithfully,
whether the match is a soft snap or not. I take no
chances, and, even for moving-picture considerations, I
won’t let the other fellow up. Has Squires beaten any
good men yet ? ’
• • • • • •
“‘Now, listen to me,’ said Jeffries, ‘and I’ll
tell you something. lam champion of the world 1 The
people who are promoting this fight want me to appear
in a championship of the world match in Rhyolite, so as
to boom it and place it on the map the same as Goldfield
was boomed through having the Gans-Nelson light-
weight championship fight there. The purse offered is
$30,000, 60 per cent, to winner, 40 per cent, to loser.
Now, understand it is money that talks to me. This
Rhyolite crowd must come through with the rhino. I
want at least $25,000, win, lose, or draw, before I will fight
in any of the Nevada mining towns. It would cost me
between three and four thousand dollars to train for a
fight, and the winning end of the purse, as the Rhyolite
people figure it, is $lB,OOO. I think I could wallop
Squires, and the $lB,OOO would come my way, but after
paying for sparring partners, &c., I would only have
about $14,000. Not me. They can give Squires what
they like, but I must have $25,000 for my appear-
ance ; and, let me tell you, I think $5OOO for
a man who has been fighting for $2OOO purses cut
into 60 for the winner and 40 for the loser, is a
very fair thing for him. Squires ought to be pleased to
come to America, and, without a trial, fight me for the
championship of the world. No, I want $25,000 win,
lose or draw, or they won’t see Jeff, in the ring. I’m not
hard up. I’ve 140 acres of land on this patch for w'hich I
paid $lOO an acre. I raise alfalfa on it. I crop it nine
times a year, and the average return per acre, each year,
is at least two tons. Alfalfa is $l5 a ton. No, I’ll fight
for nothing less than $25,000 win, lose or draw.” And
when on top of this demand by Jeffries a couple of ’Frisco
scribes “ roasted ” Squires as a man who had fought only
fifth-raters, the Rhyolite people were scared. That was
how Squires’ first match with Jeffries fell through on
New Year’s Eve, 1906.
In the light of the above conversation, the vague
rumors to the effect that the Squires-Jeffries match is
“on’’again are to be taken with considerable caution.
New York Police Gazette announces that if Squires will
go to America he and Jeffries may fight for a $30,000
purse, which means that either Jeffries has climbed down
from his attitude when interviewed by Stephens, or else
the Rhyolite Club is going to offer Squires a very small
end of the purse, win or lose. When Skinner, of the
Rhyolite Athletic Club, saw Jeffries, the champion stood
out for a $50,000 purse, but the promoter said it was
$30,000 or nothing. The Bulletin does not think Mr.
Squires has the remotest chance of fighting Mr. Jim
Jeffries for the championship of the world.
Most of the fine old Tories who talk
about the sacred rights of proputty, and the Con-
stooshun, and precedent, and add that a Britisher s
home is his castle —most of the fine old Tones
who mention these things whenever a land-tax to
burst up big idle estates is promised, dont
know that in 1534 an Act was passed in England
forbidding any grazier to have more than 2000
sheep. A tendency had set in to lay out sheep-
walks instead of carrying on agriculture, and as
danger was threatening 'from France, it was
feared there would not be enough men on the
land to successfully defend it against invasion.
The modern Australian statesman is far more con-
siderate. He proposes that Fat should be allowed
to keep 10,000 sheep if he’ll improve his holding,
and run them on a small area, and so give others
a chance to breed sheep and feed children.
The Earoscope is an American invention by
which the newly-discovered beneficial effects of vibratory
motion are applied to the delica e organism of the inner
ear. Strange as it may seem, the interior of the ear is
about the only part of the human body that cannot be
seen into during life. The brain itself has been
examined while working at full blast, and even
the remote parts of human anatomy usually included
by Billjim in the comprehensive word “innards” have
all been explored by some wizard with the aid of some
appliance that, a few hundred years ago, would have
earned him a warm death. The inner ear alone remains
an unknown territory during - life, and is inaccessible.
The “Earoscope” does not pierce the ear, or in-
terfere with it in any way. It is a vibratory
instrument, and soothes the troubled organ by
invigorating aural kinetics, which tune up the aural
cord, apply vitalising force to the delicate nerves of the
inner ear, and helping the “ossicles” to vibrate in their
usual manner. By this process an improved circulation
is set up, and diseased ears are benefited by the exercise
and the increased circulation of healthy blood. The in-
strument is sold by the “Earoscope” Co., at its Sydney
branch, 90 Pitt-street. 1 '

In his Brunswick candidature, Judkins is
giving addresses to “ women only,” though
women have no vote for the State Parliament.
But, even if they had, what has Judkins got to say
to Brunswick’s wife in secret that he hasn’t the
nerve to say to her with Mr. Brunswick looking
on? And, assuming that Jud. could ever be
elected, is he going to carry the scheme right
through, and address himself, not to Parliament,
but to Parliament’s wife ? If so, it doesn’t seem
necessary to send him to Parliament —he can talk
to Parliament’s missus quite as conveniently if
Brunswick keeps him just plain Jud.
“ Blister” : The passing of a police officer
who was one of the vanquished recalls the defeat
of an attempt made to coerce white with yellow
labor at the Lothair mine, Clunes (Vic.), in 1873.
The men, in the first place, went out because they
were refused Saturday afternoons off, and the
solid sympathy of the Clunes miners made it im-
possible for the directors to get other labor. So the
directors, including Premier Francis and (alas !)
Peter Lalor, threatened a Mongolian invasion. The
Clunes people got drunk with excitement, and on
December 8 held a mass meeting. At mid-
night, encouraged by the women, the crowd of
about 1000 enthusiasts destroyed the building
erected for the Chinese, and prepared to give
battle to the police on the following day. At
an early hour on December 9 the strikers swarmed
along the Creswick road, took possession of the
toll-gate, and, armed with pick-handles and wad-
dies, waited for the yellow and white invaders.
About 11 o’clock, a big van of Chinese and
police, under Sergeant Former, hove in sight,
but retreated when the rioters made a demon-
stration. .Lalor and the other mining politicians,
after thinking it over, gave way before the
force of public opinion, and the miners, having
effectually scared the few Chows who lingered
in the neighborhood, went back to work, victo-
rious.
Barney Allen

“ Michael Carr ” writes :
The day’s work that is liable to happen to a Victorian
railway man : On Wednesday (16th Jan.), we left Sea Lake
at 6.40 a.m., which means that the guard signed on at 6
ain The train arrived at Wycheproof about 10.15 a.m.
On the journey, the guard had to handle the goods,
assist at shunting, &c., for, at intermediate stations,
such as Boigbeat, Kaneira, Berriwillock, and Nullavil,
there is onlv a woman—or at least an almost
honorary skeleton in a skirt—in charge. At 2.30 p.m.
this same guard, after fixing up his sheets and returns
(no small matter), started back to Sea Lake with a
train of 71 trucks, dropping trucks at each station.
Arrived at Sea Lake 6.10 p.m., and left again at 7 p.m.,
picking up loaded wheat trucks all the way down to
Wycheproof, where he arrived at 10.35 p.m. I was in
the train between Kaneira and Sea Lake on the last trip,
and saw him furtively eating. Perhaps he thought I
would report him to Tait for feeding in working hours.
He was a tired man when we got to Wycheproof, for it
had been a scorching Mallee day. Apart from any sym-
pathy for the poor baked wretch himself, what a menace
to the public safety! This man was on duty for 16£
hours, doing hard manual work, too, in a sun tempera-
ture that would have lighted Tait’s cigar, and making up
his complicated returns in a moving oven in between
times.

Rabbits now make up 50 per cent, of the
goods frozen for export in the Government Cool
Stores in Melbourne; and the quantity exported
from N.S.W. exceeds that from Vic., while
figures from S.A. and Tas. and Maoriland, all
look up. Last year the Cold Country took
1,039,371 crates from Australasia, equal to 16,000
tons of meat, or just 24 million bunnies ! That
meant £650,000 paid to Australia. And so far all
that Danysz promises in return for killing this
trade is that he may cause a temporary discourage-
ment of bunny—till the rodent becomes immune.
“ When shall their glory fade ? ” Thomas
White, 70 years of age, one of the very few
remaining survivors of. the Six Hundred, applied,
on January 2, to the Medway Board of Guardians
at Chatham (Eng.) for relief. He had been for
months on the verge of starvation. He was
severely wounded in the Balaclava charge, and
was invalided on 6d. a day for 18 months—in all,
the magnificent sum of 13 guineas. He was tem-
porarily relieved with 3s. 6d. and one loaf per
week.

“THE CHILDREN’S FOOD.”
“F.H.”: A huge navvy arrived, and
gruffly remarked that he had come to remove the
verandah. It not being my verandah I raised no
objections, merely wondering how he was going
to do it without tools. Embracing the nearest
post in a bear-like hug he gave a colossal
heave. The support cracked ominously, and
a vase fell off the dining-room mantel-
piece on to the hearth. This brought
the landlady in a whirlwind of wrath,
but when she found the big stranger again
wrapping himself round the post, she fled to the
back regions, where she could be heard boxing
someone’s ears and sending for the police. On
her return she asked if I wasn’t going to do any-
thing. I was about to inquire “What?” when
the house-shifter got a purchase with his boots
and threw all his weight outwards. The post
snapped with a loud report, while the
navvy blotted out a bed of pansies with
the broad of his back. He was starting on
another post, when the policeman glode into the
incident. Realising the absurdity of trying to
arrest single-handed a person who tore up houses
by the roots, he began to talk weather, while the
landlady looked unutterable things through the
window. In ■ the course of conversation the
destroyer remarked that it was a big contract,
which he would never have taken on had
it not been for a very dear friend—
a publican in Flinders-street, who had asked
him to shift this particular verandah be-
fore he said good-bye. Here the workman
wiped away a tear and supposed he’d have to “do
a bit more.” “ We’ve got one or two things up
at the station that could do with a bit of shifting,”
said the cop, with the smile of a little child, “and
you could come back and do this when it ain’t so
’ot.” I followed afar off. Arrived at the lock-up,
the man in fustian at once saw what was wanted
—the cell door was a foot “ out.” As he stooped
to examine it, the guileless constable hurled all
his weight against him, and sent him sprawling
in, and the door clanged. A J.P., on being told
that a mad giant in d.t’s. had just been arrested,
came and held a court outside the bars. Then
four policemen and a navvy fell collectively into a
cab, which drove off to the gaol, sheddjjig its
windows as it went. It was only a working man
switched off his beer for a whole Sunday by the
new Licensing Act.

states, as evidence of the spread of the telephone
habit, that a clergyman recently surprised his
congregation by giving out, “Hymn double-six-
oh ! ”

Black Splotches All Over Face
Produced Severe Itching—Year’s
Treatment by Physicians Did No
Good and Became Despondent-
Affected Parts hfow Clear as Ever
Alabama Lady’s
CURE BY THE
CUTICURA REMEDIES
“About four years ago I was afflicted
with black splotches all over my face
and a few covering my body, which
produced a severe itching irritation, and
which caused me a great deal of annoy-
ance and suffering, to such an extent that
I was forced to call in two of the leading
physicians of my town. After a thor-
ough examination of the dreaded com-
plaint they announced it to be skin
eczema in its worst form. They treated
me for the same for the length of one
year, but the treatment did me no good.
“ Finally I became despondent and
decided to discontinue theif services.
Shortly afterwards, my husband in read-
ing a copy of a weekly New York
paper saw an advertisement of the. Cuti-
cura Remedies. He purchased the en-
tire outfit, and after using the contents
of the first bottle of Cuticura Resolvent
in connection with the Cuticura Soap
and Ointment-, the breaking out entirely
stopped. I continued the use of the
Cuticura Remedies for six months, and
after that every splotch was entirely
gone and the affected parts were left as
clear as ever. I have not felt a symp-
tom of the eczema since.
“The Cuticura Remedies not only
cured me of that dreadful disease,
eczema, but other complicated troubles
as well; and I have been the means of
others being cured of the same disease
by the Cuticura Remedies, and I don’t
hesitate in saying that* the Resolvent
is the best blood medicine that the world
has ever known. Lizzie E. Sledge,
Selma, Ala., Oct. 28, 1905.”

The Inky Way.
Queensland’s new democratic weekly, the Sun, edited !
by M.L.A. Kenna, seems to be rapidly supplanting' the
official Labor organ in popular favor. Editor Kenna,
by the way, is an ex-editor of the Worker and something
of a poet. Judging Worn the healthy democratic tone ol
his new paper, and the practical nature of its politics, it
should take firm rooti The SUN.seems to get a good deal
of exclusive political news, and is a sort of semi-official
organ of the Kidston party.
* * * «j»;
“ F.’* : “ Hassan ” (1?. 7/2/’O7) throws a too miscel-
laneous brick, when he says that the daily press “ is
ruled absolutely by its advertising columns.” The item
re girl-sweating in Melbourne, on which his noise is
founded, was printed by the Age. I have had a long ex-
perience of Syme’s rag, both as a reader and contributor ;
and the number of times tnat the Age has treated the
advertiser with a healthy disrespect is just legion. Some
examples. Every butter agent in the city advertises
in either the Age or Leader ; but, at the time of the
Butter Commission and since, David whaled them un-
mercifully, though he had nothing to gain from the co-
operative factories, which he was helping. The mail-
boats have always been advertisers, but the Age was re-
sponsible for taking the butter-carriage from them and
giving it to the outside steamers. The manure men in
Melbourne all advertise with David, and they must have
felt like cutting his throat when he fought for the
stringent Manure Act (safeguarding the cocky who
never even advertises his marriage) now in force. Syme’s
real offence is the unscrupulous manner in which he
uses his paper to help his personal friends, such as Simian
Phraser, Gillott, and Deakin, Deakin earns all the help
he gets, or, rather, deserves it ; but he would get it
whether he did or not.
* * * *
The country newspaper man who seeks a seat in Parlia-
ment will be apt to have trouble with one of the resolu-
tions carried at the Political Labor Conference recently.
That resolution binds him to pay union rates, or at least
Is. an hour, to his compositors. If he is found cutting
rates he is liable to expulsion from the party. The out-
back comp, is often as sad a scandal to unionism as the
cocky’s farm laborer. And now the ill-paid type-snatchei
will come home to roost, and will sit in his faded old clo’
between the boss and the goal of the boss’s ambition.
♦ * * -V
The gibes with which Melbourne Paunch now tries to
confute Socialism are to be found in Cruicltshank’s
“ Comic Almanack ” for 1843. They were sorry twaddle
even in their youth. Still, now that they have reached
their dotage, Paunch finds them just the thing for its
senile pages.
* * * *
“Philo”: “G.F.” (B. 17/l/’O7) may be consoled to
learn that the price paid him by William Randolph
Hearst, for his article in the New York Journal, was a
high rate in proportion to that paid by other American
publications. A few months ago I sent an article to an
important American religious print. It was given the
most important position in the paper, with a striking
headline, extending right across the page. The matter
covered nearly two pages. I got two (2) dollars for it.
Also, I have a friend who is correspondent for an American
weekly. He gets a dollar for the same amount of matter
for which The Bulletin would pay at least a guinea.
America may be a grand, country for engineers and hog
and lard speculators, but it doesn’t seem any too good
for writers.
* * " * •
“ The Hack ” does a general growl: I’ve been noticing
the frequency with which two or more journals publish
the same matter. In August last The Bulletin pub-
lished a “pome” by Horace Halloran, entitled “The
Correspondence Column”; same “pome” (slightly
altered) appeared in Steele Rudd’s Mag. for Octob r.
Another “ ponje,” entitled “ Two Feet of Stocking,”
appeared simultaneously in Steele Rudd’s Mag. for
December and Paunch Annual. Yet again, Steele
Rudd’s Mag. for June, 1905, published a story by M. For-
; rest, entitled ” A Wasted Cartridge.” This has just
appeared in the Pall Mall Mag. for January, 1907. A
personal experience : Submitted my first literary effort—
a “ pome ” —to The Bulletin. Correspondence column
duly notified : “ For print.” Waited three months for it
to appear: then wrote to ,inquire its fate. No reply.
Wrote again (somewhat Still no reply. Wrote
a third time (with great violence), and was informed that
it had been thrown out on re-consideration. Total wait
—six months. Dumped it into the Freetrade Arena in
disgust, and got a guinea for it by return post. The
Arena died shortly after its appearance. I don’t say
that “ Alone I killed it,” but, at any rate, the Protec-
tionist cause owe* Something to me. Another experi-
ence : Submitted a “ pome” to Steel® Rudd’s Magazine ;
received an equivocal reply, which I took for a rejeetioh.
Tried it on tpe Adelaide Critic. Four months later
“pome” appeared in Steele Rudd’s- Magazine. Six
weeks later still I received a note from the Critic editor,
drawing my attention to the fact, and hinting that there
is a law to punish impostors. My stuff had lain un-
acknowledged in Critic office for five and a half months
at the time 1 Hooray for Tortoise Town !
• ' * * *
“O’Fipp”: Why do photographers persist in making
a person suspected Of literary proclivities pose with the
forefinger of the right hand pressed against the massive
brow? I know of only one case in which the victim
really wanted to adopt the attitude. That was Johnson,
who once had a “ Sonnet to Distraction ” printed in the
Dairy Produce Record. The others, leaned the brain
i against the finger-nail because the beaming octopus in
the studio told them it was the correct caper. Why is it
the correct caper?
*■#■*■*■
Caf6 audiences, to whom young Leon Brodzky used to
expound his schemes for revolutionising Australian
journalism, smile when they hear he has settled down as
sub-editor to the British Australasian. Brodzky won’t
find much room for novel flights in that highly con-
ventional office; but since he is only 23, there is still
hope for him.
WHEN THE PAGANS COME TO TEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When my daily task of writing 1 little yarns and verses
ends,
There’s a glad reception waiting for my literary friends.
They are mostly incarnations of Bohemian paganree—
So it’s quite a happy function when the pagans come to
tea !
I’m, of course, the host and chairman when the Fourth
Estate’s “ at home *’
(Oh 1 I think of Julius Caesar and his pagan men of
Rome !)•
There are literary lasses (and their gods were drowned
at sea!) i
Just to give a finer flavor to the pagans’humble tea.
Are we sinners? Are we scoffers at the Titan who con- 1
trols?
No, my friend, we’ve something dearer than our puny
little souls.
There’s a doctrine nobler, purer ; and we ardently agree
How to set the world in order —ev’ry evening after tea !
And we talk of Evolution and the Socialistic Plan,
Plus the Bible’s dissertation qn the publishing of Man.
And we read the Book of Ages, writ in stream, and rock,
and tree—
A Vesuvius of knowledge are the pagans during tea.
But the world of men is calling, with a clapjor quite in-
tense 1
It is calling, calling, calling for my pagan audience.
Aye, it asks from one a story, from another 6ne a song,
And from each a fresh bombardment of the citadel of
Wrong.
So the godless lads and lasses have been drifting, one by
one,
From my office, where their poems and their paragraphs
were done;
But then other ones have hastened, in their stead, to bide
with me—
And I’ll never die of sadness while the pagans come to
tea 1
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.


Sydney Harbor Trust wants tenders for
the purchase and demolition of the old landmark
in Windmill-street, Sydney, known as M‘Bride’s
Hotel—a place which has stood in solitary gran-
deur for half a century. In the earlier, times, the
M‘Brides kept it as a sailors’lodging-house. In
the ’mid sixties, it obtained a license, though a
building of very limited capacity, and the
M'Brides still kept it. In fact, the M'Brides are
there or thereabouts even yet.
“J.A.C.”: The great Australian language
is finding its way to remote corners of the earth.
Several members of a rowdy gang, known as the
“M'Glynn Push,” were summarily dealt with in
a Glasgow police court. The Public Prosecutor
wanted to know what “push” meant. A wit-
ness replied that it meant they “ go about push-
ing for all they are worth.” What is the origin
of the term, anyhow ?
Died last Friday at Coward-street, North
Botany (N.S.W.), Mrs. Mary Ann Lutherborrow,
aged 94 years. The old lady was born at Ryde, on
the Parramatta River, in the year 1813, Governor
Macquarie being in power. She was the parent
of 13 children, nine of whom live ; likewise she
was ancestor to 42 grand-children, 81 great-grand-
children, and one great-great-grand-daughter.
The Lutherborrows, her husband’s people, were
located at Kissing Point in the twenties.
Bananaland. has such a complete system of
local government that Parliament is responsible
for the maintenance of only one bridge in the
whole of the great State. This spans the
Mclntyre River, on the border, near Goondi-
windi. At the time it was constructed, 27 years
ago, the N.S. W. Government put a bridge across
the river at Mungindi, and agreed to maintain it
if Bananahtnd painted and puttied the other one.

DIRECTORS’ STATEMENT.
Y'OUR Directors have satisfaction in presenting herewith the Audited Balance-sheet and Profit and Loss Account
for the Sixth Year of the Company’s operations, which show that th'e business has heen well maintained.
The Balance, including* £16,993 10s. 6d. brought forward from 4ast year, is .. .. .. £48,752 17 1
from which an interim dividend was paid in September last at the rate of 5 per cent,

nary Shares, amounting to 15,360 10 11
Leaving available £33,392 6 2
from which the Directors recommend the payment of a further dividend at the rate

Assets of the Company.

We, WILLIAM DENSHAM, of 58 Queen-street, and EDWJ.N HAROLD FLACK, of 9 Queen-street, both of Mel-
bourne, in the State of Victoria, having been duly appointed Auditors of HOWARD SMITH COMPANY, Limited do
hereby certify that we have examined and compared the books, accounts, vouchers, and securities of the Company
together with the certified returns as received frotn the Branches for the year ended 31st December 1906 and do
hereby declare that, so far as we are in a position tq form an opinion, the Shareholders’ Balance-sheet the Private
Balance-sheet, and the Accounts of the Company have been drawn up in accordance with the provisions of the
Companies Acts, and present a correct view of the Company’s affairs at that date.
We further certify that we have not observed nor become acquainted with during the audit any breach of the
Companies Acts committed by the Company, or by any Director, Manager, Employee, Auditor, or Shareholder
thereof, and that all our requirements as Auditors have been complied with.
Dated at Melbourne this 28th day of February, 1907.
WM. DENSHAM j
EDWIN H. FLACK f
Auditors.
We, HARRY BELLINGHAM HOWARD SMITH and WILLIAM GARDINER SPRIGG, of Melbourne, two of the
Directors of HOWARD SMITH COMPANY, Limited, do hereby certify, on behalf of the Board, that in our opinion
the Balance-sheet is drawn up so as to exhibit a correct view of the state of the Company’s affairs.

NikS.W. Reliability Race
THE FINAL RESULT
The final result of the New South Wales Relia-
bility Race has now been announced, and shows
another big' and emphatic triumph for the consist-
ently victorious Continental Tyres. Out of the nine
cars that were awarded prizes, four were fitted with
Continental Tyres,
and a fifth car that was fitted with Tyres in the
first stage of this comparatively easy contest had to
change the two back wheel tyres to Continentals
*** final stage o/ the Competition. On the nine
cars that finished there were thus
18 CONTINENTAL SHOD WHEELS
per cent, of the total number talcing part in the
contest. Other cars taking part in this competition
had to effe<fi tyre changes during the progress of
the race. This splendid performance by this popu-
lar brand of tyres demonstrates once again the great
superiority» the consistent reliability, and the
never-failing durability of Continentals over all
other makes of tyres, and repeats the performance
of this same make of tyres in the Vidorian Relia-
bility Race, when the
FIRST AND THIRD
positions, the fastest time performance, the Cup,
the Motoring Blue Ribbon of Australia, and the
Garland Cup, were all credited to Continental Tyres.
CONTINENTAL, TYRES have now proved their
sterling value in so many motoring contests, not
only in Australia, but in all parts of the world, that
they are now the chosen tyres of motorists who
Know, and have by far the

Government Notice.
Department of Lands,
Sydney, 16th February, 1907.
CLEARING OF PILLIGA SCRUB.
TENDERS are invited for the clearing of about
5000 acres of the Pilliga Scrub.
Forms of tender, plans and specifications, and
conditions may be inspected at the Information
Bureau, Department of Lands, Sydney, and at
the Crown Land Agents’ Offices at Narrabri,
Moree, and Gunnedah.
Tenderers will be required to give alternate
prices—
(a) With plant supplied by Tenderer ;
(6) With plant supplied by the Government.
The successful tenderer will be required to pay
a security deposit of per cent, on amount of
tender on the execution of contract, and to enter
into a contract with His Majesty for the due per-
formance of the work.
Tenders should be addressed to the Under
Secretary for Lands, and should be marked
“ Tender for Clearing Pilliga Scrub.”
Tenders will be received up to and inclusive of
Wednesday, the 20th day of March, 1907.
The lowest or any tender not necessarily
accepted.
Robert McDonald,
Acting Under Secretary.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and 'adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

“Stony”: According to a Melbourne daily,
“lady-helps” are becoming more plentiful, while
maids-of-all-work are showing a poor drop. I saw
things, the other day, which made me wonder
why lady-helps haven’t the independence to be-
come servant straight out. A servant can at least
call her soul her own. A heavily-gilt specimen of
the moneyed class, weighty from continuous over-
feeding and lack of exercise, waddled down the
Prince’s Bridge platform to enter-the Hawthorn
train, with a lady-help to attend her. “Push me
up the step!” gasped the heavyweight. The slim
help made a game effort, and failed. “Get inside
and •pull me in, then ! ” and after a long, strong
lift the dame was seated. It was a fairly warm
day, and the windows were open. “ Shut the
window, can’t you?” fretted the moneyed person.
“ Shut all the windows! ” The help did so. As
the train approached Richmond the haughty
purse-holder began to bounce uncomfortably upon
her cushions. “It’s frightfully close,” she snorted.
“Open the windows! Open all the windows!”
And the poor devil of a lady-help had to climb
over a row of children and a tired, fidgety old
man with white hair to do as she was told, and so
go on earning her 9d. a day—l suppose she gets as
much as 9d.
Thus “ C.N - .”:—
Pastor Tregear, of Melbourne, has a charming idea of
fair-play. It is his habit, when engaged in denouncing
the awfulness of Rome, to quote numerous authorities,
but, as he usually omits to say where the quotation
ends and where Tregear begins, the audiences are left
with the impression that the shocking “revelations”
he makes on his own account are those of the autho*
rities mentioned. For instance, Tregear might say,
“ Listen to what the Chief Inspector of Factories says,
‘ I have visited the Abbotsford Convent.’ In that insti.
tution, children two years of age are flogged with a
cat-o’-nine-tails until they carry 2cwt. up a steep stair-
case 14 hours a day.” The people would cry, “Oo !”
in horror, not having the least idea that the words in
italics were all the Chief Inspector said, and that the
rest is Tregear’s own. One of these days, he will pro-
bably remark, “ Now, friends, have you heard what the
Prime Minister said? He said, ‘Ladies and Gentlemen.’
The Pope is a thing accursed. He is at the bottom of
all the evil in the world. If I had my way, I would
give him six months’ hard. He thoroughly deserves it.
Mr. Deakin also said, ‘ltis a fine day.’ Ah, friends,
you do not realise the power of Rome.” There ought
to be a law to compel public speakers to state where
their quotes begin and end. Tregear, for instance,
might hold up two fingers to represent inverted com-
mas when he gets to the end of what the other fellow
said, if the other fellow really spoke.

Meet Your Friends and
Fellow Australasians at
Stewart Dawson’s,
19, 20 & 21 Hatton Garden, E.C.
ONE of the sights of London, and one you should not miss, is the magnificent six-story building just erected by STEWART DAWSON & CO.
for the better reception of the many visitors to London from Australia. It is miles ahead of any building anywhere devoted to the Jewellery
business. Fireproof throughout, it has superb Shipping Offices for the prompt despatch to our Australian and New Zealand Stores of all the new
things as they appear. There are Three Showrooms, Home Offices, Reception Rooms, Two Electric Lifts, Strong Rooms, and last, but by no means
the least, a Bureau where you can write your letters and meet your friends in comfort, or have a glance at the Australian and New Zealand papers.
This convenience is specially provided free of charge. Any letters addressed there will be carefully preserved until called for. All information
will be cheerfully supplied, and Mr. Dawson will always be pleased to see any one personally if they so desire.

PAIN.
Death is hastened by ignorance. Nature has endowed us with the sense of pain solely in order
that, by seeking relief, we may avoid playing into the hands of Death. When we feel pain we are out
ot health. lam long endured is a strain upon the system which Nature cannot withstand. Many of
us, however, go on suffering certain kinds of pain, just as if Science could not come to the help of her
sister .Nature, ihere is, for instance, no necessity for anyone to suffer from
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
Indigestion
Ancemia
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
The pains caused by this long list of disorders are but Nature’s means of letting us know that the
kidneys or liver are for some reason unable to perform their work efficiently, for all of these
disorders are produced by the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons, which would be
thrown off naturally if the kidneys and liver were doing their duty. Few people realise how import-
ant in the scheme of life is the healthy action of the kidneys and liver, therefore the following
description of the functions of those vital organs will be of the deepest interest to many.
? idne y s filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce!
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
£ li ieyS ’i rough either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proporrion
of the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous, and causes us to suffer from uric
disorderssuch as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Eright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition of
the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if Darticles
float about m it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy and no time must be lost
s ’ 01 Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful
'^ he^ i 7 er -^f n the J iv ? r v£ T ious substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
° f b A ] , e a r e t lus rn f f l° fro A. the blood ev , ery day ‘ The liver takes su gar from the blood, converts
it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood gradually, as the
latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is com
pletely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their life and are use-
ful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness
Anaemia, Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders. K ’ 1 iOWSnesB '

Little Witch.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
ALL the night was full of the scent
of flower-laden trees, and of the murmur
of the sea on the reef, and of the wild
sensuous glamor of the tropics, such as oversets a
man’s brain, and hurries his heart-beats. Thorold
felt it; felt it in every pulse of him, and he went
down the garden-track under the orange-trees
swiftly, glancing aside over the flower-beds for
the gleam of her dress.
But she was not there. Nor was she by the
little fountain ; nor back in the magnolia-walk,
where the fireflies shone. He lit a cigarette;
stamped it out ; lit another, and smoked it
through. And yet she did not come. And this
was bad for Thorold, for the love in him was
eating out his heart already, and his boat sailed
before the dawn.
But at last she came. Came softly through the
garden, with a long black cloak about her, and the
black hair brushed away from the delicate little
un-English face. For France had given her birth,
and Levuka had given her youthful day . . . and
Thorold had given her that which makes a new
w >rld for a woman when it first comes to her.
“I have been at my prayers,” she said, and
looked up at him, with hands hanging loose, and
eyes darkened.
Thorold laughed, stripped the cloak away, and
swung her up on the fountain-rim where the moon-
light flooded her straight dark dress and her pale
hands ana face.
•‘See me at mine, then,” he said, and
d lopped on his knee, laying his lips to the hem of
her skirt. Then he caught her to him roughly.
“ You now ! Kiss me, love. Why have vou kept
me waiting, you little witch ? Or are you a Puri-
tan, with your hair brushed like that ? ”
She twisted from him, and her voice was choked.
“N 0... no. There are things.. . I must say
them to you ..
He loosed his hold, and tilted her chin back
with a finger.
“ It’s the same little witch,” he said. “Who
taught you that kisses grow sweeter by keeping?
What do you want to say ? Have I seen any
pretty girls since I left you? Yes; a hundred.
But none like my magnolia-flower. Have I kissed
them ? Little witch, when a man is far from the
lips that he loves, he —faith, he makes up for lost
time when he gets back”
But something of the hush of her devotions
clung round her still, and he recognised it vaguely.
“No,” she said, and turned her head ; and he
laughed, half-irritated.
What then ? ” he said, watching the sweet
curve of throat and chin, and the beating of the
little pulse in her neck.
“They say”—she was steadying her voice with
difficulty, and he knew it, looking on her with the
half-smile in his eyes. “ They say that... you
have done many things that you would not tell to
me. That.. .in the Islands... yoi have done —
wrong ” —■ —
“They say? Who say?”
There was a queer glint to the smile now. And
the man who had spread the story would not have
cared to see it.
“I. . . heard. Ah .. . tell me that it is not
true !”
He laughed. But he was thinking of the man
who had told.
“Silly little one; what has that to do with
you ? I love you, sweetheart. I love you ! Look
at me, and give me the kisses that I’ve been sick
for all these months ”
She held him off, with her hands against his
oreast.
“ They say that you have ... killed. That you
go not sober sometimes on your trading-station,
that you”
He stopped her mouth, and she did not guess
at the desire that was rising in him to kill again.
“Little witch, you want to know too much.
Ihe life that a man lives to himself has nothing
to do with a woman. Look at me and say je t’aime
•.. beaucoup ... toujours ...”
“ H° w •• • your life not to do with me? Because
that I love you lam in your life. Can you sin
and I not suffer?”
“Sin!” Thorold gripped her arms, holding
her back from him. “No man says that to me ”
he said. .
(( “ But I say it,” she cried, with a flash of fire.
I say that to kill and to do other evil is sin.
Listen then ! You can hold me here close—close.
Ho you think that you can so hold my heart ?
You can kiss me because you are so much more
strong than I. But can you make me kiss you
back ? Ah-h ! Indeed, I think there are things
that you cannot do. And you cannot make this
not to hurt me—if you do evil ”
The fire in her fired Thorold.
“This is the way to answer you,” he said
‘ And this way... and this ! Can’t I make you
kiss me back? By heaven, but I think that I
can! ’
There was a fibre in her that he had not guessed
at. She looked at him, and her lips did not
quiver.
“ I love you better than my life. But I must
take myself away from you ... for always. Be-
cause it is that I must suffer too ”
Thorold bit off an impatient oath.
. “ w ho taught you that talk ? I can foot my own
bills, thank you. There is nothing for vou
to pay.” J
She looked up at the reckless, well-featured face
with the light in the eyes that were dear to her
and her words came gropingly through pain. ’
“I—did not say payment. But I too will
suffer. And... he say that there is suffering for
you .. . ah-h .. . mon brave . .. .”
. Thorold clipped her close to him, and he laughed
m his throat as her lips touched his cheek.
You little bit of thistledown ! How many
prayers did it take to key you up to all that ?
sweetheart, I shall have something to say to the
man who told you. And I think I know who
he is.”
The ring of exultance in his voice made her
shiver.
“ Ah-h ! What... to him, then ? ”
“I shall take some satisfaction out of his hide
1 Littl e witch, it was Hamilton told you’
a . nd , h T e , . have to allswer for it to me to-night’
And I think perhaps he won’t like it.”
“ But ” ——
Thorold turned Up the rosy palms of her hands
and kissed them.
“That’s the talk for you,” he said, with the
drooping tenderness in his voice that other women
had known. “Smile at me, then! Take that
KassTLSia.i?* **• ° f
Don’t,” she said, and her voice was stifled
I am not for you anymore.' You do evil and
i—it is not right that I should—should let”—
Ihorold grew angry. And he was afraid,
because he loved her.
“Can’t you understand?” he said. “Those
affairs belong to me —to me. They are mine only.
Not yours.”
“ But—you say that I, too, belong to you. Am
I not then to ”
Her breath stopped before the sudden blaze in
his eyes.
“ By ! I think that you do belong to me,”
he said.
He picked up the cloak lying like a blot on the
moonlit grass ; cast it about her head, and drew
the folds close under the soft chin. The subtle
lawlessness of the night was fire in his veins, and
the sweetness of her was sweeter than the orange-
flowers.
“Come,” he said, “who the devil cares what
the world says! Come with me, little witch.
I’ll take you straight to Pere Eustace and marry
you if you like. But, come, you darling ! where
did you get that perfect curve to your lips ? ”
She looked at him out of the dark folds.
“I—cannot,” she said. “I say to ybu that it
is good-bye for always. Because you do wrong
and do not care ”
Thorold laughed. He held up his hand,
shutting it slow and tight until the muscles of the
wrist stood out.
“See that?” he said. “ Could you open it in
a year with your little soft fingers. I have got
your heart as tight as that, and do you think
that you can open the part of me that holds it ?
You iittle, little thing ; what can you do against
a man when he loves ”
“ I can say to him ‘ No,”’ she said.
Thorold looked at her ; his face suddenly white.
Eor there was strength in her tone.
“ Do you think what you are saying ? ” he said,
slowly. “I love you. But I love you too much
to take you against your will. Do you mean
‘No ’ ? Tell me.”
“I mean it,” she said, and he did not know
that her heart was torn aparc in her.
“Ah, little witch; you don’t. You don’t!
Think of the days and the nights . . . you will
want me back every hour —every minute.”
“Notuntil you come to tell me that you will
sin no more.”
r “The devil!” he said, and turned on his heel.
Then he looked at her again. “ I have done less
evil than many men,” he said. “But I can’t be
a saint—even for you. And if you let me go I
think it probable that I shall do more evil than
many men. Well ? ”
Her little slim body was rigid. She was beat-
ing her brain into remembrance of Hamilton’s
words.
‘‘ A man is a coward who cannot walk straight
with a woman to help him,” she said. “And
if you will not be good for love of me, I may not
do wrong for love of you.”
“That’s your last word, then ? ”
She would have flung out her arms to him in a
passion of love and sweet words.' But she knew
that it was not for her to take the step down.
“ Good-bye,” she said, and went away from him
through the shadows to the low-verandahed house
flickering with light.
Until the door shut Thorold believed that she
would come back. Then he took a long breath,
and his hand dropped to his hip-pocket.
“ Hamilton,” he said, “ I think it’s up to me to
settle with you now, my friend.”
He sought through the little scattered town,
asking questions of seven men, including Hamil-
ton’s supercargo and a yellow half-caste from the
Loyalties. This last directed him to an eating-
house down by the wharf, where Hamilton was
hunting out his crew. Thorold shouldered into
the crowd about the bar and spoke at Hamilton’s
elbow.
“Get back into that spare parlor,” he said.
“ I want to speak to you.”
Hamilton removed his eyes from the half-
drunken Dago cook swaying between his mates,
and in Thorold’s face he saw a business that
would not wait. His eyebrows twitched a little.
He had seen Thorold once before when this
temper was on him. Then he looked at the men.
“ Get him back to the boat and under hatches,”
he said. “I’ll be down later. Now, Thorold.”
He led into a little dirty room smelling of stale
tobacco and bad fruit and lamp-oil.
“ Well?” he said.
Thorold turned the key in the lock with a snap.
Then he wheeled on the other man. His face was
dead-white and beaded with sweat, and his eyes
showed fire. Suddenly he was filled with a mad
hatred of this man who had broken his desire for
him.
Hamilton had seen souls in the grip of an over-
mastering passion before this day; but he showed
no fear.
“ Well ? ” he said again.
But Thorold had a weapon at which Hamilton
had not guessed.
“You told Mrs. Delisle something against me
last night,” he said. “And she told someone
else.”
Hamilton winced, whitening. The words had
hit him in an undefended place.
“Keep Mrs. Delisle’s name off your lips,” he
said, harshly.
“Ah ! I thought that was the way. Why did
you tell her ? ”
Hamilton was silent. Well he knew Mrs.
Delisle’s deep love for the younger girl, and well
he knew Thorold.
“Why?”
Still Hamilton kept silence.
“Why?”
It was the level, deadly coldness of the voice
that jagged Hamilton into speech.
“ That’s not your business,” he said.
“No? I think that it is.”
Thorold’s little Smith-Wesson slipped from the
belt and lay across his palm. Yet Hamilton had
no fear, although there was killing in the very
carriage of the man facing him in the smoky
lamp-light. For the name of a woman had roused
the devil in him even as the touch of a woman
had roused it in Thorold. He laughed.
“If you want an apology,” he said, “you’ll
have to go to Hell to get it.”
“ I’ll take you along. Why?”
“ I have not said that I told her.”
Thorold’s breath came unevenly. He was
stripped of the gay, daring calm that was his
nature, and his whole soul was shaken with re-
membrance of a girl’s white upturned face in the
moonlight and the nearness of her lips.
“ Mrs. Delisle is Murdoch’s wife,” he said.
Again Hamilton was caught between the joints
of his armor. Thorold grinned a little, seeing
the other’s face.
“Hit the gold again, eh? Murdoch didn’t
know that she changed her name when she ran
away to escape him, did he? But he’d like to
know.”
“ I never said ”
“ Oh, yes, you did. Your face is saying it now.
Hamilton, you know what Murdoch is. Would
you rather he found his wife again, or would you
rather that she told somebody -1 know that you
had been telling her lies? I give you the choice,
and three minutes to take it in.”
The sweat was on Hamilton’s drawn face.
“Thorold... I can’t make her unsay it. For
God’s sake”
“ We’ll leave that part out. Will you make
her?”
“I—can’t.”
“ Put yourself above her, do you ? Murdoch is
not quite ”
Hamilton’s words might have been wrenched
from a man on the rack.
“ Are you devil enough ? ”
“ Hold on. We’re not ready for the shooters
yet. It’s to your hand, Hamilton. Murdoch’s
wife is nothing to me; but what she has told is—
a good deal.” His voice was uncertain for the
moment. “I’ve a fancy to have it unsaid, and to
have some other things said instead. And you
are the only man who can arrange the matter for
me.”
“I can’t,” said Hamilton with dry lips.
Thorold’s eyes laughed. It was not the laughter
that the girl in the garden knew.
“All right. We’ll talk straighter, then. Get
out your gun an’ go over to the wall.”
“You go to Hell,” said Hamilton, curtly.
Outside a gay French chanson was lilting on
the hot air, and the gallop of it was in Thorold’s
veins.
“Give me the passport—if you can. Well ?”
“Thorold, I can’t make her unsay it. You
know that it was true.”
Thorold was tense with the passion that ruled
him, and his head sang.
“True! There’s going to be more than that
true of me directly ! D’you think I permit any
man to meddle in my private business ? By ——,
I’ll show you.”
There was a note of utter mercilessness and
pain in the voice that had lost its gay lightness.
“Thorold, I’m sorry.”
And then Thorold cursed him in speech that
took Hamilton to position on the instant.
“Call,” he said, sharply; and the two shots
cracked out as one.
Hamilton was on his knees beside Thorold as
the younger man went down.
“Thorold,” he cried ; “old fellow . . .”
Thorold was fighting with mortal pain ; but the
love of his life ruled yet.
“Wi11... you make her tell now?” he said.
“There’s a girl... she kissed me to-night—but
... because of what you said... she would...
would never have done... it again ”
Hamilton laid the head back and flung the door
wide. His hands were sticky with blood.
“ I’ll send for her,” he cried. “ We’ll have a
doctor in a minute. It’ll be all right ”
“ You ... won’t send for her. I’ve said good-
bye. But you ... you’ll let her think... that I
wasn’t... wasn’t...”
Hamilton stooped over the dying man.
“She shall tell her all that you might have
been,” he said, gently. “All that you may be—
if there’s another life.”
And a little later Thorold went to find out.
M.L.
G. B. LANCASTER.
“ Gip ” : A par. in a recent Bulletin
reminds me. I once knew a young couple,
scarcely more than boy and girl, who for their sins
were banished to an extremely lonely part of the
bush. Their little humpy was buried near a
creek, high ranges rising all round them, and the
only habitation near them was a clematis-covered,
snake-infested and haunted hut. It must have
been haunted, because everybody said so. How-
ever, the young couple, both ridiculously super-
stitious and quite unused to the bush, decided
that the uneasy spirits had made a shift, for their
own place showed every indication of being
violently haunted also. At night, when lights
were out and all was quiet, the most uncanny
sounds came from under the humpy. No voice
did they hear, only dull, sickening thuds, as
of a body slung against a wall.
The girl-wife, with frightened eyes strain-
ing into the darkness, would clutch at her
mate, scarcely daring to breathe, remembering
that under her head was a hole in the flooring,
through which the grass grew pale and lank. To
make matters worse, an indescribable odor began
to fill the dwelling, and the young couple thought
seriously of making a move. One evening about
dusk, the lonely girl sat dejectedly on the wood-
heap, thinking of home and mother, when she
saw her large grey cat leap through a gap in the
fence, carrying in its mouth considerable of a
black snake. Of course, she screamed. The cat
dropped the snake and ran under the humpy.
The girl also tore for safety, and, after barring
the doors, peeped through a crevice in the slabs.
In a few minutes the cat stealthily emerged, seized
the badly mauled but still wriggling snake, and
took it under the house. Next day the young
people raised the flooring and found, in addition
to the black snake, four smaller ones in different
stages of decay, two hares, one rabbit, a magpie,
and numerous bones and feathers. The snakes
all seemed to have had their heads flattened;
presumably the embittered cat had banged them
against the slabs. Now a boy-husband and a girl-
wife live in peace, and there is a homeless grey cat.
