Horse-shoe Luck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE was teller in a bank,
And a personage of rank,
In the little town wherein he used
to be;
And he led a happy life
With a charming little wife,
And a baby that was beautiful to see.
But Ambition—eldest sin—
On a woeful day crept in
To the bosom of Augustus Neville Brown;
And his yearning keen by day,
And by night his dream alway,
Was to be a big bank manager in town.
Then it chanced one eve that he,
As he walked home quietly,
Saw what seemed to be a Horse-shoe on the road.
It was queer of shape withal,
Yet upon his parlor-wall
He suspended it—to prosper his abode.
Yes, its shape was very queer,
And its nail-holes seemed to leer,
And it had at night a way of looking red;
But Augustus Neville Brown
Was quite sure it would bring down
Any quantity of luck upon his head.
Baby Brown, at childish play,
Sat upon the floor one day
Underneath the Shoe—which fell upon his brow.
And so cracked his little head
That they picked him up for dead—
And his little soul is with the angels now.
Then maternal rage possessed
Mrs. Brown’s once gentle breast
(For she laid the blame of baby’s death on Brown),
And she swore revenge—and, lo !
Three months afterwards, or so,
She eloped with a beer-traveller from town.
Thus the hapless husband, he
Lost his wife and family
At a stroke almost, and took to drink, and drank
Till his eyes grew bleared and red
And were bursting from his head —
And they fired him from his billet in the bank.
Then that bank clerk, broken down,
Tramped away from the small town
Where his days had onoe so happy been —Eheu /
He was down in Luck’s lee scuppers,
And was treading on his uppers—
But he took with him the terrible Horse-shoe.
Later on his corpse was found—
It had been some two weeks drowned—
And the papers said it was a curious thing
That around the corpse’s neelc,
As though meant its rise to check,
Was a Horse-shoe fastened by apiece of string.
CREEVE ROE.
Euchred.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE subject was Beer.
“Yes,” said the bailiff, “there’s no
drink like it, Bill, and it’s better out uv a
pewter than any other way.”
His assistant did not answer, as his speaking
apparatus was obscured by one of the utensils
referred to, but an assenting shake of the visible
end of his pint encouraged his companion to en-
large on the topic.
“ I useter think beer tasted more luscious out of
a billy—get both ’ands round the stern, and let
the rim press ’ard agenst yer chiv, so there’s no
leak ; but now I’d as soon drink it outer a pony, and
I’ll tell y’ fer why.
“When I started this game, one o’ the first
jobs I got was a distraint for rent. The boss
gave me directions, an’ told me to be careful,'ez
the tenant was a nold ’and. The ’ouse was in a
long terrace, but I ’ad the number, and soon
struck it. No one answered the door, so I did a
mooch up the side, an’ sights my man just cornin’
outer the back door onter the verandah. I got
in before ’e could stop me, an’ I must say ’e
took it like a lamb. ’E sed it. was on’y a tem-
p’ry embarrassment—they all do—an’ I’d be
paid out before the five days was up. ’E
didn’t blame me, of course, and knew it was
my painful dooty, etsetyrer, an’ ’oped I’d take
the inventory before the missus came back, so’s
’e could tell ’er I was ’is brother, just come
down fer a spell. ’E sed p’raps if I went ’ome
and brought, a portmanter, it’d look better, but
I winked an’ told ’im a man in posesh couldn’t
leave the premises. My innercent ’adn’t thought
o’ that. ’E-’ad no experience, an’ reckoned I’d
’ave to ’ave a gammy leg, to keep th’ old woman
quiet. , i
“Well, ter make a long story short, before I
took the inventory we got to talkin’ sociable like
in the kitchen. He arst me if I was T.T., an’
said a rubber o’ euchre fer drinks wouldn’t be
a bad scheme.
“’E played a fair game, but y’ know what a
bonzer I am; I lined ’im a treat, an’ ’e seemed
ter get fair riled over it. ‘ Blime,’ sez ’e, ‘ I’ll pay
fer the beer, but I’m blowed if I’m goin’ fer it.
’Ere’s a sprat an’ the billy. Better go ter the
Stork ; they keep the best.’
“’E did it so well, that I ’ad me ’at on an’ the
door ’arf open when it struck jne. As it was, e’
nearly ’ad the door shut on me, I tell yer I felt
dead wet, as I reckoned he’d let me do a perish
when ’e couldn’t do me, but he on’y larfed and sed
I was pretty smart, an’ ’e went for the red stuff
’imself.
“’E kem back still larfin’at my cuteness, and
gev me the can to t’ave first drink, as ’e didn’t
want the missus to smell it on!the cups. I was as
dry as George Reid’s dorg, and got a fair ’arf in
the first drink. That was. all I remember.
“Doctored? O’course, an’ serve me right fer
bein’ such a mug.
“I woke up just after dark on the back
verandah, with a ’norful ’ed an’ as sore as a ’oiler
tooth. They ’adn’t laid me down gently or put
pillers under me, I can tell you. I knew every-
thing ’ad bin shifted, but tried the back door on
spec. It wasn’t locked, an’ y’ could ’ave knocked me
down with a naxe when I saw the kitchen stuff Was
still there. I s’posed they’d on’y taken the best
sticks, and I went through inter the next room.
It was pretty dark, an’ I fell over a chair, an’ a
woman’s voice sed * Who’s that ? ’ An ole fowl
came in fr’m the front. ‘Oo are you, man, an’
what do y’ want ? ’ sez she. I wasn’t feelin’ too
perlite an’ tole ’er I’d soon let ’er an’ ’er blessed
’usband see what I wanted. She started ter yell
like a steam whistle, an’ a man kem rushin’
through th’ ’ouse. ’E didn’t arst any question,
but just sailed inter me. I did*me best, but I was
Murphy to ’is Squires. ’E landed me out in th’
yard and then sooled his dorg onter me. Th’ ole
woman was yellin’ all the time, an’ about forty
people an’ a John kem in, an’ I’m blowed if ’is
nibs didn’t give me in charge for burglary and
assault. I tried to explain, but me ’ed was too
thick with the dope, an’ afore I knew where I
was I was in the booby ’atch.
“ I ’ad the dickens own job gettin’ ’em to send
fer the boss, an’ when I tole ’im what ’ad ’appened,
he torked like a phonnygraf. ’E went straight to
th’ ’ouse, but, o’ course, not a stick was there.
“ Bad luck, y’ say ? Wait till I finish.
“I thought the worst was over an’ I’d be dis-
charged in the mornin’, but lo ’n’ be’old next day
the bloke turns up as bold as brass, an’ I only
escaped bein’ committed be the skin o’ me teeth.
It seems I was in the wrong ’ouse after all. After
dopin’me the cows had carted me down the right-
o’-way and dumped me on another back verander
in the terris, an’ I was too dazed ter know the
difference when I woke.
“ I lorst me billet, me clo’s, and me karracter,
and was laid up fer a fortnight.
“ Mine’s a pewter, miss.”
W.H.D.
The Presentation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Henderson was leaving.
Henderson was the ledger-keeper and,
on his 26th birthday, the doctor had
spoken to him very earnestly and quietly, and
informed him that he would either have to give
up night work or his hopes of a green old age. So
Henderson immediately started to hunt for
another billet.
The idea of going to the boss and telling him
that he could not consent to work back any more
never occurred to Henderson. It wouldn’t have
occurred to any of us. “ Going back ” was such a
recognised institution in our firm, and we had all
practised it for so long, that it seemed unnatural
to go home for tea. None of the fellows ever got
engaged or married, and very few of them ever
got into trouble —they hadn’t the time. During
my term two of them found time to die, but that’s
about all they ever accomplished outside their
work. It was generally thought that the firm’s
policy would eventually breed a race of clerks
who would require neither food nor rest, and who
would flop round the office like seals in a tank.
We got up a shilling subscription and- bought
Henderson a cheap travelling bag. He had
secured a job with a humane firm in the next
street, so we thought he would probably get a
holiday some time or other. Then he could use
the bag and remember us kindly.
The afternoon before he left, Brown, as the
prime mover in the little compliment, tip-toed
into the boss’s office and asked the old man in a
hiished voice if he would mind the lads knocking
off next evening at a quarter to six, so that they
could get the presentation over and go to tea at the
usual time —and hash -foundry.
The old man pawed his beard and frowned, but
finally granted the request, and said that, in
consideration of Henderson’s ten years’ service,
he would make the presentation’-himself. Brown
thanked him in a choking voice, and withdrew
with many salaams.
night we knocked off punctually at
25 minutes to six; and fell round until a quarter
to. Then we strolled and straggled from all
parts of the building into the sample room, and
stood in a whispering mob at one end of the
table, while Henderson, looking as happy as a
convicted felon, sat at the other end and leant
his head on his hand.
So we waited until the boss came in, and placed
a large, fatherly hand on the bag.
Henderson, from force of habit, scuffled his feet
and stood up, but immediately sank back into his
chair and tried to smile.
“ Mr. Henderson !” said the old man.
Henderson heaved himself to his feet.
“Mr. Henderson! On the—er—eve of your
departure from this firm, your fellow employees
have asked me (we hadn’t asked him) to hand you
a small token of their—er—esteem and—er—
esteem and goodwill (faint applause). During
your stay with us our relations have always been
of the happiest (loud applause); and, speaking
for myself (no applause), speaking for myself (dead
silence), I—er—l must say I have always found
you to be a most exemplary clerk, and one de-
serving the—er—the—er—highest—er ”
“ Wages ! ” said a voice.
The boss stopped for an instant and looked at
the mob with a dull, fishy eye.
'‘Commendation /” he hissed, between his set
teeth. “One deserving the highest ”
“ Screw ! ” said the voice.
The boss grew suddenly purple, reached up for
his whiskers, and resumed with a jolt.
“Mr. Henderson, although you are leaving us,
we are glad to think you are not going very far
away, and I hope—in fact, I may say we all hope
—that you will often come in and see us!
(Applause.) Come in, Mr. Henderson, come
in ”
“Any night,” said the voice; “we’re always
here ! ” ;
The old man choked, threw up his arm, and
pointed over our heads.
We turned like one man and beheld Brown,
wearing a whisky-and-soda expression, hanging
limply to the door.
“Brown !” howled the boss, “you’re dismissed.”
“Hooray!” said Brown, and fell under the table.
“Take him out!’’yelled the boss. “ Take—
him, ”
“Any blanky ni’, Hendy, ole chap,” said the
prostrate Brown. “Drop in when y’ cornin’
home from theatre—don’ matter wha’ time ’tis —
always same ole shpot! ”
The meeting broke up in disorder, half the staff
shepherding the boss, and the rest dragging
Brown out of the firing line.
Henderson left that night. So did Brown.
Henderson is now a wealthier and a happier man.
So also is Brown. And this is an immoral story.

TO BE HEALTHY
Do As the Healthy Do.
The healthy are always well on}y because they never permit themselves
to be unwell. You will have noticed that a clock left to itself is rarely
right; it requires to be regulated carefully. If it is two minutes slow now
it will be a quarter of an hour behind next week, and it is never two days,
alike. We ourselves are very like clocks. Only in rare instances can we
be left to go by ourselves. To keep Greenwich time—always to be right
and never to be wrong—we want to be regulated periodically. The healthy
attend to themselves, therein showing a wise care which all can emulate, if '
they will. It is simple enough.
TAKE
notice of the least system of disquietude, and promptly attend to yourself.
That disinclination to exertion which you have to-day is the first warning;
the stomach wants regulating. If you are wise you will put the mischief
right while it is easy to do it. A little neglect and the stomach will get
hopelessly slow, and will get slower and slower until it will need drastic
measures to make it go at all. It will be much worse than an irregular
clock; this only causes you to lose your train; an uncertain stomach will
make you lose your work. Keep your stomach up to time and the whole
system keeps time, too ; work is a pleasure and comes easy. It is as
easy to be healthy as to be always ailing; do as the healthy do, take
